China finds cryptocurrencies hard to kill

By Gabriel Wildau 
949 words
23 October 2017
13:00
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2017 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Blockchain is reputed to be immune to hacking and fraud, but in the wake of a Chinese crackdown on bitcoin trading and initial coin offerings, it is also showing its resistance to regulatory attack.

The world of Chinese cryptocurrencies and blockchain tokens suffered a blow last month when authoritiesshut downlocal bitcoin exchanges andbanned ICOs. But both markets have bounced back quickly, proving that the decentralised nature of the market makes it resilient even to the heavy hand of authoritarian government.

"China s government can t stop ICO investing from an individual perspective as long as the individuals are savvy enough to use virtual private networks (a low bar) and know what projects they want to invest in,  saysJohn Riggins, Shanghai-based head of Asia operations for BTC Media, a fintech information platform.

About65 ICOs raised an estimated Rmb2.6bn ($392m) in the first seven months of 2017, according to the Beijing Internet Finance Association. But the People s Bank of China said that 90 per cent of these deals were fraudulent, prompting the shutdown.

Meanwhile, after initially allowing Chinese bitcoin exchanges to continue operating so long as they tightened up know-your-client and anti-money-laundering protections, authorities finally brought the axe down, forcing the exchanges to shut down entirely.

Market observers said the huge run-up in the price of bitcoin and rival cryptocurrency ethereum made regulators nervous about the consequences if their prices were to suffer a sudden correction.

In the run-up to the five-yearly leadership transition that will conclude in Beijing this week, the concern for stability was even higher than normal. President Xi Jinpingcalled onthe Communist party to make financial security  a top priority this year.

But over-the-counter bitcoin and ethereum trading continues. While Tencent s ubiquitous WeChat mobile messaging app is sometimes used to coordinate trades, concern about surveillance has pushed communication onto encrypted platforms likeTelegram.

Mr Riggins said that a Chinese investor would have little trouble selling 100 bitcoins   worth about $600,000 at the current price   through OTC trades.

As for ICOs, they are even harder to stop. Because investors subscribe to ICOs using bitcoin or ethereum, capital controls cannot prevent Chinese investors who already own cryptocurrencies from subscribing. Investors in aChinese ICO need only create an account on an ICO platform in Hong Kong or Singapore.

Aurlien Menant, chief executive of Gatecoin, a blockchain token exchange in Hong Kong,told the South China Morning Postthat he has received a large number of inquiries from mainland blockchain projects that want to list their tokens on Gatecoin, as well as a surge in registrations from Chinese users.

Moreover, while the shutdown of Chinese bitcoin exchanges may have eliminated some bitcoin demand by making it more difficult to buy bitcoin using renminbi, the parallel shutdown of mainland ICOs has pushed the marketin the opposite direction. That isbecause Chinese ICO sponsors need to buy bitcoin to comply with the government s regulation.

 All ICO teams in China should return funds as bitcoin or ethereum to their investors. So demand is high,  said Sandy Liang, operating director in Hong Kong for Bitcan, a bitcoin wallet, news and price quotation app.

Further fintech fascination

Wolf warns bitcoin sheepJordan Belfort, the former penny-stock broker immortalised in the filmThe Wolf of Wall Street, has dismissed the current craze for initial coin offerings as  the biggest scam ever  that is bound to  blow up in 	. 	. 	. 	people s faces .The warningcomes afterICOshave soared in popularity. Mr Belfort, who spent 22 months in prison after pleading guilty to securities fraud and money-laundering, drew parallels to the fashion for  blind pools  in the 1970s and 80s, when companies raised funds from investors without specifying how the money would be spent. Many pools were dissolved without making a single investment afterbrokers made off with big fees.  Promoters [of ICOs] are perpetuating a massive scam of the highest order on everyone,  he said.  Probably 85 per cent of people out there don t have bad intentions, but the problem is, if five or 10 per cent are trying to scam you, it s a f**king disaster. 

Crypto crime riskThe rising value of cryptocurrencies is likely to push upcorporate ransom and extortion demandsto atleast $25mbecause technological changes in virtual currencies are making it easier for criminals to move sums anonymously, says cyber security specialist MWR InfoSecurity.

Bank blockadeBritish banks areshunning companiesthat handle cryptocurrencies, forcing many to open accounts in Gibraltar, Poland and Bulgaria and prompting some to question the UK s ambitions to be a global hub for the fast-growing fintech sector.

Bits and bobs

Mosaic graduatesJPMorgan Chase has agreed to roll out the data-analytics software of start-upMosaic Smart Dataacross all of its leading fixed income sales and trading operations. Mosaic is the first company to "graduate" from JPMorgan's"in-residence"programme for developingpromising fintech start-ups in the capital markets field.

SoFi for saleSoFi explored a sale earlier this year when the US online lenderheldtalkswith companies including Schwab, the San Francisco-based broker. The sale discussions at SoFi, which has since been rocked by sexual harassment allegations, were triggered by an indicative offer of $6bn from a foreign bank.

Chinese debutQudian, one of China s largest online lenders, hada debut to rememberon the New York Stock Exchange this week. Its shares closed up 22 per cent, making a multi-millionaire of founder and chief executive Min Luo.

The fund prices quoted in FTfm are supplied by the operator of the relevant fund. Details of funds, including prices, are for informational purposes only. The Financial Times Limited makes no representation as to their accuracy or completeness, and they should not be relied upon when making an investment decision. The sale of interests in the funds listed in FTfm in certain jurisdictions may be restricted by law and the funds will not necessarily be available to persons in all jurisdictions in which the publication circulates. Persons in any doubt should take appropriate professional advice.

Data collated by Morningstar. For all other queries, contact the FT at reader.enquiries@ft.com. The fund prices published in this edition along with additional information are also available on the Financial Times website at "http://www.ft.com/funds". Charges for this advertising service are based on the number of lines published and the classification of the fund. Please contact data@ft.com or call +44 (0)20 7873 3132. The funds published on these pages are grouped together by fund management company.

Prices are in pence unless otherwise indicated and those designated $ with no prefix refer to US dollars. Yields % allow for buying expenses. Prices of certain older insurance linked plans are subject to capital gains tax on sales. Some Property Unit Trusts are limited to investors who are UK tax exempt.

All dealings are subject to individual Trust Deed rules. The sale prices for these funds are estimates.

Guide to pricing of Authorised Investment Funds.

Compiled with the assistance of the IMA. The Investment Management Association, 65 Kingsway, London WC2B 6TD. +44 (0)20 7831 0898. All funds within this section, whether OEICs or unit trusts are authorised in the UK by the Financial Services Authority. The prices quoted should only be used as a guide.

OEIC: Open-Ended Investment Company. Similar to a unit trust but using a company rather than a trust structure.

Share Classes: Separate classes of share are denoted by a letter or number after the name of the fund. Different classes are issued to reflect a different currency, charging structure or type of holder. Buying price: Also called offer price. The price at which units in a unit trust are bought by investors. Includes manager's initial charge. Selling price: Also called bid price. The price at which units in a unit trust are sold by investors.

Single price: Based on a mid-market valuation of the underlying investments. The buying and selling price for shares of an OEIC and units of a single priced unit trust are the same. Exit Charges: The letter E denotes that an exit charge may be made when you sell units, contact the manager/operator for full details. Time: The time shown alongside the fund manager's/operator's name is the time of the unit trust's/OEIC's valuation point unless another time is indicated by the symbol alongside the individual unit trust/OEIC name. The symbols are as follows:m0001 to 1100 hours;. 1101 to 1400 hours;.1401 to 1700 hours; # 1701 to midnight. Daily dealing prices are set on the basis of the valuation point, a short period of time may elapse before prices become available.

Yield: Funds comprising mainly of bonds normally quote a gross redemption yield after all charges but before tax has been deducted. Funds mainly made up of equities normally quote a yield representing the estimated annual payout net of tax for basic rate taxpayer. For further information contact the management company.

Historic pricing: The letter H denotes that the managers/operators will normally deal on the price set at the most recent valuation. The prices shown are the latest available before publication and may not be the current dealing levels because of an intervening portfolio revaluation or a switch to a forward pricing basis. The managers/operators must deal at a forward price on request, and may move to forward pricing at any time. Forward pricing: The letter F denotes that that managers/operators deal at the price to be set at the next valuation. Investors can be given no definite price in advance of the purchase or sale being carried out. The prices appearing in the newspaper are the most recent provided by the managers/operators. Scheme particulars, prospectus, key features and reports: The most recent report, scheme particulars, prospectus and key features document may be obtained free of charge from fund managers/operators.

* Indicates funds which do not price on Fridays.

Other explanatory notes are contained in the last column of the FT Managed Funds Service. Prospectus data, price histories, charges and risk analytics on the funds within these pages is available online at "http://www.ft.com/funds".

Marketing Analytics  Firm ActionIQ Raises $30 Million; Andreessen Horowitz leads Series B round

118 words
23 October 2017
12:30
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

ActionIQ Inc., a customer-data analytics platform for marketing campaigns, said it raised $30 million in new financing led by Andreessen Horowitz .

Marketers with companies including Verizon Communications Inc ., Weight Watchers International and Shutterstock Inc . use the software to take in and analyze customer data from channels such as the web or mobile platforms and then target the same customer in another channel.

Previous investors Sequoia Capital and Firstmark Capital returned for the Series B round, with participation from new investor Shutterstock .

The New York company has raised a total of $45 million to date.

Andreessen Partner Martin Casado will join ActionIQ's board.

Hartford to Acquire Aetna Group Life, Disability Business; With $1.45 billion purchase, Hartford will nearly double size of its business selling to employers' benefits programs

By Leslie Scism 
683 words
23 October 2017
12:38
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Hartford Financial Services Group Inc. has agreed to pay $1.45 billion to health insurer Aetna Inc. for a unit that provides life-, disability-income and other insurance products to employers' benefits programs in the U.S.

The acquisition will nearly double the Connecticut company's business of selling benefits to employers, one of its main operations outside of property-casualty insurance.

Hartford Chairman and Chief Executive Christopher Swift said in an interview that the acquisition "will make us one of the top-two players" in sales of the group-benefits products. Hartford's annual premium in the business will expand to about $5 billion from $3.15 billion in 2016, and there will be more than 20 million people insured by the combined operation.

Property-casualty insurance will remain Hartford's dominant business with more than $10.5 billion in annual premium. Hartford also has a "https://www.wsj.com/articles/hartford-funds-to-buy-lattice-strategies-and-its-etfs-1463602968".

Hartford's rivals in group insurance include Lincoln National Corp., MetLife Inc., Prudential Financial Inc. and Unum Group.

The deal marks another sign of Hartford's turnaround from struggles during the 2008-09 global markets meltdown. It was among the hardest-hit insurers, when a long market-leading position in sales of a stock-market-linked retirement-income product proved riskier than anticipated. It took "https://www.wsj.com/articles/SB10001424052702304520804576341113561821194", since repaid.

In a news release, Aetna President Karen S. Lynch said the divestiture allows "a stronger focus on our strategy of creating a personalized approach to improving member health." Aetna said options for deploying sales proceeds include internal investments, share repurchases and debt repayment.

Hartford doesn't intend to issue debt or equity to fund the purchase. It said it would use some money allocated for share buybacks and said the transaction would add to its 2018 earnings.

Hartford President Doug Elliot said the acquisition comes with important digital technology. Hartford will use Aetna's claims data in its advanced analytics to try to find ways to speed up recoveries of people drawing on disability-income and workers' compensation policies. Workers' compensation is one of Hartford's major property-casualty product lines.

The deal includes a multiyear arrangement in which Aetna's sales team will offer Hartford's group-insurance products to clients.

At Hartford during the financial crisis, then-newly installed Chief Executive Liam McGee faced pressure from hedge-fund manager John Paulson to focus on its property-casualty business, possibly by spinning it off. Mr. McGee divested some operations to "https://www.wsj.com/articles/SB10001424052702304636404577294972124322432".

Hartford continues to wind down the precrisis book of variable annuities, which it quit selling after the crisis. In the interview, Mr. Swift said: "We've told our investors we're not the natural long-term owner [of the runoff unit], but we're happy running it off & It produces decent earnings." In 2014, Mr. Swift succeeded Mr. McGee, who died of cancer the following year.

Hartford has shown up on some Wall Street analysts' lists as both a buyer and seller in potential merger-and-acquisition activity. It is viewed as attractive as a target partly because it is a leading seller of property-casualty insurance to small and midsize companies, and this is an area many large insurers consider ripe for increased use of algorithms in sizing up risk.

But at about $21 billion in market capitalization, Hartford is a size where there are a limited number of potential acquirers.

"We are in attractive aspects of the market--property-and-casualty and benefits with good execution, innovation. ...I feel like we can control our destiny," Mr. Swift said.

Many insurance deals over the past couple of years have had Chinese, Japanese and Canadian investment firms and insurers as buyers. In contrast, the 207-year-old Hartford is a longtime neighbor in Connecticut's capital to Aetna, which was founded in 1853.

Write to Leslie Scism at "mailto:leslie.scism@wsj.com"

Pro Bankruptcy
Think Finance Files for Chapter 11 Bankruptcy; Fintech company provides software and analytics  to online lenders

By Patrick Fitzgerald 
452 words
24 October 2017
00:25
WSJ Pro Financial Regulation
RSTPROFR
English
Copyright  2017, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Fintech company Think Finance LLC, a software developer and analytics provider to online lenders, filed for chapter 11 bankruptcy protection Monday.

The company, based in Addison, Texas, filed for court protection along with six affiliates. It listed assets of between $100 million and $500 million and liabilities of $10 million to $50 million "https://pdf.inforuptcy.com/pacer/txnbke/456275/dockets/1/1-819A12B2-B837-11E7-8C0D-D70A8EA6B44C" filed with the U.S. Bankruptcy Court in Dallas.

In court papers the company blamed its financial woes on Chicago private-equity firm Victory Park Capital Advisors, which cut off its access to cash earlier this year.

Think Finance said Victory Park cashed out earlier this year in a distribution to investors including $105 million to funds directly or indirectly managed by Victory Park and no longer has any beneficial interest in the company.

Boutique investment bank Marlin & Associates is the company's largest unsecured creditor with a disputed claim of more than $4.1 million. Marlin sued Think Finance Inc. earlier this year for $4 million, plus interest, claiming the fintech company shortchanged it on fees tied to the refinancing of its credit facility.

Bankruptcy court papers say Think Finance LLC also operated under the name Think Finance Inc. and Think Cash Inc.

According to "https://www.documentcloud.org/documents/4116046-ThinkFinanceComplaint.html" against Think Finance Inc. earlier this year, the company and its affiliates are in the business of making so-called "payday" loans, installment loans and other subprime consumer loans at high interest rates together with affiliated Native American tribes, including the Otoe-Missouria Tribe of Indians and the Tunica-Biloxi Tribe of Louisiana, among others.

The Marlin lawsuit says consumers, states and other governmental agencies have alleged that Think Finance and certain of its affiliates are engaged in what is known as a "rent-a-tribe" scheme "through which loans are made nominally by the tribes in an effort to skirt state usury laws, but in which Think Finance and its affiliates have the bulk of the economic risk as well as the majority of the financial gain."

In court filings, Think Finance denies it made consumer loans and denies that its credit facility was running out.

Representatives of Think Finance and Marlin couldn't immediately be reached for comment. A Victory park representative declined to comment.

The law firm of Hunton & Williams is handling the bankruptcy matter, case number 17-33964. Alvarez & Marsal is the company's financial adviser. A company representative couldn't immediately be reached for comment.

Judge Harlin DeWayne Hale has been assigned the case.

Peg Brickley contributed to this article.

Write to Patrick Fitzgerald at "mailto:patrick.fitzgerald@wsj.com"

China fuels boom in domestic tech start-ups

Yuan Yang in Beijing 
500 words
22 October 2017
06:21
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2017 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

The Chinese government has spent more than $1bn on domestic start-ups in the hope of becoming a world leader in artificial intelligence and other technologies, according to accountancy KPMG.

The spending is part of the  Made in China 2025  plan, put in place in 2015, to improve China s technology industry by spending hundreds of billions of dollars through venture capital funds guided by government policy.

In recent years private investors around the world have cooled on funding early-stage companies, particularly in the US.

In the second quarter of this year, investments in private tech companies in Asia topped those in the US for the first time, according to analysis by CB Insights and PwC, with $19.3bn pouring into Asian start-ups compared with $18.4bn in the US, the traditional home of venture capital.

In China, the government has invested more than $1bn to date in early-stage funding to help young start-ups go commercial, according to KPMG. Beijing has made it a priority to support strategic technology sectors such as AI and robotics.

 China needs to go to higher value-added manufacturing, and this is the government s way of getting there,  said Jonas Short, Beijing head of Sun Hung Kai Financial.

As a result, local governments and state-owned companies have set up funds for investment in local tech companies. All cities have funds to distribute, ranging from $10m to $50m, according to KPMG.

Those  government guidance funds  are set to eclipse private VC funds in China in size, according to data compiled by SHKF. As of September 2016, government guidance funds had a total fundraising target of Rmb3.3tn ($500bn), compared with Rmb2.2tn raised in 2016 by private funds.

 The aim is to provide support for strategic industries rather than control the firms,  Mr Short added.

One of the largest guidance funds is owned by the provincial government of Hubei, which made a Rmb40bn injection into the Hubei Yangtze River Belt Industrial Fund   a fund of funds including other private investors that aims to reach Rmb200bn in size.

As of the end of May, the Jiangsu Provincial Government Investment Fund had raised Rmb80bn, of which Rmb10.3bn was given by the government.

Chinese government support  has significantly sped up the development of early-stage tech start-ups in China , said Philip Ng, partner at KPMG China.  Government-backed incentives, including tax incentives, grants, low- or no-interest loans and rental subsidies are offered to early-stage companies,  Mr Ng added.

The results can already be seen: the country is experiencing a boom in private fundraising by  unicorns    tech companies valued at more than $1bn.

China attracted $10.2bn of investment in the third quarter, according to KPMG, compared to a global total of $39.4bn. The mainland accounted for half of the top 10 global VC financings in the quarter, including the $2bn raised by Chinese news aggregator Toutiao.

Business
Wealth funds seek closer relationship with Valley VCs

Richard Waters 
848 words
20 October 2017
Financial Times
FTFT
1; National
14
English
Copyright 2017 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

INSIDE BUSINESS TECHNOLOGY

I brahim Ajami admits to being concerned about the high valuations of tech start-ups. But not so much that he is not ready to put another $600m of venture money to work   not to mention the $15bn his group already ploughed in last year. As the man in charge of venture investing for Abu Dhabi's Mubadala sovereign wealth fund, Mr Ajami's general wariness is appropriate. Funds like his have come late to the party, at what, for a while, has been looking like a cyclical peak in start-up funding. His defence is that good companies never have a problem going public, and that there are enough truly disruptive, world-beating companies to make it worthwhile.

It is true that buying into tech start-ups at what look like historically excessive valuations, does not necessarily end in tears. You just have to make sure it is the right start-ups you are over-paying for.

Sovereign wealth funds have been a feature on the start-up landscape for the past two or three years, ever since late-stage venture rounds started to top $1bn. They are now going one step further, setting up dedicated funds and local offices of their own in San Francisco.

Mr Ajami's Mubadala this week said its own local office would run a $400m venture fund   half of the money being provided by Japan's SoftBank   and a $200m "fund of funds", placing money with other local VCs. Malaysia's Khazana has been on the ground since 2013, though, according to Crunchbase, has only led an investment round in one local company, Fractal Analytics.

The desire to get a seat at the local table is understandable. For the wave of sovereign cash hitting tech, the challenge has been one of access. Putting very large amounts to work risks distorting the normal patterns of venture investing, and is particularly problematic for a group of investors without either close ties to the Silicon Valley network or much to offer other than cold, hard cash. That can lead to high-risk bets. When the Kuwait Investment Authority led a $165m round in then-struggling wearable tech company Jawbone early last year, it at least went in at half the valuation of earlier investors. But little more than a year later, Jawbone was on the rocks.

SWFs are setting up dedicated The $502m investment this week in augmented reality start-up Magic Leap, led by the Singaporean state investment fund Temasek, takes the amount raised by that company to nearly $2bn, even though it has yet to release a product.

funds and offices of their own in San Francisco Local venture capitalists naturally worry that the sheer scale of the sovereign wealth money is inevitably driving up valuations of latestage businesses. They also fear that it is leading companies to stay private for longer, further delaying the moment when traditional early-stage investors see a return.

Saudi Arabia's $3.5bn bet on Uber last year marked the peak valuation for the ride-hailing company, at least for now. Its investment lifted the previous valuation by nearly a quarter, to $68.5bn on a post-money basis.

SoftBank's Vision Fund (largely backed by Saudi money, with $15bn from Mubadala) is now negotiating to buy a large secondary stake in Uber at a valuation said to be around $50bn. Not the least of the considerations is how to structure the deal in a way that saves the Saudi blushes and avoids too many allusions to "dumb money".

Mubadala's $400m investment vehicle, by contrast, looks more like a traditional early- and mid-stage venture fund. The money is earmarked for about 25 investments, and the fund has already made two in life science companies.

Getting into the right deals will be key. The returns in the venture capital industry have always gone disproportionately to a small handful of funds, with the top decile taking just about all of the industry's profits.

That makes it essential to build relationships. Through investments in other VC funds, Mubadala already claims connections with VC firms like Data Collective, Arch Ventures and Sherpa Capital. Getting a foot in the door with the venture world's A-list is the next essential step. Hence the $200m "fund of funds": Mr Ajami lists firms such as Sequoia Capital and Andreessen Horowitz as the sort he hopes to build a relationship with by investing that money.

With its Vision Fund, SoftBank offered investors like Mubadala a quick way to put a lot of money to work, with both limited risk and limited return. Of the Abu Dhabi money in the fund, $9bn is in the form of preferred stock that pays a guaranteed dividend, with only $6bn in pure equity. Now, by trying to put down local roots, Mubadala is signalling that it wants to be all-in.

richard.waters@ft.com

Business
Tough times loom for LSE after Rolet bows out

PHILIP STAFFORD 
1,124 words
20 October 2017
Financial Times
FTFT
1; National
15
English
Copyright 2017 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Chief 's successor will inherit a company facing the challenges of adapting to Brexit and evolving with new technology

Xavier Rolet transformed the London Stock Exchange Group from a share trading venue to a global data and clearing powerhouse, delighting shareholders with its hugely enhanced value. His successor will inherit a company facing the political challenges of the UK's departure from the European Union hanging over it while markets rapidly evolve to encompass new technologies such as blockchain.

"He's had a great run but it's going to get a lot more difficult for the LSE," says Niki Beattie, head of Market Structure Partners, a capital markets consultancy. "The LSE board will have to play a crucial role in thinking about the challenges the exchange will face."

When the 57-year old announced his retirement from the 319-year-old institution for a second time, he stressed there would be no immediate change to the LSE's strategy. "The fact that I'm going to be here for some time means it will be business as usual," Mr Rolet told analysts on a conference call yesterday.

When the merger with Deutsche Brse was agreed, Mr Rolet was due to retire and strategy was based on the UK being in the EU. The deal fell apart and the next incumbent will need to reshape the company from the model that Mr Rolet created nearly a decade ago.

Less than a year before Mr Rolet's arrival, the LSE, home to some of Europe's biggest banks, suffered an IT glitch for seven hours days before Lehman Brothers collapsed.

Investors   who had watched the LSE lose market share to new cheaper competition and turn down takeover attempts from bigger rivals Nasdaq and Deutsche Brse   were aghast.

Now, its $17bn market capitalisation makes it the world's fourth-largest exchange, and bigger than Nasdaq.

"I would expect the vacancy to attract a high-profile candidate with M&A experience and for the board to look internationally. Today's figures highlight that the business is in great shape," says Trevor Green, head of UK equities at Aviva Investors, a long-term LSE shareholder.

The first of the many deals completed in Mr Rolet's tenure was the $30m purchase of MillienniumIT to fix the LSE's defective technology. But the LSE was a mid-sized company that had missed out on several prized industry assets, notably the Liffe derivatives exchange. Instead Mr Rolet, a former Goldman Sachs and Lehman banker, spent his time building closer relationships with big banks and more than 4bn on data and clearing businesses as markets evolved after the financial crisis.

Deals for index compilers Russell Investments and the 50 per cent it did not own in FTSE International were in response to changing trends in fund management; the purchase of a controlling stake in LCH, the clearing house, was to exploit new rules demanding that more over-the-counter derivatives deals negotiated by banks were processed through the independent risk managers.

He has also focused on developing small and medium businesses from around the world, either to list or develop in a mentoring programme.

As a consequence the LSE's operating profits have risen from 182m on revenues of 605m in 2010 to 426.8m on revenues of 1.5bn. "At times we took risks, we made a few moves, sometimes they could be qualified as bold moves," Mr Rolet told analysts yesterday.

Mr Rolet has at least paved the way for the board to attract candidates from across financial markets. In her final year his predecessor Clara Furse was paid a base salary of 540,000 with a bonus of 648,000. Mr Rolet's base salary is 800,000 and his total remuneration last year was 5.7m, down from 6.5m a year ago.

Below the revenue and profit growth, his successor inherits problems.

"There's a lack of technical integration across a number of the assets. The organisation is quite siloed, and that may impact performance without a strong and respected leader," says Jake Pugh, an industry consultant.

Indicating the cost pressure in the fund management industry, this week State Street abandoned three FTSE exchange-trade funds benchmarks in favour of an in-house alternative.

A future chief will have to deal with the UK's departure from the EU and incoming markets rules, known as Mifid II. The legislation will mandate greater transparency and competition in fixed income and derivatives markets, areas at which Mr Rolet has directed much of the LSE's attention in recent years.

But the biggest issue will be the one that has dogged the LSE since the turn of the century, the one that Mr Rolet tried to resolve with the Deutsche Brse deal; its independence. For now, uncertainty over the UK's future has deterred bidders. A successor may have to come up with another answer.

See Lex Search effort Up to a year to find the right candidate Xavier Rolet could take up to a year, the London Stock Exchange admitted, as it said the chief executive would leave by the end of December 2018.

The LSE said it would work closely with Mr Rolet and would look internally and externally to find the right candidate.

Few names emerged yesterday, although analysts and executives expect there to be lots of interest.

With Mr Rolet's appointment in 2009, the LSE was among the first exchanges to recruit a former investment banker as its chief executive   a now-common industry trend. Evolving markets and exchange strategies could mean the LSE again bucks the norm.

"The board will probably conduct a broad international search, and could seek candidates that have established careers in either information services or post trade businesses, not in the traditional trading arena that exchange groups are historically known for," said Peter Lenardos, an analyst at RBC Capital Markets.

For many years the industry touted David Lester, Mr Rolet's head of strategy, as a potential successor. However he left over the summer after more than 16 years at the LSE to pursue other interests.

One potential internal candidate is Mark Makepeace, group director of information services, which accounts for nearly 40 per cent of the LSE's profits. He was also a driving force in the LSE's last big deal, the $685m purchase of Citigroup's global bond analytics business in August.

'At times we took risks, we made a few moves, sometimes they could be qualified as bold moves'

Japan's SoftBank Plans a Second Giant Technology Fund; Masayoshi Son's brainchild hasn't finished raising money but the next could be double in size

By Mayumi Negishi 
731 words
20 October 2017
13:21
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

TOKYO SoftBank Group Corp., which runs what is already the world's biggest technology investment fund, is planning to create a second fund that could be even larger, according to people close to the Japanese telecommunications and investment giant.

SoftBank, whose $100 billion Vision Fund hasn't finished raising money, is floating tentative plans for a second Vision Fund that could be about $200 billion in size, said one person familiar with the company's plans.

In an interview with Japan's Nikkei business daily published Friday, SoftBank chief executive Masayoshi Son said he hopes to launch a new fund every two or three years, amassing a huge pool of capital to invest in technology companies around the world. The "https://www.wsj.com/articles/softbank-carries-bulk-of-the-risk-in-worlds-biggest-tech-fund-1495828542", backed by contributions from sovereign-wealth funds and tech giants such as Apple Inc., was started earlier this year.

"The Vision Fund was just the first step. Ten trillion yen ($88 billion) is simply not enough," Mr. Son told the Nikkei, saying that the Vision Fund will have invested its entire $100 billion investment pool in about two years. "I want to create a mechanism by which we can build our funding capability by 10 trillion, 20 trillion, 100 trillion."

Through the funds, SoftBank hopes to invest in about 1,000 companies in 10 years, he said. A SoftBank spokesman confirmed Mr. Son made those statements.

Several people close to the company said the plans are at an early stage and that SoftBank hasn't started marketing a second fund. SoftBank said in May it plans to complete capital raising for the first Vision Fund by the end of November.

The gigantic numbers Mr. Son has thrown about show how his ambitions are shaking up the tech-investment world. Backed by a $45 billion pledge from Saudi Arabia's state-owned Public Investment Fund, the first Vision Fund already dwarfs other rival tech investors. The $100 billion targeted for the first Vision Fund of which "https://www.wsj.com/articles/web-retailer-fanatics-raises-1-billion-from-softbanks-vision-fund-1502219551" is roughly equal to the total amount invested in venture businesses globally last year, according to research firm CB Insights.

Rival tech investors say the fund's sheer size has the potential to inflate company valuations, while some entrepreneurs worry that spurning investments from the Vision Fund could send capital to rival businesses. The availability of private money from the fund is also enabling late-stage technology companies to put off going public for longer periods, say analysts and investors.

"It will take opportunities away from public markets," Sam Altman, president of the tech incubator Y Combinator, said at a panel at The Wall Street Journal's "https://www.wsj.com/articles/investors-are-cautious-on-techs-big-startups-many-may-be-overvalued-1508360252" earlier this week. "That's really bad."

SoftBank declined to say where the money for additional Vision Funds would come from. One idea is that SoftBank could open a portion of one fund to retail investors, according to a person familiar with the company.

There is also the question of what companies additional Vision Funds could invest in. The first fund has largely targeted companies with valuations of at least $1 billion. It has so far committed more than $25 billion in investments, according to calculations by the Journal, and is trying to "https://www.wsj.com/articles/uber-shareholders-divided-over-softbanks-investment-offer-1505409810" into ride-hailing firm Uber Technologies Inc., according to people familiar with the matter.

There are 217 private companies globally valued at $1 billion or more, worth roughly $750 billion in total, according to CB Insights. Many of these so-called unicorns have raised money at lofty valuations but have yet to deliver promised growth.

The Vision Fund is the brainchild of Mr. Son, 60 years old, a Korean-Japanese entrepreneur known for his brash investment style and long-term views on technological trends. He was behind SoftBank's early investments in companies such as Yahoo Inc. and Alibaba Group Holding Ltd. that were highly profitable.

Mr. Son has said his goal is to create a loose global network of companies that will solve future technological problems together, and capitalize on bewilderingly fast-moving changes in areas like artificial intelligence, robotics and biotech.

Phred Dvorak and Eliot Brown

Cerberus Operations helps source opportunities, conducts due diligences, executes acquisitions and improve the performance of Cerberus's portfolio investments.

Time for a Social Network Credit Union? | Leveraged-Loans Crackdown Could Falter | Ackerman's Take: Cloudy Outlook on Credit-Reporting Bills; The Wall Street Journal's financial regulation newsletter for Friday, October 20, 2017.

Ackerman's Take: Cloudy Outlook on Credit-Reporting Bills

A Social Network Credit Union? Regulator Asks,  Why Not?'

Auditor's Decision Deals Blow to U.S. Crackdown on Leveraged Loans

SEC Approves Chicago Stock Exchange's  Speed Bump' for Trading

Treasury Official: Remarks on Size of Clearinghouses  May Have Been Misinterpreted'

Cloudy Outlook on Credit-Reporting Bills

The "https://www.wsj.com/articles/weve-been-breached-inside-the-equifax-hack-1505693318" has led lawmakers in both chambers of Congress to introduce legislation to rein in credit-reporting companies. But despite a political furor over the breach, demonstrated by no fewer than five congressional hearings into either Equifax or the companies' business model, it remains unclear if Congress consumed with other priorities will pass a bill to turn the screws on the firms.

In the House, Rep. Patrick McHenry (R., N.C.) "https://www.wsj.com/articles/key-house-republican-plans-bill-to-rein-in-credit-bureaus-after-equifax-hack-1507802400" to require the three major credit-reporting firms Equifax, Experian PLC and TransUnion to submit to federal cybersecurity reviews for the first time, provide free credit freezes for certain groups of consumers and phase out their use of Social Security numbers to verify consumers' identities. "http://bit.ly/2yBwLws" is largely a starting point for the Financial Services Committee to build on, according to congressional aides.

Meanwhile in the Senate, David Perdue (R., Ga.) on Wednesday "http://bit.ly/2yAPurx" to Mr. McHenry's. The Senate bill excludes cybersecurity reviews in deference to Senate Banking Committee Chairman Mike Crapo (R., Idaho), who is reviewing gaps in the regulatory structure. The Georgia lawmaker, according to people familiar with his thinking, didn't want to lay down a marker on the issue until Mr. Crapo's work is further along.

In hearings, Mr. Crapo has said he found the hack shocking and urged a "meaningful discussion" about how to protect and limit access to consumers' personal information. He is also pressing banking regulators on whether they need more authority to supervise the credit-reporting firms to ensure they adequately protect consumer data. "I am concerned there may be a regulatory gap with respect to supervision of credit reporting agencies for data security standards," he wrote in a letter to the heads of the Federal Reserve, Office of the Comptroller of the Currency and Federal Deposit Insurance Corp. last week. Mr. Crapo asked the policy makers to respond by today.

Though neither existing bill has any co-sponsors, analysts say Mr. McHenry's bill could eventually clear the House a chamber where the North Carolina lawmaker serves as the chief deputy whip and holds significant sway among fellow Republicans. But its fate is far murkier in the Senate, where Democrats hold more clout than in the House and might push for a more aggressive response that upends the companies' business models.

For instance, Ohio Sen. Sherrod Brown, the top Democrat on Mr. Crapo's panel, has noted that consumers have more control over their personal medical data, and questioned why similar standards shouldn't be applied to financial information.

Such comments raise questions about how the industry can be overhauled, and whether doing so would make it harder or more cumbersome for consumers to obtain credit an outcome most lawmakers say they want to avoid.

"We maintain our view that Congress is unlikely to pass sweeping, model-busting credit reporting legislation," analysts at Compass Point Research & Trading LLC wrote in a note last week.

Key Developments in Washington, on Wall Street, and Beyond

A Social Network Credit Union? Regulator Asks,  Why Not?'

By Ryan Tracy

ALEXANDRIA, Va. Congress should consider allowing credit unions to serve communities that exist only online, the industry's top regulator said remarks that could stir ire in the banking industry, which competes with credit unions and is fighting their ability to expand their fields of membership.

National Credit Union Administration Chairman J. Mark McWatters said such a change would better reflect today's society.

Under federal law, a credit union can serve a community with a "common bond," a concept that regulators and Congress have expanded over the years. The number of credit-union memberships in the U.S. has moved past 100 million in recent years, but the law still generally limits credit unions' field of membership to pools of people living or working near one another.

"We need to think about how we can create common bonds, or how we can reflect in the Federal Credit Union Act, what people today use as common bonds," Mr. McWatters said in an interview this week at the regulator's headquarters. "Chances are [such a change] would be very broad, and chances are it would be something that the bankers might not be totally in love with," he said. "But again I am trying to parallel reality today as opposed to what it was a generation or two or three or four generations ago."

Mr. McWatters didn't rule out the possibility of a huge social network forming a "common bond" for a big credit union. Pressed on what a web-based credit union might look like, he gave a narrower example an LGBTQ online community organized around individuals' sexual orientation as one way credit-union members might form an online-only "common bond."

For such a credit union to be created, first Congress would likely have to change the Federal Credit Union Act to tweak the definition of a "common bond." Then the credit-union regulator could evaluate any groups wishing to launch a credit union to serve the online community.

Mr. McWatters suggested in June congressional testimony that Congress consider a web-based field of membership, but didn't elaborate on the idea. Last year, he endorsed a regulatory change that expanded how credit unions may define their field of membership.

Any further expansion of credit unions' membership would likely be opposed by banks, which have fought for years to limit competition from credit unions. Credit unions enjoy tax exemptions but also can't raise capital or lend as freely as banks.

The American Bankers Association is suing the credit-union regulator over the new field of membership regulations.

"The taxpayer-subsidized credit union industry is already exceeding the legal boundaries when it comes to membership, which is why we have asked the courts to intervene," said a spokesman for the bankers association in response to Mr. McWatters's comments. "The fact that NCUA now wants to open the doors even further would be laughable if it wasn't for the fact that taxpayers would once again be left holding the bag."

Separately, Mr. McWatters defended his agency's recent decision to take much of the remnants of a fund created to stabilize failing credit unions during the financial crisis and dedicate the money to the federal insurance fund for the industry.

Opponents of the decision had been hoping more of the crisis fund would be returned to credit unions. It had a balance of about $2 billion as of the second quarter, thanks in part to legal settlements the regulator won related to crisis-era misdeeds.

Mr. McWatters said the agency relied on economic projections from the Federal Reserve and an analysis by BlackRock Inc. to determine that the insurance fund needed more resources to be able to weather a moderate recession.

"What [credit unions who oppose the decision] are saying is,  We just want more money,' and I get that," he said. "We have done the numbers &the trade associations and the like that have objected, they have not come back with their numbers. They just come out with something to pull out of the thin blue sky."

He said the agency could potentially distribute more funds to credit unions in the future if it judges that risks facing the insurance fund are reduced.

The National Association of Federally Insured Credit Unions said the agency is being too conservative.

"While the regulator may take a completely risk averse position, too risk averse of a position keeps capital out of the markets," said Carrie Hunt, the trade group's general counsel, in a statement. "This is not about disagreeing about forecasts but who is in the best position to make money work for Americans."

Mr. McWatters reiterated his plan to revisit a risk-based capital rule for credit unions adopted by his predecessor, saying its structure doesn't hew enough to the Federal Credit Union Act.

He also touted a new effort at the agency to explore "virtual examination" of credit unions, including the creation of a team at the agency to study the idea. "Is there a way for us to supervise, from our safety and soundness perspective, these credit unions without being in the face of management all the time?" he said. "I think the answer is,  Yeah, we can.'"

Auditor's Decision Deals Blow to U.S. Crackdown on Leveraged Loans

By Ryan Tracy and Miriam Gottfried

WASHINGTON U.S. regulators' crackdown on a risky form of lending to indebted companies was dealt a blow Thursday when a government auditor's ruling raised questions about its future.

The Government Accountability Office said a document issued by U.S. regulators in 2013, directing banks to beef up underwriting standards for leveraged loans, constituted a formal federal rule. The agencies had contended the document was voluntary guidance.

Under the 1996 Congressional Review Act, regulators must notify Congress about rules before they take effect. Regulators never notified Congress about the leveraged lending guidance.

The practical impact of the GAO decision wasn't clear. Regardless of the legal status of the 2013 document, regulators have broad powers to crack down on banking practices they view as unsafe or unsound.

Representatives of the regulators involved the Federal Reserve, Office of the Comptroller of the Currency and Federal Deposit Insurance Corp. declined to comment on the GAO decision.

Regulators told the GAO that the guidance was a "statement that explains how they will exercise their broad enforcement discretion [and] does not establish legally binding standards," as a rule does, according to a letter describing the GAO's decision.

Legal experts said the GAO decision would diminish regulators' ability to crack down on leveraged lending, which has been a sore point between banks and regulators for years. Leveraged loans are often used to fund private-equity firms' leveraged buyouts of corporations, known as LBOs.

"This could transform LBO leverage levels overnight," said Richard Farley, a leveraged finance lawyer with Kramer Levin Naftalis & Frankel LLP, referring to the possibility that banks could now facilitate riskier deals without fear of a regulatory reprisal.

The GAO was responding to a letter from Sen. Pat Toomey (R., Pa.), who had inquired about the legal status of leveraged-lending guidance.

"This is an important reminder that agencies have a responsibility to live up to their obligations under the Congressional Review Act," Mr. Toomey said in a statement. "When they don't, Congress should hold them accountable. I will explore steps to do so."

One possibility for Mr. Toomey and his Republican colleagues: They could exercise their authority under the act to repeal the leveraged lending policy. That would require approval by simple majority in the House and Senate, and a signature by the president.

The Trump administration, in a report issued by the Treasury Department in June, said the leveraged lending guidance wasn't clear enough and should be reissued.

Regulators might have difficulty agreeing on how to treat leveraged lending in the short term. One of the three banking overseers, the OCC, is led by an appointee of President Donald Trump, and two are led by appointees of President Barack Obama.

Jaret Seiberg, an analyst with Cowen & Co., said Trump appointees could act on their own to ease restrictions on leveraged lending. "This guidance already was in trouble," he wrote in a note to clients Thursday.

SEC Approves Chicago Stock Exchange's  Speed Bump' for Trading

By Alexander Osipovich

The Securities and Exchange Commission has approved a plan by the tiny Chicago Stock Exchange to introduce a new "speed bump," a person familiar with the situation said.

The Chicago exchange's speed bump is a brief delay designed to help protect slower traders from certain high-frequency trading strategies, and the SEC's decision comes as several other exchanges have also experimented with such delays.

An SEC spokesman wasn't immediately available for comment.

The decision marks a win for the Chicago bourse, which has been trying to sell itself to a group of Chinese and U.S. investors, in a deal that has fueled opposition on Capitol Hill amid fears it could expose U.S. markets to Chinese hacking.

That deal is in limbo as it awaits a vote by the SEC's commissioners. CHX Holdings Inc., parent company of the Chicago exchange, denies that its proposed sale would endanger U.S. markets.

CHX's speed bump plan, which it unveiled in August 2016 and amended several times since then, could help the beleaguered Chicago exchange boost its volumes. The exchange's share of U.S. stock-trading volume has tumbled from more than 5% in 1990 to less than 0.5%, according to Tabb Group.

Under a recent amendment to its plan, the Chicago Stock Exchange will first run a 24-month pilot program to allow regulators to see how it impacts the markets, before making the speed bump a permanent feature.

"We appreciate the opportunity to implement our pilot program and demonstrate [the plan's] positive effect on market quality," CHX Chief Executive Officer John Kerin said in an emailed statement.

The plan sparked opposition from some big trading firms, who say it would harm the integrity of U.S. markets.

Like IEX Group, the upstart exchange made famous by Michael Lewis's book "Flash Boys," the Chicago market will introduce a delay of 350 microseconds, or millionths of a second, on incoming trades.

Unlike IEX's speed bump, though, the Chicago exchange's speed bump won't apply equally to all trades. Instead, market-makers the firms that post a steady stream of orders to buy or sell stocks will be able to engage in much of their trading activity without being subjected to a 350-microsecond delay.

Under the amended CHX plan, a group of so-called "LEAD Market Makers" will be able to cancel many of their orders and adjust the prices they are quoting on the Chicago exchange without waiting 350 microseconds.

That could help shield them from being "picked off" by high-speed traders who detect that the price of a stock is about to change and try to profit by buying if its price is about to rise or selling if it is about to fall at the expense of the slower-moving market maker.

Large electronic trading firms including Citadel Securities LLC opposed the plan, saying it unfairly discriminated between market participants. But others, including Virtu Financial LLC, supported the plan.

Besides IEX, the New York Stock Exchange has also introduced a 350-microsecond speed bump on one of its smaller sister exchanges, NYSE American.

WSJ Pro: Treasury Official: Remarks on Size of Clearinghouses  May Have Been Misinterpreted'

By Gabriel T. Rubin

CHICAGO A senior Treasury Department official tried to dispel worries about the resiliency of clearinghouses on Thursday, saying some of the department's recent remarks on the financial market utilities "may have been misinterpreted."

A report on capital markets by the Treasury Department released earlier in October detailed the expanding role of clearinghouses and recommended ways to strengthen their ability to withstand a crisis situation. National Economic Council Director Gary Cohn earlier this week separately said the growing amount of illiquid assets parked in clearinghouses raises concerns about a "new systemic problem in the system."

Craig Phillips, counsel to Treasury Secretary Steven Mnuchin and the department's regulatory point person, said, "The work of planning for recovery and resolution [of clearinghouses] remains incomplete." Speaking at a Chicago derivatives industry conference, he added that the fact those plans haven't been completed "probably shows that people have confidence" in clearinghouses' ability to withstand a crisis.

He also said the Dodd-Frank Act mandate to clear swaps has been successful while at the same time raising questions about the recovery and resolution of large clearinghouses.

Clearinghouses, mostly used by banks and investment firms, are meant to help avoid a marketwide collapse by ensuring that either party in a derivatives transaction is paid if the other side falters. Their role has grown since the 2008 financial crisis as regulators encouraged derivatives to be routed through them.

Mr. Cohn raised questions about the clearinghouse structure on Sunday at a Group of 30 banking seminar in Washington. "I don't think we really understand the magnitude of continuously putting things in clearinghouses without the proper guardrails in the clearinghouse," he said.

A day after Mr. Cohn's comments, the Commodity Futures Trading Commission released the results of "stress tests" conducted on major clearinghouses to gauge their ability to withstand a crisis, and all of the tested entities passed.

U.K. Regulator Asks Banks to Probe Links With South Africa's Gupta Family

By Margot Patrick in London and Gabriele Steinhauser in Johannesburg

HSBC Holdings PLC and Standard Chartered PLC have been asked by the U.K. financial regulator to review possible business with South Africa's Gupta family, becoming the latest firms to be hit by the fallout from a corruption scandal in Africa's most developed economy.

After a request from U.K. Chancellor Philip Hammond, the U.K. Financial Conduct Authority said Thursday it has been in contact with the two banks about their possible dealings with the family and "will consider carefully further responses received."

Standard Chartered in a statement said it shut down some accounts linked to the Guptas in 2014 after an internal investigation. HSBC declined to comment.

South African police and prosecutors have said they are investigating allegations of corruption, including potential kickbacks from international companies, which were brought to light by a flood of emails and other documents that appear to have been obtained from Gupta-controlled companies. The documents have buttressed longstanding suspicions among many South Africans that the powerful business clan leveraged its connection to President Jacob Zuma and other government officials to amass great personal wealth.

The Guptas and Mr. Zuma have denied wrongdoing.

The scandal so far has ensnared international firms including KPMG International, SAP SE and McKinsey & Co., and led to the collapse of the U.K. arm of public relations firm Bell Pottinger over its work for a Gupta holding company.

KPMG, which audited Gupta companies for 15 years, last month removed the leadership of its South African branch after an internal probe found it fell short of its own standards during the 15 years it audited Gupta companies.

McKinsey this week said it had disciplined some staff for violating professional standards on a contract with a South African company that was owned by a close associate of the Guptas. SAP said it would release the findings of an internal investigation over alleged kickbacks to a Gutpa company by the end of this month.

KPMG and McKinsey have denied any wrongdoing. SAP's South African office denied the allegations of kickbacks when they first surfaced in media reports in July, but the statement was removed from the company's website days later when the software maker launched the internal probe. A spokesman has declined to comment on why the statement was removed.

Peter Hain, a former cabinet minister and a member of the House of Lords, confirmed on Thursday that he had written to Mr. Hammond last month about allegations by a whistleblower that HSBC and Standard Chartered may have inadvertently served as conduits for corrupt proceeds. An exchange of letters between Mr. Hain and the Treasury became public on Wednesday.

"We take allegations of financial misconduct very seriously, and have passed Lord Hain's letter on to the Financial Conduct Authority and relevant U.K. law enforcement agencies, including the National Crime Agency and Serious Fraud Office, to agree the right action," a Treasury spokeswoman said.

The NCA and SFO said they are aware of the matter.

According to documents that appear to have been come from a Gupta company hard drive, and which have been reviewed by The Wall Street Journal, several Standard Chartered accounts were used in 2013 to transfer money between Gupta-controlled companies in South Africa and Dubai to pay for a lavish family wedding.

The documents also show that the funds originated from a government contract to run a dairy farm for poor black South Africans. Standard Chartered has previously said that it closed the accounts used the in transactions in early 2014.

The U.K. regulators request to the banks comes after Indonesia officials last week said they are probing holders of $1.4 billion in trust accounts at Standard Chartered for possible tax due.

The transfer of the accounts in 2015 from Guernsey and Singapore prompted investigations by regulators around the bank's anti-money laundering checks on customers.

Both banks have so-called deferred prosecution agreements with the U.S. over failings in their anti-money laundering controls, among other breaches. As part of the agreements to avoid criminal prosecution, HSBC and Standard Chartered have spent billions of dollars improving their compliance systems..

Trump Announces Nominees for Two FTC Vacancies

By Brent Kendall

WASHINGTON President Donald Trump on Thursday announced nominees for two longstanding vacancies at the Federal Trade Commission, which has been short-handed at an unprecedented level with three empty seats.

Mr. Trump said he intended to nominate private antitrust lawyer Joseph Simons as FTC chairman. Mr. Simons, who has been co-chair of the antitrust practice at law firm Paul, Weiss, Rifkind, Wharton & Garrison, is well known in antitrust circles. He previously served as the director of the FTC's bureau of competition during the George W. Bush era.

The president also announced plans to nominate Rohit Chopra, a former official at the Consumer Financial Protection Bureau and the Education Department, for another FTC vacancy.

Senate Minority Leader Chuck Schumer (D., N.Y.) recommended Mr. Chopra, who would fill a Democratic seat. Only three commissioners on the five-member FTC can come from the same political party.

The commission handles antitrust enforcement, including reviews of pending mergers, and consumer protection matters, such as preventing deceptive or fraudulent business conduct.

Mr. Trump could move to fill the remaining FTC vacancy soon. The seat is likely to go to Noah Phillips, a staffer for Sen. John Cornyn (R., Texas), according to a person familiar with the matter. The White House didn't respond to a request for comment on that vacancy.

Although Republicans have been in control of much of Washington since Mr. Trump's inauguration, that hasn't been true at the FTC, which has been split 1-to-1 between a Republican and a Democrat. The two current commissioners generally have worked well together, but have had some disagreements.

The FTC hasn't been able to challenge a merger or a questionable business practice, or approve a settlement, unless there is agreement between Republican Maureen Ohlhausen, who has served as the acting chairwoman, and Democratic Commissioner Terrell McSweeny.

WSJ Pro: Market Talk: Daily Analysis Roundup

TD Ameritrade Worries Trade Database Is Security Risk

TD Ameritrade fears a huge new database of stock and options trades will become a prime target for hackers, according to a strategist at the popular retail brokerage. The company's main concern about the Consolidated Audit Trail, or CAT, is that it will contain investors' personally identifiable information, which could be stolen by cybercriminals, says JJ Kinahan, chief market strategist at TD Ameritrade. Exchanges are set to start reporting data to the CAT in November, following a multiyear push by the SEC, which hopes to use the database to enhance its surveillance of US markets. (alexo@wsj.com; @aosipovich)

Equifax Data Breach Complicates College Financial Aid

With college financial-aid application season in full swing, some parents are worried about the impact of the Equifax data breach. One immediate impact is on parents who want to apply for a federal PLUS loan (loans that are made in the parents' names and are available to families of any income level after they pass a credit check) , says Mark Kantrowitz, a college-financial aid expert. Parents who had their information stolen and then froze their credit, will now have to unfreeze their credit and keep it unfrozen until they are approved for their loan. SAP: Little Impact from New Accounting Standard

German software giant SAP SE has spent a single-digit million to a low double-digit million amount on implementing a new accounting standard taking effect in 2018, Finance Chief Luka Mucic says. IFRS 15, a standard introduced by the International Accounting Standards Board, deals with revenue recognition from contracts with customers and will have profound implications for some companies. In SAP's case however, the impact will be limited,. Mucic said. "There will be instances where some figures will change," he said. "But, in terms of the overall revenue, we don't expect significant changes." (nina.rrentmann@wsj.com; @Nina_Trentmann)

More Traders Eye Bitcoin Amid Low Volatility Elsewhere

The protracted slump in volatility in stocks and other financial assets is driving interest in bitcoin trading, according to an official at CBOE Global Markets, which is looking to launch futures on the digital currency. "The low level of vol across pretty much every other asset class has really attracted an awful lot of people to the idea of derivatives on bitcoin," CBOE Managing Director Michael Mollet says an industry conference. Chicago-based CBOE is seeking to launch bitcoin futures by the end of this year, subject to regulatory approval. (alexo@wsj.com; @aosipovich)

Cannabis-Related Offerings Light Up Canadian Equities

Companies linked to Canada's growing cannabis sector have done $455 million worth of deals in the domestic stock market this year, according to Dealogic. "There has been at least one cannabis-related ECM offering announced every month so far this year," said Dealogic analyst Laura Molina in a report. The largest offering of the 23 done this year, was an IPO for MedReLeaf, which raised $75 million. The number of deals this year outpaces the 17 done in 2016, when companies raised $313 million. "Eventually, in 2018, cannabis will be legal for recreational use nationwide in Canada as the industry continues to grow," Molina writes. (vipal.monga@wsj.com; @vipalmonga)

Rural Lenders See Farm Foreclosures Challenging Banks

Lenders in the U.S. Farm Belt are bracing for a spate of farm foreclosures as crop prices and farm incomes remain low. A monthly survey on the rural economy by Creighton University found that 10% of rural bankers believe farm foreclosures present the greatest risk to banks over the next five years. The concerns come as nearly half of all bankers queried in the survey which covers 10 states, from Wyoming to Illinois say corn prices are below breakeven for farmers who rent the land they raise crops on. "Concerns about trade, especially current Nafta negotiations, and low agriculture commodity prices impaired bankers' economic outlook for the month," Creighton economist Ernie Goss says. (jesse.newman@wsj.com; @jessenewman13)

Goldman Finds Buyer for Foreclosed Yacht

Goldman Sachs has found a buyer for a 217-foot yacht it seized in July after a client defaulted on the ship's mortgage. Per a court filing, a Maltese buyer has agreed to pay $27.5 million for the Natita well below its onetime asking price of roughly $60 million, but enough to cover most of the remaining $28 million balance on Goldman's loan, which was extended in 2014 to a Texas oilman billionaire who ran into money troubles. Goldman will have to fight off claims on the sale proceeds from former employees and a Palm Beach marina. (liz.hoffman@wsj.com; @lizrhoffman)

Goldman Chief Talks Up Frankfurt Move on Twitter

The Chief Executive of investment bank Goldman Sachs Group Inc. speaks on Twitter about the bank's proposed move from London to Frankfurt due to Britain's plan to leave the EU. Lloyd Blankfein tweeted from his official account: "Just left Frankfurt. Great meetings, great weather, really enjoyed it. Good, because I'll be spending a lot more time there. #Brexit." Goldman Sachs, which reportedly employs 6,000 people in the U.K., has lined up new office space in Frankfurt as banks prepare to move staff from the U.K. after Brexit. It has taken a lease on several floors of the new Marienturm office tower in Germany's financial center. The building is expected to be complete in 2019. (philip.waller@wsj.com)

ECB Says Greek Banks Capital Levels "Adequate", Challenges Remain

The European Central Bank deems the Greek banks' capital levels are "adequate," Daniele Nouy, who heads the banking supervision arm of the ECB said in a written response to a Greek MEP. She added though that the Greek banks still face major challenges, including the need to reduce their stock of bad loans, which stands at the highest level in all banking union countries. Greek bank will face stress tests next year earlier than their European counterparts. "We intend to make the outcome of this exercise known ahead of the conclusion of the third [bailout] for Greece, so that any warranted follow-up action can be taken in good time," Ms. Nouy said. (nektaria.stamouli@wsj.com)

China Financial Supervision to Get Tighter, Says Banking Regulator

China's financial supervision is set to become tighter, says Guo Shuqing, chairman of the China Banking Regulatory Commission. Guo says the banking regulator has worked to ward off risks from areas like wealth management products, property bubbles, shadow banking and local government debt. All these efforts will help prevent funds from circulating within the financial system and ensure the real economy get the financial support. (jie.yang@wsj.com)

Buying Into a Bubble?

Investors thinking about three big themes bitcoin and blockchain, artificial intelligence and treatment of aging would do well to "https://www.wsj.com/articles/why-bitcoin-buyers-should-heed-lessons-of-chinas-bubbles-1508436387": Being early is as good as being wrong, and buying into a bubble doesn't usually work out well.

Bitcoin has already jumped 10-fold in just over a year, and other cryptocurrencies have had even more extreme moves so if they pop, they might take the record. True believers argue bitcoin is different, and its price rise is justified because it is the future of money. The history of bubbles suggests that's unlikely but not impossible: Boston fund manager GMO found that one in seven price moves that look like a bubble turns out to be a paradigm shift.

Investors trying to spot the next big theme need to beware of thinking they are early when they are late, as they might be with bitcoin. They also need to be ready to sell if it looks like a bubble is developing and to have the strength not to be sucked back in if the bubble keeps going for a long time.

The CCAR program's focus on distributions may have contributed to an extra degree of caution about raising dividends to levels that might not be sustainable in stressed economic conditions.
