Ackerman's Take: Cloudy Outlook on Credit-Reporting Bills

A Social Network Credit Union? Regulator Asks,  Why Not?'

Auditor's Decision Deals Blow to U.S. Crackdown on Leveraged Loans

SEC Approves Chicago Stock Exchange's  Speed Bump' for Trading

Treasury Official: Remarks on Size of Clearinghouses  May Have Been Misinterpreted'

Cloudy Outlook on Credit-Reporting Bills

The "https://www.wsj.com/articles/weve-been-breached-inside-the-equifax-hack-1505693318" has led lawmakers in both chambers of Congress to introduce legislation to rein in credit-reporting companies. But despite a political furor over the breach, demonstrated by no fewer than five congressional hearings into either Equifax or the companies' business model, it remains unclear if Congress consumed with other priorities will pass a bill to turn the screws on the firms.

In the House, Rep. Patrick McHenry (R., N.C.) "https://www.wsj.com/articles/key-house-republican-plans-bill-to-rein-in-credit-bureaus-after-equifax-hack-1507802400" to require the three major credit-reporting firms Equifax, Experian PLC and TransUnion to submit to federal cybersecurity reviews for the first time, provide free credit freezes for certain groups of consumers and phase out their use of Social Security numbers to verify consumers' identities. "http://bit.ly/2yBwLws" is largely a starting point for the Financial Services Committee to build on, according to congressional aides.

Meanwhile in the Senate, David Perdue (R., Ga.) on Wednesday "http://bit.ly/2yAPurx" to Mr. McHenry's. The Senate bill excludes cybersecurity reviews in deference to Senate Banking Committee Chairman Mike Crapo (R., Idaho), who is reviewing gaps in the regulatory structure. The Georgia lawmaker, according to people familiar with his thinking, didn't want to lay down a marker on the issue until Mr. Crapo's work is further along.

In hearings, Mr. Crapo has said he found the hack shocking and urged a "meaningful discussion" about how to protect and limit access to consumers' personal information. He is also pressing banking regulators on whether they need more authority to supervise the credit-reporting firms to ensure they adequately protect consumer data. "I am concerned there may be a regulatory gap with respect to supervision of credit reporting agencies for data security standards," he wrote in a letter to the heads of the Federal Reserve, Office of the Comptroller of the Currency and Federal Deposit Insurance Corp. last week. Mr. Crapo asked the policy makers to respond by today.

Though neither existing bill has any co-sponsors, analysts say Mr. McHenry's bill could eventually clear the House a chamber where the North Carolina lawmaker serves as the chief deputy whip and holds significant sway among fellow Republicans. But its fate is far murkier in the Senate, where Democrats hold more clout than in the House and might push for a more aggressive response that upends the companies' business models.

For instance, Ohio Sen. Sherrod Brown, the top Democrat on Mr. Crapo's panel, has noted that consumers have more control over their personal medical data, and questioned why similar standards shouldn't be applied to financial information.

Such comments raise questions about how the industry can be overhauled, and whether doing so would make it harder or more cumbersome for consumers to obtain credit an outcome most lawmakers say they want to avoid.

"We maintain our view that Congress is unlikely to pass sweeping, model-busting credit reporting legislation," analysts at Compass Point Research & Trading LLC wrote in a note last week.

Key Developments in Washington, on Wall Street, and Beyond

A Social Network Credit Union? Regulator Asks,  Why Not?'

"https://www.wsj.com/articles/a-social-network-credit-union-regulator-asks-why-not-1508493601" allowing credit unions to serve communities that exist only online, the industry's top regulator said remarks that could stir ire in the banking industry, which competes with credit unions and is fighting their ability to expand their fields of membership.

National Credit Union Administration Chairman J. Mark McWatters said such a change would better reflect today's society.

Under federal law, a credit union can serve a community with a "common bond," a concept that regulators and Congress have expanded over the years. The number of credit-union memberships in the U.S. has moved past 100 million in recent years, but the law still generally limits credit unions' field of membership to pools of people living or working near one another.

Auditor's Decision Deals Blow to U.S. Crackdown on Leveraged Loans

"https://www.wsj.com/articles/auditors-decision-deals-blow-to-u-s-crackdown-on-leveraged-loans-1508445534" of lending to indebted companies was dealt a blow Thursday when a government auditor's ruling raised questions about its future.

The Government Accountability Office said a document issued by U.S. regulators in 2013, directing banks to beef up underwriting standards for leveraged loans, constituted a formal federal rule. The agencies had contended the document was voluntary guidance. Under the 1996 Congressional Review Act, regulators must notify Congress about rules before they take effect. Regulators never notified Congress about the leveraged lending guidance.

The practical impact of the GAO decision wasn't clear. Legal experts said the move would diminish regulators' ability to crack down on leveraged lending, which has been a sore point between banks and regulators for years.

SEC Approves Chicago Stock Exchange's  Speed Bump' for Trading

"https://www.wsj.com/articles/sec-approves-chicago-stock-exchanges-speed-bump-for-trading-1508453511" a plan by the tiny Chicago Stock Exchange to introduce a new "speed bump," a person familiar with the situation said.

The Chicago exchange's speed bump is a brief delay designed to help protect slower traders from certain high-frequency trading strategies, and the SEC's decision comes as several other exchanges have also experimented with such delays.

The decision marks a win for the Chicago bourse, which has been trying to sell itself to a group of Chinese and U.S. investors, in a deal that has fueled opposition on Capitol Hill amid fears it could expose U.S. markets to Chinese hacking.

WSJ Pro: Treasury Official: Remarks on Size of Clearinghouses  May Have Been Misinterpreted'

"https://www.wsj.com/articles/treasury-official-remarks-on-size-of-clearinghouses-may-have-been-misinterpreted-1508431076" to dispel worries about the resiliency of clearinghouses on Thursday, saying some of the department's recent remarks on the financial market utilities "may have been misinterpreted."

A report on capital markets by the Treasury Department released earlier in October detailed the expanding role of clearinghouses and recommended ways to strengthen their ability to withstand a crisis situation. National Economic Council Director Gary Cohn earlier this week separately said the growing amount of illiquid assets parked in clearinghouses raises concerns about a "new systemic problem in the system."

Craig Phillips, counsel to Treasury Secretary Steven Mnuchin and the department's regulatory point person, said, "The work of planning for recovery and resolution [of clearinghouses] remains incomplete." Speaking at a Chicago derivatives industry conference, he added that the fact those plans haven't been completed "probably shows that people have confidence" in clearinghouses' ability to withstand a crisis.

U.K. Regulator Asks Banks to Probe Links With South Africa's Gupta Family

"https://www.wsj.com/articles/u-k-regulator-asks-banks-to-probe-links-with-south-africas-gupta-family-1508420850" have been asked by the U.K. financial regulator to review possible business with South Africa's Gupta family, becoming the latest firms to be hit by the fallout from a corruption scandal in Africa's most developed economy.

After a request from U.K. Chancellor Philip Hammond, the U.K. Financial Conduct Authority said Thursday it has been in contact with the two banks about their possible dealings with the family and "will consider carefully further responses received."

Standard Chartered in a statement said it shut down some accounts linked to the Guptas in 2014 after an internal investigation. HSBC declined to comment.

Trump Announces Nominees for Two FTC Vacancies

"https://www.wsj.com/articles/donald-trump-announces-nominees-for-two-ftc-vacancies-1508453261" nominees for two longstanding vacancies at the Federal Trade Commission, which has been short-handed at an unprecedented level with three empty seats.

Mr. Trump said he intended to nominate private antitrust lawyer Joseph Simons as FTC chairman. Mr. Simons, who has been co-chair of the antitrust practice at law firm Paul, Weiss, Rifkind, Wharton & Garrison, is well known in antitrust circles. He previously served as the director of the FTC's bureau of competition during the George W. Bush era.

The president also announced plans to nominate Rohit Chopra, a former official at the Consumer Financial Protection Bureau and the Education Department, for another FTC vacancy. Senate Minority Leader Chuck Schumer (D., N.Y.) recommended Mr. Chopra, who would fill a Democratic seat.

WSJ Pro: Market Talk: Daily Analysis Roundup

"https://www.wsj.com/articles/market-talk-daily-analysis-roundup-1508448182" by Wall Street Journal reporters. TD Ameritrade Worries Trade Database Is Security Risk; Equifax Data Breach Complicates College Financial Aid; Bitcoin Derivatives Start Trading on LedgerX

Pro Private Markets
Tech Execs Support Basic Income; YC's plan to test universal basic income is gaining interest

By Tomio Geron 
448 words
20 October 2017
02:17
WSJ Pro Venture Capital
RSTPROVC
English
Copyright  2017, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Silicon Valley is increasingly interested in mitigating the effects of artificial intelligence and automation.

One idea that has gotten traction among some in technology is universal basic income, a free cash program for all people, which could help people put out of jobs by automation. The "Cash Conference" in San Francisco focused on the topic Thursday, organized by the Economic Security Project, a group cofounded by Facebook cofounder Chris Hughes.

Y Combinator's research arm is moving ahead with an ambitious project on basic income, which has support of the group's president, Sam Altman.

Y Combinator's effort recently resulted in a finished a feasibility study in Oakland, Calif., and it announced plans to run a randomized controlled trial in two U.S. states on basic income. That plan includes 1,000 people receiving $1,000 per month for up to five years and 2,000 people receiving $50 per month as a control group to compare.

Participants are eager to be part of YC's study and are willing to share personal quantitative and qualitative information, including what they spend money on and what they do with their time. "We feel confident moving forward this is something we can do," said Elizabeth Rhodes, research director for the project.

Basic income is not a panacea that alone will solve all social problems, she said, but the study could help identify problems that cash could or could not solve. "Basic income is neither a silver bullet nor a singular policy option," Ms. Rhodes said.

YC's research project is separate from YC the investment firm, but individuals at the firm are able to help and provide advice, particularly if technology is needed for the research or for collecting data, she said. The project aims to stay out of the political debates on basic income, though basic income if it grows would become a policy issue.

Basic income could also make entrepreneurship more accessible for more people, said Roy Bahat of Bloomberg Beta. He relayed an anecdote from the head of a tech accelerator. "Here's a dirty secret. All the best founders are rich already," the person told Bahat. "Silicon valley startups are the exception not the rule."

Research is increasing on the idea. The Economic Security Project on Wednesday announced a $1 million grant to test basic income in Stockton, Calif.

"The most effective thing we can do is give money," said Mr. Hughes. "It isn't a silver bullet, but it's been so rigorously researched," he said, pointing to studies in other countries.

China Leans on Old Habits to Reach 6.8% Growth for the Third Quarter; To keep near-term growth steady, Beijing has delayed fixing some of its long-running problems

By Lingling Wei 
885 words
19 October 2017
09:06
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Corrections & Amplifications

Xi Jinping delivered a speech Wednesday that marked the opening of the Communist Party congress. An earlier version of this article incorrectly stated that his speech was Thursday. (10/18) 

BEIJING China's economy expanded at a robust 6.8% pace in the third quarter, meeting market expectations, as traditional growth drivers such as manufacturing and exports gained steam.

The strong showing is welcome news for "https://www.wsj.com/articles/chinas-xi-feeds-expectations-for-a-lengthy-reign-1508333178". To prepare for the event, which is expected to strengthen President Xi Jinping's reign over the nation for years to come, various levels of government have for months mounted massive efforts to keep the world's second-largest economy on an even keel.

The third-quarter pace slowed from 6.9% in the second quarter, though it puts the full-year target set by the leadership for growth of about 6.5% well within reach. Adding to a sense of optimism in official circles, China's central-bank Gov. Zhou Xiaochuan said just days ahead of the political conclave that second-half growth could reach 7%.

Increased industrial activity and higher commodity prices were major reasons behind the strong growth. The latter, partly a result of policies to cut overcapacity, benefited state-owned manufacturers in particular. That is because the capacity cuts led to the closures of many private firms, especially in industries like coal and metals.

Recovering global demand also helped, giving China some economic breathing room, which has meant an economic slowdown that many expected hasn't materialized.

Industrial production, a main gauge of manufacturing activity, rose 6.6% in September from a year earlier, according to data released Thursday by the National Bureau of Statistics. Retail sales jumped 10.3%, though the pace of growth slowed from the first half of the year, indicating consumers have grown wary of opening their wallets.

Analysts say increased output was largely driven by state firms that have seen a sharp rebound in profits this year. In the first nine months of 2017, according to official data, large state firms controlled by the central government which dominate China's smokestack industries saw their profits jump 18.4% from a year earlier to 1.1 trillion yuan ($167 billion).

Still-solid property investment, despite government efforts to cool the housing market, has also increased demand for steel, furniture and other materials, helping to lift profits in the manufacturing sector. Meanwhile, a global economic recovery has helped Chinese exporters.

This doesn't mean China has entered a new cycle of accelerating growth, economists warn. To keep near-term growth steady, Beijing has delayed fixing some of its long-running problems, such as industrial overcapacity and excessive debt. That, in turn, economists say, means risks for a prolonged slowdown in the economy are rising, not declining.

Economists also have long questioned whether the government's statistics reflect the true state of the economy. The Conference Board, a nonprofit research firm, estimates that China's growth rate is around 4%.

In a speech Wednesday that marked the opening of the party congress, Mr. Xi laid out a sweeping plan to transform China into a strong global power that stresses expanding the party's control over the economy and all other corners of the nation. But unlike his predecessor, who said China's national income would double over a decade, Mr. Xi outlined his vision without specifying a broad target for economic expansion, even as he reaffirmed Beijing's commitment to development.

The omission indicated "a subtle de-emphasizing of specific growth targets," said UBS China economist Wang Tao. Ms. Wang projects that the government will soften its growth target slightly for next year.

But to tackle the economy's long-term ills, some analysts say, Beijing may need to significantly lower its annual growth targets. So far, there have been few signs that the government is willing to accept the trade-off.

A recent survey by China Beige Book, a data provider that tracks China's economy, of more than 3,000 Chinese companies shows that from July through September, hundreds of coal, steel, aluminum and copper companies reported a sixth straight quarter of overall capacity rising, not falling. Meanwhile, after a brief slowdown in credit growth, Chinese banks handed out more loans last month, indicating Beijing's preference for near-term stability over any drop in economic activity.

"This will not persist," said Yukon Huang, a former country director at the World Bank in China, referring to the near-term recovery of China's economy.

"Long-term growth depends on more fundamental drivers such as productivity growth," said Mr. Huang, who is now a senior fellow at the Carnegie Endowment for International Peace. "That hasn't happened yet."

Tough times loom for LSE after Rolet bows out

Philip Stafford in London 
1,208 words
19 October 2017
19:00
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2017 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Xavier Rolet transformed the London Stock Exchange Group from a share-trading venue to a global data and clearing powerhouse, delighting shareholders with its hugely-enhanced value.

His successor will inherit a company facing the political challenges of the UK s departure from the European Union hanging over it while markets rapidly evolve to encompass new technologies such as blockchain.

 He s had a great run but it s going to get a lot more difficult for the LSE,  says Niki Beattie, head of Market Structure Partners, a capital markets consultancy.  The LSE board will have to play a crucial role in thinking about the challenges the exchange will face. 

When the 57-year-old announced his retirement from the 319-year-old institution for a second time, he stressed there would be no immediate change to LSE s strategy.  The fact that I m going to be here for some time means it will be business as usual,  Mr Rolet told analysts on a conference call on Thursday.

When the merger with Deutsche Brse was agreed, Mr Rolet had been due to retire and the strategy set   based on the UK being in the EU. However, the deal fell apart and the next incumbent will need to reshape the company from the model that Mr Rolet created nearly a decade ago.

Less than a year before Mr Rolet s arrival as chief executive, the home to the shares of some of Europe s biggest banks, suffered an IT glitch for seven hours just days before Lehman Brothers collapsed.

Investors   who had watched the exchange lose market share to new cheaper competition and turn down takeover attempts from bigger rivals Nasdaq and Deutsche Brse   were aghast.

Now, its $17bn market capitalisation makes it the world s fifth largest exchange, and bigger than Nasdaq.

 I would expect the vacancy to attract a high-profile candidate with M&A experience and for the board to look internationally. Today s figures highlight that the business is in great shape,  says Trevor Green, head of UK equities at Aviva Investors, a long-term LSE shareholder.

The first of the many deals completed in Mr Rolet s tenure was the $30m purchase of MillienniumIT to fix defective technology.

But LSE was a mid-sized company that had missed out on several prized industry assets, notably the Liffe derivatives exchange.

Instead Mr Rolet, a former Goldman and Lehman banker, spent his time building closer relationships with big banks and spending more than 4bn on data and clearing businesses as markets evolved after the financial crisis.

Deals for index compilers Russell Investments and the 50 per cent it did not own in FTSE International were made in response to changing trends in fund management; the purchase of a controlling stake in LCH, the clearing house, was to exploit new rules demanding that more over-the-counter derivatives deals negotiated by banks were processed through the independent risk managers.

He has also focused on developing small and medium businesses from around the world, either to list or develop in a mentoring programme.

As a consequence, LSE s operating profits have risen from 182m on revenues of 605m in 2010 to 426.8m on revenues of 1.5bn.  At times we took risks, we made a few moves, sometimes they could be qualified as bold moves,  Mr Rolet told analysts on Thursday.

Mr Rolet has at least paved the way for the board to attract candidates from across financial markets. In her final year his predecessor Clara Furse was paid a base salary of 540,000 with a bonus of 648,000. Mr Rolet s base salary is 800,000 and his total remuneration last year was 5.7m, down from 6.5m a year ago.

But below the revenue and profit growth, his successor inherits problems.

 There s a lack of technical integration across a number of the assets. The organisation is quite siloed and that may impact performance without a strong and respected leader,  says Jake Pugh, an industry consultant.

Indicating the cost pressure in the fund management industry, this week State Street abandoned three FTSE exchange-trade funds benchmarks in favour of an in-house alternative.

A future chief executive will have to deal with the UK s departure from the EU as LSE s French clearing house and Borsa Italiana will remain inside the bloc. The successor will also have to address Europe s new incoming markets rules, known as Mifid II. The legislation will mandate greater transparency and competition in fixed income and derivatives markets, areas at which Mr Rolet has directed much of the LSE s attention in recent years.

Mr Rolet had been a big backer of the rule changes that give fund managers and banks more choice over what and where they trade. At a conference in Paris this week, he likened the current market to someone being forced to buy all insurance from the same place, bundled together.

 That would not make any sense to you, particularly in the age of internet and comparison sites. So why should the financial industry, the futures market in particular, continue to operate that way? 

Brussels may change the rules once the UK has left the EU.

But the biggest issue Mr Rolet s successor will face is the one that has dogged LSE since the turn of the century, the one that Mr Rolet tried to resolve with the Deutsche Brse deal   its independence.

For now uncertainty over the UK s future has deterred potential bidders. A successor may have to come up with another answer.

Hunt for successor could take up to a year

Hiring and installing a successor to Xavier Rolet could take up to a year, the London Stock Exchange admitted, as it said the chief executive would leave by the end of December 2018.

LSE said it would work closely with Mr Rolet to find a new chief and would look internally and externally to find the right candidate.

Few names emerged on Thursday, although analysts and executives expect there to be no shortage of interest.

With Mr Rolet s appointment in 2009, the LSE was among the first exchanges to recruit a former investment banker as its chief executive   a now-common industry trend. Evolving markets and exchange strategies could mean LSE again bucks the norm.

 The board will probably conduct a broad international search, and could seek candidates that have established careers in either information services or post-trade businesses, not in the traditional trading arena that exchange groups are historically known for,  said Peter Lenardos, an analyst at RBC Capital Markets.

For many years the industry touted David Lester, Mr Rolet s head of strategy and the architect of many of his deals, as a potential successor.

However Mr Lester left over the summer after more than 16 years at the LSE to pursue other interests.

One potential internal candidate is Mark Makepeace, group director of information services, which accounts for nearly 40 per cent of LSE profits. He was also a driving force in LSE s last big deal, the $685m purchase of Citigroup s global bond analytics business in August.

Pro Private Markets
Mya, With New Funding, Automates Recruiting; AI company is focused on business conversations

By Cat Zakrzewski 
456 words
19 October 2017
12:30
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Venture capitalists have been opening their wallets for artificial intelligence startups automating business conversations, from sales to customer support calls.

Mya Systems Inc. is applying this technology, known as natural language processing, to recruiting and hiring. When companies receive a large number of applications, Mya's chatbot helps screen candidates by asking them questions about their resume and scheduling interviews with them through SMS messages.

The company said on Thursday it had raised $18 million in Series B funding. With the new funding, the company plans to roll out its product in multiple languages by next year.

Foundation Capital led the round, with participation from Emergence Capital.

Forrester Analyst Rob Koplowitz said language expansion is very difficult because most machine learning systems are taught a very specific brand of English. He said many machines can't handle accents and dialects, let alone support multiple languages.

"You don't just have to teach it the language," Mr. Koplowitz said. "You have to teach it the idiomatic language."

Mya may have an advantage as it pushes into new international markets because it can apply its subject matter expertise in recruiting, Mr. Koplowitz said. He said they're not just teaching the machines a new language, they're teaching it recruiting terminology in those languages.

Launched in 2012, the company has raised about $30 million in venture funding to date. Mya Chief Executive Eyal Grayevsky said the company supports three of the world's five largest recruiting agencies, including Adecco.

Mya's advantage in expanding its languages it supports lies in its customers and partners, Foundation Capital Partner Joanne Chen said.

Mya has access to some of the largest recruiting data sets in the world through partners, Ms. Chen said. "With translation, it's just a data problem," she said.

In recent years, so-called chatbots have been hyped as AI technology, though not all efforts have been breakthrough innovations.

"There is a lot of hype around chatbots, but there is also a great deal of promise in the long-term," Ms. Chen said.

Consumer-facing chatbots have faltered, she said, but chatbots in the enterprise have been able to provide value by helping companies cut costs.

Mr. Grayevsky said Mya is committed to creating a seamless experience for recruiters and job candidates, no matter what language Mya is using to communicate with applicants. According to a survey the company conducted with prospective employees, 72% of job candidates thought Mya was a human.

"It only takes one bad experience," Mr. Grayevsky said, "to lose trust with the user or candidate."

Dow Hits Another Milestone, But Signs of Caution Loom; More money has flowed out of U.S. stock mutual and exchange-traded funds than has flowed in this year, according to EPFR Global

By Corrie Driebusch and Michael Wursthorn 
1,250 words
19 October 2017
02:04
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

The Dow Jones Industrial Average "https://www.wsj.com/articles/global-markets-rise-slightly-1508294771"on Wednesday, but the latest milestone masks a potentially perplexing trend: Investors keep yanking money out of stock funds.

Investors "https://blogs.wsj.com/moneybeat/2017/08/28/investors-have-pulled-30-billion-from-the-stock-market/"out of U.S. stock mutual and exchange-traded funds in the third quarter, according to EPFR Global. Overall in 2017, more money has flowed out of such funds than has flowed in, EPFR data show, even as the Dow has climbed to 51 fresh highs this year. A surge in stock buybacks and growing inflows from foreign investors and sovereign-wealth funds helped offset outflows from U.S. stock funds.

Many investors are concerned that the steady rise in U.S. indexes has left shares looking expensive. They also recently have grappled with elevated tensions between the U.S. and North Korea, hurricane-related disruptions to the economy and signals that the Federal Reserve is planning to "https://www.wsj.com/articles/fed-keeps-december-rate-rise-on-the-table-eyes-slower-path-for-increases-1505930513" and wind down its unprecedented "https://www.wsj.com/articles/the-fed-a-decade-after-the-crisis-is-about-to-embark-on-the-great-unwinding-1505746843"

The S&P 500 hasn't suffered a daily pullback of 3% or more since Nov. 4, the longest stretch without a decline of that magnitude since the mid-1990s. So far this year the Dow has surged 17%, compared with the S&P 500's 14% rise. Some investors worry that means stocks are overdue for a substantial selloff.

Others think the money leaving U.S. stock funds is a positive development. The outflows have coincided with elevated cash holdings and could signal major indexes can climb even higher. They figure with less invested in U.S. stock funds, there is plenty of money to put back in if investors get more optimistic.

These analysts and investors reckon some caution is healthy in a rising market. What would make them worry that a peak may be near, they say, is the kind of exuberance they remember seeing from investors in the dot-com boom in the late-1990s.

"As we hit milestones, it creates a lot of dialogue around 'oh my goodness the market is at all-time highs yet again,'" said Erik Davidson, chief investment officer at Wells Fargo Private Bank. He said investors he speaks with are happy their stock portfolios are going up, but they're also perplexed, concerned about news out of Washington, D.C., North Korea and natural disasters.

That worry makes Mr. Davidson feel more confident about the market's climb.

"The skepticism is healthy. There's not a lot of euphoria out there, and that's good for the further advancement of the market," he said.

On Wednesday, the "https://www.wsj.com/articles/global-markets-rise-slightly-1508294771", lifted by a jump in shares of International Business Machines Corp. IBM was the biggest gainer in the Dow, adding 89 points to the average, after "https://www.wsj.com/articles/ibm-has-another-revenue-drop-during-its-transition-1508273024" despite its 22nd consecutive quarter of year-to-year revenue declines.

 Robust corporate earnings and economic data have helped support stocks, "https://www.wsj.com/articles/market-volatility-has-vanished-around-the-world-1497860894", and the prospect of a "https://www.wsj.com/articles/republicans-unveil-plan-to-overhaul-u-s-tax-code-1506522802"has kept some investors hopeful.

 "There are some vultures waiting on the side of the road for the next downturn so they can step in," said Kristina Hooper, global market strategist at Invesco.

There were slight inflows into U.S. stock funds in September, according to EPFR's monthly figures. Before that, the last time money flowed in was March, capping a stretch of inflows that began the month of the U.S. election.

The amount of cash in investors' portfolios remains elevated historically, even though cash balances slipped to their lowest level in 2 years in October, according to a Bank of America Merrill Lynch survey of fund managers, which took into account responses from 179 fund managers with $516 billion in assets.

Stocks' climb has coincided with a recovery in U.S. corporate earnings, which slumped for several quarters before resuming growth in the second half of last year.

America's biggest companies are generally projected to report earnings growth again for the third quarter, even though expectations have been tempered in part because of the impact of major summer storms on insurers and other firms. As of the end of last month, analysts expected earnings at S&P 500 companies to rise 4.2% in the third quarter, according to FactSet.

"Investors continue to say there is a pretty positive story in terms of earnings growth," said Ron Temple, head of U.S. equities at Lazard Asset Management. "The core underpinnings of the market's strength is better than they have been in a long time."

Company stock repurchases have helped support share prices, some analysts say. In the second quarter, companies in the S&P 500 bought back $120 billion worth of stock, according to data from S&P Dow Jones Indices. Many analysts expect share repurchases among S&P 500 companies continued at a similar pace in the third quarter, though data aren't yet available. Buybacks can lift stock prices because they can decrease the number of shares available in the market, though some investors say they would rather companies spend that money on business development and expansion.

Yield-seekers outside the U.S. also could account for some of the stock market's gains.

Foreign investors have put roughly a net $40 billion into U.S. stocks so far this year through August, compared with more than $3.5 billion in net outflows last year, according to Deutsche Bank Global Markets Research.

The expectation that interest rates would rise slowly in the U.S. "is an important reason behind why we've seen an increase in allocation from foreigners," said Torsten Slok, chief international economist at Deutsche Bank.

The Federal Reserve has been gradually raising short-term interest rates since late 2015, and the central bank signaled that it is considering another increase in December. Interest rates have been ultralow in developed economies around the world since the financial crisis.

State-owned investment funds have snapped up U.S. equities. In 2017 through the end of August, about $5.1 billion in money directly from sovereign-wealth funds has flowed into U.S. stocks, according to data from the Sovereign Wealth Fund Institute. The data don't include money allocated to external fund managers, who in turn could invest in stocks.

Few analysts and investors see an imminent end to the rally that has taken the Dow industrials to several milestones since the election. The Dow advanced from 20000 to 21000 "https://www.wsj.com/articles/global-markets-higher-ahead-of-trump-address-1488332430" in March, tied for the fastest thousand-point gain in the index's history. The blue-chip index has finished the final calendar quarter higher than the previous quarter seven of the past eight years.

Banks Aim to Unleash the Robots on Bonds

By Telis Demos 
692 words
19 October 2017
The Wall Street Journal
J
B11
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Big investment banks are pushing to stoke more fully automated trading in the $6 trillion market for investment-grade corporate debt.

Banks including Credit Suisse Group AG, Goldman Sachs Group Inc. and Morgan Stanley have unleashed new trading software systems in recent months to pick up share.

In part because of its complexity and relatively low transaction volumes, that corner of Wall Street has been one of the more resistant to technological changes sweeping trading, lending and just about everything else in banking. A recent study by the Bank for International Settlements estimated that only 40% of investment-grade corporate bond trading was executed through computers rather than over the phone, compared with 75% in Treasury-debt trading, 80% in stocks and 90% in a broad array of futures contracts.

The investment banks are targeting small trades, those under $1 million, that otherwise might fall through the cracks. It is yet another example of banks turning to technology to try to generate revenue growth at relatively low cost.

Starting with the smaller trades is a safer route for the banks because it doesn't threaten the profit margins on the big trades they do with institutions.

While the banks are taking it slow, the smaller trades eventually may be a precursor to fully automating some bigger trades, which are often trickier to execute and require more human hand-holding.

"Banks see an opportunity to take advantage of trading opportunities they were missing in the past," said Richard Schiffman, head of open trading at MarketAxess Holdings Inc., operator of an electronic bond-trading network.

Some past efforts to change the patterns of bond trading have met resistance or petered out. Platforms offered several years ago by Wall Street firms including Goldman and money manager BlackRock Inc. were shelved or integrated into other efforts.

But more recent initiatives, such as networks connecting bond investors to trade with each other, have started to show traction.

Credit Suisse launched its system, called CSLiveEx, this year, making the decision to fully automate the smallest trades in the U.S. market for bonds of corporations with top credit. That means the trades have no direct human involvement.

In corporate bonds, traders working the phone still dominate the market, in part because there are so many various bonds to follow, all with different permutations such as maturity and yield. Some corporate bonds also don't trade that often after their initial sale, even for major companies like Apple Inc. In that type of market, salespeople who can influence buyers and sellers still add value.

"We are running with the machines, rather than letting them do the running on their own," said Samik Chandarana, a veteran credit trader who was recently named head of analytics and data science at J.P. Morgan Chase & Co.'s investment bank. J.P. Morgan and its peers, which tend to have bigger trading desks, have invested in technology as well, primarily to aid human traders.

While automated service is more prevalent in trading for retail bond investors, and has been technically feasible for several years, it is rarely used to respond to electronic trade requests from institutions such as pensions and hedge funds.

But the increasing amounts and sophistication of data is starting to change that. And banks are eager to make money out of the thousands of small electronic trade requests they get daily.

Credit Suisse says its new technology will help the firm serve more clients without adding extra traders or bond inventory. CSLiveEx is "designed to have as many customers as possible, without using a lot of balance sheet," said Julian Pomfret-Pudelsky, a fixed-income algorithmic trader at Credit Suisse who began working on the project in early 2016.

Goldman in 2015 began making a limited number of trades under $1 million via software called GS Algo and has this year expanded that to some 7,500 different individual bonds.

Morgan Stanley has also recently begun fully automated trading for smaller investment-grade corporate bonds, people familiar with the matter said.

Tracy's Take: Last  SIFI' Weighs How to Shake Label

SEC Names J.P. Morgan Executive as Top Regulator of Exchanges

Tezos Raised $232 Million in a Hot Coin Offering, Then a Fight Broke Out
