Canada's Immigrant-Friendly Startups Feast on Capital; Liberal immigration policies and access to universities are drawing tech investment

By Scott Martin 
339 words
17 October 2017
12:30
WSJ Pro Venture Capital
RSTPROVC
English
Copyright  2017, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Canadian startups, beneficiaries of coveted university machine learning talent and friendly immigration policies, are on pace this year to haul in record levels of venture-capital funding.

Investment in Canadian startups in the third quarter soared in terms of both dollars and deals. The region attracted a 32% uptick in funding, or $613 million, in the period compared with a year ago. The number of deals was up 18%.

Canada's startup scene may have directly benefited from an unfriendly U.S. immigration stance that has been disruptive for Silicon Valley entrepreneurs seeking foreign-born talent.

"Trump is helping us for sure. We have a startup visa," said Chris Arsenault, a managing partner at iNovia Capital, based in Montreal. "There is a lot money coming into Canadian companies."

The rise in capital to Canada's venture-backed startups sets the stage for reaching an all-time high, even as the Trump administration has "https://www.wsj.com/articles/trump-administration-moves-to-dismantle-obamas-startup-visa-rule-1499725991" visas for foreign-born entrepreneurs seeking entry into the U.S.

Top Canadian startup recipients of venture-capital financing included LeddarTech Inc., Milestone Pharmaceuticals Inc. and 2ndSite Inc., which scored $101 million, $55 million and $43 million, respectively.

Access to Canadian universities remains a draw for U.S. companies seeking the best and the brightest mathematical minds focused on machine learning. Google, Facebook, Samsung and IBM have all recently sought a presence in the area, said Mr. Arsenault, to tap into the area's talent. Facebook"https://www.wsj.com/articles/facebook-to-open-artificialintelligence-center-in-montreal-1505430201" an artificial intelligence office in Montreal.

"Toronto is an exciting startup hub, and it's gotten more exciting," said David Hornik, a general partner at August Capital, based in Menlo Park, Calif. "The combination of tax credits and a liberal immigration policy makes it a much more desirable place than a lot of cities in the U.S."

J.P. Morgan 's Jamie Dimon May Hate Bitcoin, but He Loves Blockchain; Bank rolls out a pilot program to use the record-keeping technology to speed global payments

By Emily Glazer 
582 words
16 October 2017
20:46
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

J.P. Morgan Chase & Co. Chief Executive James Dimon recently trashed the digital currency bitcoin as a fraud that will blow up. But he is still enamored with the technology that underpins it and other virtual currencies.

The latest sign came Monday, when the nation's largest bank rolled out its next pilot program to use the record-keeping technology, known as blockchain. The initiative will enable the faster, more secure transfer of cross-border payments between J.P. Morgan , Royal Bank of Canada and Australia and New Zealand Banking Group Ltd .

The new program doesn't use and won't trade bitcoin, which has "https://blogs.wsj.com/moneybeat/2017/09/12/jamie-dimon-says-bitcoin-is-a-fraud-that-will-blow-up"from a wide variety of financiers, including Mr. Dimon and BlackRock CEO Laurence Fink . But it will use technology that underpins the second-largest virtual currency behind bitcoin, Ethereum .

Both cryptocurrencies have soared this year in part due to optimism about the blockchain technology they use to track trades. In J.P. Morgan 's case, executives say the technology can dramatically speed up global payments

Processing cross-border payments today means dealing with a complex web of layers of communication between various participants to verify and process transactions. In some cases, global transactions from individuals or large corporations that could take as long as 15 days now could be shaved down to hours.

The new network will essentially link bank information together through a secure distributed ledger. That better connects the parties, who will continue to keep reams of their own information on separate private servers. Connecting the data through the shared network should significantly reduce the number of steps needed, for instance, to check and rectify mismatches in a cross-border payment.

J.P. Morgan receives on average 150,000 inquiries on payments annually. Those inquiries can range from getting the full client name, address or date of birth to a digit missing from an account number, said Emma Loftus, head of global payments and FX for J.P. Morgan Treasury Services.

Ms. Loftus added that the bank will also use its traditional payment methods as it tests the pilot.

RBC and ANZ are the first two banks to join the network, dubbed Interbank Information Network, but J.P. Morgan hopes to increase the number of bank partners to about two dozen in the next six to 12 months, she said. Executives at RBC and ANZ said they joined with J.P. Morgan in efforts to improve their cross-border payments.

J.P. Morgan has used blockchain technology to improve processes in securities, derivatives, custody services and transaction banking. The bank recently had about a half a dozen prototypes in the works using blockchain. This latest use has been in the works for about six months, said Umar Farooq, head of channels, analytics and innovation for the bank's treasury services division.

The efforts fall in line with "https://blogs.wsj.com/moneybeat/2017/10/13/why-james-dimon-and-larry-fink-arent-buying-bitcoin/" on Friday in which he criticized bitcoin as having "no actual value" and predicted "governments are going to crush it." But he praised technology innovations that could come from blockchain.

"We actually use it. It will be useful for a lot of different things," Mr. Dimon said at a conference in Washington. "God bless the blockchain."

Ryan Tracy contributed to this article.

Clearinghouses Pass Stress Tests | Dimon Sour on Bitcoin but Sweet on Blockchain | Buell's Take: The Road Toward New Overnight Rates; The Wall Street Journal's financial regulation newsletter for Monday, October 16, 2017.

35,315 words
16 October 2017
13:13
WSJ Pro Financial Regulation
RSTPROFR
English
Copyright  2017, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Buell's Take: The Road Toward New Overnight Rates

Major Clearinghouses Can Avoid Liquidity Crunch in Crisis, CFTC Says

J.P. Morgan's Jamie Dimon May Hate Bitcoin, But He Still Loves the Blockchain

CFPB Moves Back to Renovated Building Near White House

Regulator Fight Flares Anew Over Arbitration Rule As GOP Gears Up To Vote

The Road Toward New Overnight Rates

A "https://www.wsj.com/articles/ecb-set-to-publish-new-overnight-lending-rate-1505986875" could change the benchmark for assessing financial contracts and add credibility to a system tarnished by scandal.

The ECB said last month that it was working to develop an overnight benchmark interest rate, which it hopes to have in place by 2020. The move is part of a global trend of central banks and regulators reassessing overnight interest rates, most prominently Libor, in the wake of scandals.

"The whole rationale behind all of this is to move away from questionable practices," said Michael Huertas, a banking lawyer with Baker McKenzie in Frankfurt.

Libor, or the London interbank offered rate, is "https://www.wsj.com/articles/u-k-calls-time-on-scandal-hit-libor-1501148216". The benchmark, used to set the price of trillions of dollars in loans and derivatives world-wide, is an imperfect measure: It is based on asking banks to estimate how much it might cost them to borrow from other banks. "https://www.wsj.com/articles/libor-a-eulogy-for-the-worlds-most-important-number-1501170720" that resulted in financial penalties for banks and prison time for traders.

The ECB's new rate, in contrast, is based on actual transactions. The central bank says its new rate is meant to supplement existing ones, not necessarily replace them. But given the failures of the private-sector rates, it seems reasonable to expect that market participants will increasingly turn to rates provided by the ECB and other central banks.

The Federal Reserve in August opened for public comment "https://www.wsj.com/articles/fed-seeks-comment-on-three-proposed-borrowing-benchmarks-1503605867" for firms using U.S. Treasurys as collateral for short-term loans.

In the eurozone, the ECB's move could have implications for citizens who don't generally read specialist financial publications.

In much of southern Europe, the "https://www.wsj.com/articles/as-interest-benchmarks-go-negative-banks-may-have-to-pay-borrowers-1428939338" is the euro interbank offered rate, or Euribor. The rate is based on how much it costs European banks to borrow from each other. The European Commission estimates that Euribor underpins  180 trillion ($213 trillion) in contracts.

The new ECB rate is officially seen more as a complement to another overnight rate, Eonia, or the euro overnight index average, rather than Euribor. In short, Eonia is based on transaction data, while Euribor is based on quotes provided by banks. But both rely on data submissions that are voluntary and hard to verify, say experts.

"The general concept of Eonia and Euribor is similar," said Falko Fecht, a professor at the Frankfurt School of Finance and Management. Both are "susceptible to misreporting," he added.

Eonia relies on data from 28 banks, with one contribution from each bank. The new ECB rate envisages collecting data from 52 banks and will rely on individual transactions, resulting in a broader set of data to work with, and one that's harder to manipulate.

Mr. Fecht said he "very much" expects the ECB's new rate to replace existing benchmark rates on mortgage contracts. Mr. Huertas concurred, with the caveat that much will depend on how the new European rate is determined.

Key Developments in Washington, on Wall Street, and Beyond

Major Clearinghouses Can Avoid Liquidity Crunch in Crisis, CFTC Says

By Gabriel T. Rubin

WASHINGTON Major clearinghouses for derivatives can withstand substantial stress to the financial system and avoid a liquidity crunch in a crisis, the Commodity Futures Trading Commission said Monday in a report that marks the second time it has "stress-tested" the entities and found they passed.

The CFTC's report came a day after National Economic Council Director Gary Cohn said he was worried about whether clearinghouses are being overused, adding that regulators don't have guidelines for resolving a clearinghouse "in an orderly fashion." Mr. Cohn's remarks Sunday at a Group of 30 banking seminar in Washington add to calls from U.S. policy makers to keep a close eye on clearinghouses.

The CFTC tested three of the largest U.S. clearinghouses  CME Group Inc.'s CME Clearing, Intercontinental Exchange Inc.'s ICE Clear U.S. and LCH Clearnet Group Ltd.'s LCH Ltd. and found that each would be able to generate sufficient liquidity to keep markets functioning even if two of their major clearing member banks were to default.

Clearinghouses, mostly used by banks and investment firms, are meant to help avoid a marketwide collapse by making sure that either party in a derivatives transaction is paid if the other side falters. Their role has grown since the 2008 financial crisis as regulators encouraged derivatives to be routed through them.

Clearinghouses conduct their own tests to determine how they would function under extreme market conditions. While the CFTC tests examined what would happen if two major clearing members defaulted, some clearinghouses test what would happen if as many as four member banks failed simultaneously. Given the size of clearing member banks, such a situation would undoubtedly be in the midst of a major financial crisis.

The supersizing of certain clearinghouses after the financial crisis has gotten significant attention from U.S. policy makers in recent months. The House Agriculture Committee held a July hearing devoted to clearinghouse resolution, and Federal Reserve governor Jerome Powell has said repeatedly in public remarks that stress tests for clearinghouses must be more robust and reflective of the risk they pose to financial stability.

"Central clearing will only make the financial system safer if [clearinghouses] themselves are run safely," Mr. Powell, who is said to be a top candidate for Fed chairman, said in a June speech.

The Treasury Department has also taken an interest in stress tests for clearinghouses, saying in its report on capital markets earlier this month that regulators should build on existing tests to make sure clearinghouses have plans for winding down in an orderly fashion in the event of a major crisis.

J.P. Morgan's Jamie Dimon May Hate Bitcoin, But He Still Loves the Blockchain

By Emily Glazer

J.P. Morgan Chase JPM -0.14% & Co. CEO James Dimon recently trashed the digital currency bitcoin as a fraud that will blow up. But he's still enamored with the technology that underpins it and other virtual currencies.

The latest sign is expected Monday, when the nation's largest bank rolls out its next pilot program to use the record-keeping technology, known as blockchain. The initiative will enable the faster, more secure transfer of cross-border payments between J.P. Morgan, Royal Bank of Canada RY 0.27% and Australia and New Zealand Banking Group Ltd. ANZBY 0.96%

The new program doesn't use and won't trade bitcoin, which has generated skepticism from a wide variety of financiers, including Mr. Dimon and BlackRock CEO Laurence Fink. But it will use technology that underpins the second-largest virtual currency behind bitcoin, Ethereum.

Both cryptocurrencies have soared this year in part due to optimism about the blockchain technology they use to track trades. In J.P. Morgan's case, executives say the technology can dramatically speed up global payments

Processing cross-border payments today means dealing with a complex web of layers of communication between various participants to verify and process transactions. In some cases, global transactions from individuals or large corporations that could take as long as 15 days now could be shaved down to hours.

The new network will essentially link bank information together through a secure distributed ledger. That better connects the parties, who will continue to keep reams of their own information on separate private servers. Connecting the data through the shared network should significantly reduce the number of steps needed, for instance, to check and rectify mismatches in a cross-border payment.

J.P. Morgan receives on average 150,000 inquiries on payments annually. Those inquiries can range from getting the full client name, address or date of birth to a digit missing from an account number, said Emma Loftus, head of global payments and FX for J.P. Morgan Treasury Services.

Ms. Loftus added that the bank will also use its traditional payment methods as it tests the pilot.

RBC and ANZ are the first two banks to join the network, dubbed Interbank Information Network, but J.P. Morgan hopes to increase the number of bank partners to about two dozen in the next six to 12 months, she said. Executives at RBC and ANZ said they joined with J.P. Morgan in efforts to improve their cross-border payments.

J.P. Morgan has used blockchain technology to improve processes in securities, derivatives, custody services and transaction banking. The bank recently had about a half a dozen prototypes in the works using blockchain. This latest use has been in the works for about six months, said Umar Farooq, head of channels, analytics and innovation for the bank's treasury services division.

The efforts fall in line with comments from Mr. Dimon on Friday in which he criticized bitcoin as having "no actual value" and predicted "governments are going to crush it." But he praised technology innovations that could come from blockchain.

"We actually use it. It will be useful for a lot of different things," Mr. Dimon said at a conference in Washington. "God bless the blockchain."

 Ryan Tracy contributed to this article.

CFPB Moves Back to Renovated Building Near White House

By Yuka Hayashi

WASHINGTON A consumer financial regulator some Republicans have been trying to kill for years is moving back to a fully renovated building in a prime location across from the White House.

The Consumer Financial Protection Bureau's director, Richard Cordray, is expected to move into the building at 1700 G St. NW this week following a $145 million renovation project. Mr. Cordray's term at the independent agency runs until July 2018 but Republican critics of the bureau have called on President Donald Trump to fire him.

The renovated building will house about 1,200 of the bureau's 1,600 employees. The move has taken place in phases in recent weeks and is mostly complete. The transition has been a low-key affair, with no ribbon-cutting ceremony scheduled.

The CFPB's Democratic founders, including Sen. Elizabeth Warren (D., Mass.), hoped the prominent location of its headquarters would raise the public profile of the agency, which was created after the financial crisis to empower consumers. But like much of the CFPB's work, the renovation of a building that once housed the defunct Office of Thrift Supervision became mired in partisan controversy.

The move comes at a pivotal time for the six-year-old agency. Mr. Cordray might step down soon to run for Ohio governor, and the CFPB is likely to go through a significant overhaul once a Trump appointee takes over its helm.

Yet the relocation is another example of how the CFPB is pressing ahead with its longstanding agenda despite pressure from Congress and the White House to curb its power. Since Republicans won the White House, the CFPB has brought enforcement actions at a steady pace and rolled out two major regulations in recent months on payday loans and arbitration despite opposition from industry and Republicans.

The bureau's opponents have criticized the building project for years.

House Financial Services Committee Chairman Jeb Hensarling (R., Texas) reproved its glass staircase and a waterfall in the lobby, comparing its building cost to that of Trump World Tower in Manhattan. Mr. Hensarling suggested a cheaper location could have been found elsewhere, such as Reston, Va., a Washington suburb.

After an examination of the project in 2015, the Office of Inspector General said the construction costs "appeared reasonable."

Mr. Cordray has defended the renovation as unfortunate but necessary. The work, managed by the General Services Administration, includes upgrading air conditioning, plumbing and electrical systems and replacing 35-year-old windows with energy-efficient ones.

"This is a government-owned building that needed renovations to properly ensure the health and safety of its occupants," said CFPB spokesman Sam Gilford.

During the renovation, which began in 2014, most CFPB employees have been working in a temporary location across town.

The 300,000-square-foot building sits across the street from the Eisenhower Executive Office Building, which houses a majority of White House staff.

The CFPB declined to say where Mr. Cordray's office is located. From the street, a large flag with gold trims is visible in a fourth-floor corner office facing toward the White House.

"With its headquarters in the heart of Washington, D.C. just across the street from the White House complex the new agency will offer a very tangible presence for anyone who comes to our nation's capital," Ms. Warren said in 2011.

Built in 1977, the building is an example of Brutalist architecture, which left a number of boxy concrete structures across Washington. It was leased to the CFPB under a 20-year contract by the Office of the Comptroller of the Currency, which absorbed the OTS when it was shut down by the 2010 Dodd-Frank financial-overhaul law.

Regulator Fight Flares Anew Over Arbitration Rule As GOP Gears Up To Vote

By Yuka Hayashi

WASHINGTON A war of words between two regulators flared anew Friday over a rule barring financial firms from requiring customers to use arbitration to settle disputes, as the Senate moved closer to a possible vote to reject the initiative.

The dispute relates to a regulation unveiled by the Consumer Financial Protection Bureau in July that makes it easier for consumers to bring group lawsuits by banning fine-print requirements for arbitration in financial services contracts. On one side of the fight is the CFPB led by Director Richard Cordray, an Obama appointee whose term runs till next July. Confronting him is acting Comptroller of the Currency Keith Noreika, a banking industry lawyer brought in by the Trump administration until a permanent replacement is confirmed.

The unusual back-and-forth between the top regulators started when the CFPB rule was announced.

Senate Republicans are gearing up for a possible vote as early as next week on a measure to overturn the rule. It is widely opposed by the financial industry, which has sued to stop it. It isn't clear whether lawmakers have enough votes to kill the rule.

Mr. Noreika kicked off the latest chapter earlier Friday with an op-ed in the Hill. In it, he accused the CFPB of failing to disclose data that he said undermined the legitimacy of the rule. Mr. Noreika said an analysis by the regulator he is temporarily running showed an 88% chance that the rule would boost consumer credit costs for consumers, resulting in a 3.5 point increase in credit-card rates.

"For an agency that demands transparency from the companies it supervises, the omission is an appalling abdication of the bureau's responsibility to consumers," Mr. Noreika wrote.

The CFPB countered Mr. Noreika's attack by publishing a new report on the rule's effect on consumers, arguing there is no statistical evidence it would have a significant impact on credit costs. The bureau also said some of the data it used when designing the rule is confidential information not suitable for publishing.

The CFPB said the OCC's analysis is "based on flawed statistics and is contradicted by publicly available historical data that the OCC did not consider."

Congressional Republicans hope to use the Congressional Review Act, a legislative tool that allows them to overturn a new regulation with a simple majority vote.

The House voted in July to kill the rule; the Senate has until early November.

 Andrew Ackerman contributed to this article

Social Security Numbers: Hacked, Hated and Irreplaceable

By Telis Demos and Peter Rudegeair

No one intended for the Social Security number to become the cornerstone of Americans' financial lives. But it has, and the Equifax Inc. hack has shown how ill-suited the nine-digit figure is to this role.

Now, bankers and policy makers are saying it is time to find a better tool to help establish an individual's identity. The trouble is, at least when it comes to finance, there is no easy replacement.

Having a universal, standardized data point for establishing someone's identity has helped fuel the drive toward increasingly seamless digital access to financial services and credit. Millions of consumers can open a bank account or get approved for a credit card online with just a few pieces of information, among them their Social Security number.

Alternatives are available, including ones based on smartphones or biometric data. But they each come with their own problems, and rarely have the ubiquity of Social Security numbers.

The risk is that new methods of authenticating a person's identity throw sand in the gears of the U.S. consumer credit system, making it slower or more difficult for people to get financing. That could affect the U.S. economy: Americans had $3.7 trillion in outstanding credit in the second quarter, helping to fuel $13.3 trillion in spending roughly 70% of the total U.S. gross domestic product, according to Federal Reserve data.

"There isn't in the market right now a viable singular replacement for a Social Security number," said Al Pascual, research director and head of fraud and security at consulting firm Javelin Strategy & Research. "That Social Security number has become a linchpin from an identity point and an authentication standpoint."

But the flaws in the status quo are becoming increasingly apparent. The Equifax breach exposed Social Security numbers, along with other vital pieces of information used to authenticate someone's identity, of potentially 145.5 million Americans.

That has prompted policy makers to think about ways to cut back on the use of Social Security numbers. Earlier this month, the Trump administration's top cybersecurity official said the Social Security number has "outlived its usefulness." Last week, a top Republican in Congress introduced a bill that would require the major credit-reporting firms to phase out by 2020 the use of Social Security numbers to verify consumers' identities.

Despite its pervasiveness, the Social Security number, created in 1936, was "never intended to serve as a personal identification document," according to a history published by the government agency that administers it.

Yet, by the 1960s, its use was widespread. Banks began using the identifier to solve their own headaches matching up the account records of "literally millions of Smiths, Joneses, Williamses, and Johnsons," according to a 1968 report by the American Bankers Association.

"When the banks adopted the Social Security number, it lent legitimacy to the entire idea" in the private sector, said Josh Lauer, an associate professor at the University of New Hampshire and the author of a history of credit-reporting firms.

The ABA said at the time that it liked the Social Security number because it was unique, permanent and used by more than 80% of adults. It also said the number was secure because "knowledge of an individual's Social Security number gives no one any access to any information about that individual."

Credit-reporting firms such as Equifax also started using the number to help authenticate applicants. Such firms took on the role of stockpiling consumers' digits because the Social Security Administration made it difficult to cross-check an applicant's information with what it had on file.

After 1972, Social Security cards no longer had "Not for Identification" printed on them.

The advent of the digital era only accelerated the trend. As banks moved away from branch-based service in which they could at least eyeball every customer as part of verifying them toward web and mobile services, the need for a speedy way to identify unknown borrowers increased greatly.

The problem, credit specialists said, is that Social Security numbers weren't simply used as numerical versions of a first name to identify a person, but as a keyword to authenticate that the person is who they say they are. "It is basically a public number," said Ram Palaniappan, chief executive of financial-technology startup ActiveHours. "It should have never been used for authentication, to unlock anything."

Over the years, banks have defended this usage. In both 2001 and 2006, the Financial Services Sector Coordinating Council, a banking industry group, told Congress that by using Social Security numbers to "verify individual identities," credit-reporting firms can "quickly provide financial institutions with accurate credit histories."

Restrictions could result in "costly delays" and drive "up processing costs," the group said.

More recently, banking industry groups have advocated the use of multiple ways to verify identity. Some lenders have started asking applicants to submit additional electronic documents to prove their identities, such as a picture of a driver's license. Others have enlisted service providers that can pull in additional information about customers behind-the-scenes.

One set of those tools scans the features of the computers or smartphones that their customers use to log on to their accounts. It then matches that device history with what they have on file. Another tool looks at behavior quirks of customers, such as how long they take to type in their credentials, and flags logins that look unusual.

Consumers may also simply be issued new identifiers. For example the government agency overseeing Medicare next year will begin shifting away from its current IDs, based on Social Security numbers, and issue new, unique codes.

While some of those processes could make the credit system more cumbersome, that might be the price to pay to ditch Social Security numbers. "Lenders will have to introduce friction. That's the only stopgap that works today," said Thomas Nicholas, chief executive of Alloy, an identification software startup that works with banks. "There may be long-term, frictionless solutions. But the rollout of that &might be decades."

EU's Dombrovskis Sees Trump Team Cooperating Positively on Financial Rules

By Ryan Tracy

WASHINGTON European officials see cooperation with the Trump administration on financial sector oversight "developing in a positive way," according to a top official who met with U.S. regulators on Friday.

European Commission Vice President Valdis Dombrovskis in an interview said European Union fears about President Donald Trump's financial deregulatory agenda haven't been realized.

"We had certain concerns after having listened to President Trump's announcements of doing a big number on Dodd-Frank and what the implications of that would be," Mr. Dombrovskis said, referring to the U.S. president's promises to dismantle the 2010 financial regulatory law.

"What is reassuring for us [is] we are not seeing a push toward financial sector deregulation," but rather the U.S. and Europe are both seeking "targeted adjustments" to the regulatory regime, he said. "There is a clear understanding of the lessons learned from the crisis."

Mr. Dombrovskis leads financial-services policy work at the EU's executive arm and spoke after recent meetings with Treasury Secretary Steven Mnuchin, Federal Reserve Vice Chairman of Supervision Randal Quarles, Securities and Exchange Commission Chairman Jay Clayton and other senior U.S. officials.

One example of cooperation occurred Friday when U.S. and European policy makers agreed on steps toward harmonizing postcrisis swap rules. The moves will allow U.S. and European firms to follow home region rules while doing business across the Atlantic.

"These measures are critical to maintaining the integrity of our swaps markets," Commodity Futures Trading Commission Chairman J. Christopher Giancarlo said at a joint press conference with Mr. Dombrovskis.

Mr. Dombrovskis has expressed concerns in the past about signals from the Trump administration that it would pare back regulations the Obama administration had agreed to implement as part of international agreements.

For instance, a report from the Treasury Department in June suggested that the U.S. pause work on rules related to banks' liquidity and trading books.

After meeting with U.S. officials, Mr. Dombrovskis said it appears they are willing to work with other countries to recalibrate the rules, rather than pulling away from them unilaterally.

"It was also clear that the U.S. is willing to work on those standards," he said. "What we are emphasizing is not to depart unilaterally from the agreements and I would say today's meetings were also more reassuring in this regard &. We currently see that there is commitment for international financial cooperation."

Asked about the progressing international discussions about modifications to bank capital rules, Mr. Dombrovskis said "we believe that the agreement is within reach."

That was consistent with comments earlier Friday by William Coen, secretary-general of the Basel Committee group of global bank regulators, which is working on sticking points between U.S. and European regulators. The outlines of the agreement are "fairly clear," Mr. Coen said.

Mr. Dombrovskis also gave an upbeat assessment of the global economy, saying he sees "broad-based economic growth" across the world.

He said European banks in particular have made progress to improve their health, though he acknowledged some large firms have been struggling to achieve sustained profitability, as outlined by the International Monetary Fund in a report earlier this week.

"Yes, this is an issue, but we believe that Europe's banking system can cope with this issue," he said, pointing out that over time the European Central Bank should be able to raise interest rates, which should help boost bank profits.

Mr. Dombrovskis said consolidation between banks could help improve efficiency, and he doesn't believe authorities are holding back mergers: "So far, I would not say that there have been major regulatory barriers for mergers, especially for those banking sectors where those mergers would help to improve profitability and bank efficiency."

 Gabriel T. Rubin contributed to this article.

CFTC, EU Reach Swaps Agreements as New European Rules Loom

By Gabriel T. Rubin

WASHINGTON U.S. and European policy makers Friday took steps toward harmonizing postcrisis swap rules with agreements that will allow U.S. and European firms to follow home region rules while doing business across the Atlantic.

The Commodity Futures Trading Commission and the European Commission reached an agreement to recognize each other's collateral rules and vowed to complete a plan to do the same for swap trading venues that operate in each others' jurisdictions.

Global regulators have generally sought to harmonize their approach to these rules, but progress has been slow, particularly in Europe where the completion of collateral rules was delayed repeatedly. And now, with major new market rules, known as Mifid II, slated to come into effect in Europe in 2018, U.S. and European regulators needed to reach agreements that would allow firms to continue operating in each others' jurisdictions while not causing major market disruption and fragmentation.

"These measures are critical to maintaining the integrity of our swaps markets," CFTC Chairman J. Christopher Giancarlo said at a press conference with European Commission Vice President Valdis Dombrovskis, who was in Washington for the annual meetings of the International Monetary Fund and World Bank.

"It is important that European counterparts can continue trading in derivatives on authorized U.S. markets," Mr. Dombrovskis said.

The agreement on collateral affects derivatives rules in both the U.S. and Europe that require banks to set aside cash as a cushion against the risk of certain swaps trades going bad. The cash cushion, or margin, applies to swaps contracts that aren't routed through central clearinghouses.

The second half of the agreement, on trading venues for uncleared swaps, won't be finished until November because the European Commission must vote on its terms following a period of consultations. But with Mr. Dombrovskis' strong support, the agreement is virtually assured of passage.

Under the terms of the trading venue agreement, the CFTC will provide exemptions for certain European swap trading platforms that want to do business in the U.S. After Mifid II goes into effect in 2018, European regulators will give the CFTC a list of Mifid-compliant firms that would be eligible for exemptions from U.S. rules. The CFTC will vote on that exemption once Europe has provided the list of eligible trading platforms, according to Eric Pan, the CFTC's director of the Office of International Affairs.

Mr. Giancarlo said the agreement on trading venues "goes a long way toward assuaging concerns" about market fragmentation that could result from the implementation of Mifid II.

The two agreements come as the EU and CFTC are engaged in more tense negotiations over standards for clearinghouse supervision. While an agreement was reached in early 2016 after years of hard-fought negotiations, the deal is now at risk of falling apart amid the uncertainty of Brexit.

The European Commission has proposed legislation to have its own regulators, including the European Central Bank, oversee U.S. clearinghouses that clear euro-denominated swaps. The proposed law could force clearinghouses that do a large of chunk of business in euros to move into the EU and could subject large, or "systemic," clearinghouses operating outside the EU to tougher oversight, including on-site inspections and access to financial accounts.

Currently, under the 2016 agreement, the EU accepts CFTC oversight of U.S. clearinghouses operating in London. The agreement allows major U.S. clearinghouses like CME Group Inc. and Intercontinental Exchange Inc. to do business with European banks while adhering to U.S. regulatory standards, because both the U.S. and the EU accept each other's rules as equivalent.

Mr. Giancarlo told a congressional committee earlier this week that the EU plan posed major risks for markets and that he wasn't interested in renegotiating the agreement.

"Where I come from, a deal is a deal," he said.

Mr. Dombrovskis didn't comment on clearinghouse supervision during Friday's briefing.

Two Chinese Firms Bidding for Chicago Exchange Withdraw From Deal

By Dave Michaels

WASHINGTON Several Chinese investors vying for a piece of the U.S.'s last regional stock exchange have dropped out of the deal, people familiar with the matter said, after the deal weathered intense political criticism of their bid.

Two of the Chinese investors, Chongqing Jintian Industrial Co., Ltd. and Chongqing Longshang Decoration Co., Ltd., withdrew in the past two weeks, after the Securities and Exchange Commission sought more details from the participants about their bid, the people said. The SEC's three commissioners are deciding whether to approve the sale after the commission's chairman overrode an earlier staff recommendation to approve it.

The Chicago exchange plans to file records with the SEC disclosing the investors' exit as soon as next week, the people said. The filing will probably require the SEC to once again seek public comment on the ownership change, further delaying a decision on a deal that was announced 20 months ago. A ruling from the SEC now likely won't be issued before November.

An SEC spokeswoman declined to comment.

Chinese conglomerate Chongqing Casin Enterprise Group Co. which is seeking to buy 20% of CHX Holdings Inc., with other Chinese and U.S. investors buying the rest remains the lead investor in the deal. It isn't clear whether existing U.S. bidders plan to increase their stakes to replace the Chinese suitors who dropped out. Alternately, CHX Holdings could recruit new American investors to replace the lost funding, the people said.

Chongqing Jintian Industrial Co. Ltd. and Chongqing Longshang Decoration Co. Ltd. had agreed to invest about $8 million as part of a consortium that offered $20 million to purchase the exchange, rising to $25 million if the exchange hit future revenue targets. The firms' managers dropped out about two weeks ago because they felt abused by political criticism of the deal in Congress and the slow progress of approval at the SEC, the people said.

Their withdrawal means China-based investors would own just 20% of the exchange, down from 49.5%. The group's U.S. investors include Raptor Group, the family office of former hedge-fund manager Jim Pallotta; and Anthony Saliba, a current board member of CHX Holdings who runs an options-trading and technology firm, Matrix Holding Group.

Chongqing Jintian is a real estate and construction firm, while Chongqing Longshang produces indoor and outdoor decorations, according to documents that Casin filed with the SEC.

The sale sparked a political outcry about a Chinese takeover of a key U.S. market utility, despite the Chicago exchange's tiny role in the American equity market. The Chicago exchange handles less than 1% of daily trading volume.

Rep. Robert Pittenger (R., N.C.) has led a political attack against Casin's role in buying the Chicago bourse. Mr. Pittenger started criticizing the deal just after it was announced, citing national security concerns in a letter to regulators that was signed by 45 other House lawmakers.

"While it is alarming that several Chinese government-affiliated companies made it this far in the process, this is a good sign that our system still promotes the well-being and security of our national interests," Mr. Pittenger said Friday.

One of Mr. Pittenger's own former staff members, Chas Thomas, has signed up to lobby Congress against the deal. Mr. Thomas's firm, Thorn Run Partners, disclosed on Sept. 28 that it is lobbying alongside Adam Olsen, who represents another group of potential investors seeking to buy the Chicago market if the SEC denies Casin's proposal.

The members of Mr. Olsen's investor group remain a secret. He said in late September that his clients are "very substantial." He didn't respond to a request this week seeking comment.

CHX Holdings has recently pushed back against Mr. Pittenger's criticism, saying the lawmaker's rhetoric has mischaracterized the deal and the independence of its investors.

CHX and Casin executives say the Chinese company is independent of its national government. Casin has 1,300 employees and $1.2 billion in assets, with interests in several banks, insurance and construction firms, according to a recent letter it filed with the SEC. Casin has agreed to make all of its pertinent records available to the SEC and would respond to any regulatory or legal inquiries in the future.

"CHX has responded to concerns from legitimate commenters as well as  fake news' comment letters containing blatantly false and misleading information," wrote James Ongena, CHX's general counsel. "A constructive dialogue must be based upon accurate facts."
