WSJ Pro Transcript: Panel Discussion With BOE's Haldane, ECB's Coeur

Bank of England Chief Economist Andrew Haldane gave a presentation that was followed by a panel discussion with Princeton University professor Markus Brunnermeier, European Central Bank executive board member Benot Coeur, Brookings Institution's Hutchins Center on Fiscal and Monetary Policy senior fellow Nellie Liang, and Harvard University professor Jeremy Stein, during a Peterson Institute for International Economics conference on "Rethinking Macroeconomic Policy" in Washington on Thursday, Oct. 12, 2017. Peterson Institute President Adam S. Posen served as moderator. Here is a transcript of the exchange, lightly edited for length and clarity.

ADAM S. POSEN: I look at this panel and I'm awed by its intellect and its real-world relevance. And then I worry these are too many insiders. So let's see where this discussion goes.

Let me turn first to Markus Brunnermeier of Princeton University.

MARKUS BRUNNERMEIER: Thanks a lot for inviting me. It's a pleasure to be here. It's always hard to follow up on [Bank of England Chief Economist] Andy [Haldane], because I don't have a Frisbee and I didn't bring a dog either.

So I recommend highly to read this piece of work Andy wrote with his co-authors, which covers a lot of ground. It's very detailed and it goes   it's very comprehensive on what happened in the regulatory framework. And it also goes into macro models, to what extent we can use these macro models to calibrate, you know, what capital requirements we need and all the other dimensions Andy mentioned.

So rather than going over the whole thing, because it covers too much, in my eight minutes I will just highlight five different points I think where there were shifts in our thinking in the last few years how to deal with that.

The first thing I would say, within the macro models, is a shift away from thinking about stocks and flows to think much more about the risk focus. And I will mention this paradox of prudence compared to a paradox of thrift later on.

The second dimension I would like to mention is the focus before the crisis was very much on the risk of a financial institution on its own and the risk in isolation, focusing much more on the spillovers, the externalities an institution can cause onto the system. That was a fundamental shift how you measure risk and how you capture risk.

And the third dimension is more in the time dimension, where it's essentially saying if you measure volatility, current volatility, it's very low. That's actually not a sign that the system is healthy. It might be a sign that there's some risk building up in the background. OK, so that's what [Peterson Institute senior fellow] Olivier [Blanchard] mentioned early on. So it can be very misleading if current volatility measures are very low. That's the volatility paradox.

The fourth dimension I would like to mention is the separation principle we saw before the crisis all over the place. You put things in little boxes and you treat them in the box separately. And we saw that actually many of these things, in particular prudential policy, which is now macroprudential, is very closely tied to monetary policy. And monetary policy has a lot to do also with shifting wealth around and recapitalizing certain sectors.

And finally, I would like to contrast some stability focus and contrast it with some resiliency dimension.

OK, let me focus on the first one. The first one is shifting from a focus on stocks and flows to much more focusing on the risk dimension. What I mean by the risk dimension, of course, we always have models where there's some uncertainty and risk in it. But to focus on the volatility dynamics itself, rather than have a one-time shock and then you see how the impulse response is and how you go back, you focus on the whole volatility dynamics, how the volatility would play out. If you have a one-time shock, is the volatility going up further or further down? What's the whole volatility dynamics?

I think this is very important to focus on these high-water moments because it then also feeds back in precautionary savings and consumption behavior and so forth. And also, one aspect which played out very prominently in the crisis was this flight to safety, flight into safe assets, and the role of safe assets, which became way more prominent in the crisis, and in the research has enlarged and has   (inaudible)   in the optimal regulatory framework.

Let me contrast this just with two paradoxes. And Andy mentioned this in the paper, this fallacy of composition, which is very, very important. You should not consider something which is true in the microsphere is also true in the macro sphere. And there's the famous Keynes paradox of thrift. If everybody is more thrifty, spends less   your spending is my income, essentially   then my income is going down. And if everybody lowers their marginal propensity to consume, actually, at the end of the day, I try to save more, but in dollar terms actually I might actually save less, because the economy contracts because of that.

You can do a similar thing   and Yuliy and I, we're pushing this term, "paradox of prudence." When you try to be more prudent instead of cutting back your spending, you actually cut back on your risk-taking, so you're more   less risk-taking. Actually, this increases endogenous risk in the system. So everybody tries to take on less risk, but by doing so, actually endogenously the risk in the system is going up.

So what's micro-prudent is actually not macro prudent, because by being more prudent on your own, on your microsphere, and banking regulation is imposing it on you, then actually you create more risk in the macro sphere, and that actually makes the whole system much more volatile. And actually, that's very counterproductive.

So the same thing you can do in the stock-and-flow basis on the Keynes consumption-savings decision, you can also do it in portfolio choice decision on the risks base as well. So that's the first, this movement away from, you know, impulse response functions. One-time shock and have an impulse response, much more to overall analysis of the whole volatility dynamics, how the volatility dynamics is playing out.

The second aspect I would like to mention is   which is not in a time dimension; it's much more in the cross-section   is a focus away from the riskiness of a financial institution. How likely is it that the financial institution is going under? But the riskiness of a financial institution being in difficulties causes on the whole system. What are the spillovers? What are the externalities in terms of risk on the system?

And these spillovers come in two flavors. One of the spillovers are direct contractual links. So if we have some contractual connections, there's a whole network structure you can actually figure out how the bankruptcy of one bank causes a bankruptcy for the next bank, and so forth. And this is the first network domino-effect type of spillovers.

And then there are, of course, the indirect ones, which turn out to be way more powerful than the direct ones. That's through some liquidity spirals through common exposure to similar assets, so there will be some   (inaudible)   asset prices going down, and that drags down others as well. And that can, again, come in the level space, where the asset price is going down. It can also come in the volatility space, where the asset price become more volatile, hence the margins are actually going up. And that's why it forces people to de-lever. So the value at risk is going up, people have to de-lever. And this actually feeds on its own, makes the situation even worse.

And what's a fundamental difference between the first one and the second one, the first one you can characterize very nicely, because you know when a bank goes bankrupt, then it will cause some direct spillover effects. The second one depends on the behavioral response of the particular player in the marketplace. So you have to figure out what is the behavioral response on that. Measuring this behavioral response is way more difficult. And then the outcome is a general-equilibrium outcome, which has a lot of feedback effects and amplification going on.

So what one needs essentially is some response indicators. So we have to collect data, not about positions or something, but on something on the funding conditions, combined with the market liquidity of these positions, which indicates will this particular player be a shock absorber or will it be a shock amplifier. So essentially you can classify things. If you have these response indicators, you can then say, oh, this institution, it's more likely to   when there's a negative shock, it will actually contribute to the shock and will amplify the shock. And the other institution is   you know, the price is going down, it sees it as a good buying opportunity, is actually providing liquidity, it will be a shock absorber. So to classify these institutions in different ways.

And you can liken the domino effects. You can build up a network with virtual links, which, because they're not contractually linked directly, they will be virtual links, but you can say some of them are shock amplifiers and some of them are shock absorbers. It depends where in the network they are placed how stable the whole network is at the end of the day.

So that was a second dimension.

The third dimension is, again, the volatility paradox, where essentially if you measure contemporaneous risk with the value at risk or some volatility measure, it can be very misleading. Right now all the volatility measures are very, very down. That actually can be very misleading, because this leads to lower margins, as I mentioned earlier; leads to higher margin debt, higher leverage. And that makes you vulnerable to some more risk down the road.

And then, when there is a shock and the vulnerable are shocked, then actually the value at risk is shooting up, and then you suddenly have this huge adjustment and the whole thing goes into reverse. You have this risk-on, risk-off phenomena playing out. So you have this huge pro-cyclicality. And the paper talks at length how the countercyclical buffer helps to overcome this pro-cyclicality. The old Basel II framework essentially was very dangerous in this regard because, you know, the risk weights were, to some extent, very much related to the value at risk, and having this countercyclical buffer is actually helping on this dimension.

On a more subtle note, you can show in models that actually if you have a financial system which can better ensure idiosyncratic risk, endogenous risk of the system is actually higher. So actually going from a very incomplete market setting to a less incomplete market setting, you make the whole system less stable because, essentially, if you insure people's idiosyncratic risk, they will take on more leverage to bet more along the aggregate-risk dimension, and that makes the whole system more risky.

So these are all complications which we have learned during the last   and the paper touches upon them. That makes calibrating macro models or finding the right macro models challenging, because whether you put this mechanism in or not affects very much your calibration of capital requirements.

So let me just illustrate this buildup that is just a little   craft a buildup of this risk in the background. Oh, two minutes, OK.

So the buildup of the background. So if that's just a best response function, as a function of what the others are doing. And when the risk is building up in the background, nothing happens, you have this equilibrium up there. This is that dot. And it's all   only what happens is actually the best response changes, but you don't see the best response because it's off equilibrium. But now if there is   a shock occurs, then actually you move down dramatically because there was first amplification. And later   I mean, there's also multiplicity in this particular example. We can jump to a much lower equilibrium as well. So the high nonlinearity is showing up.

The fourth dimension I would like to mention is that macropru and monetary policy are highly connected. I think there is a tendency to have separation principles everywhere and have little boxes to put this together. Why is this so highly connected? If you look at emerging economies, it's also the case that you don't want those. Macropru now in the advanced economies is actually part of their monetary policy toolbox in emerging economies. Of course, they are connected also because most of the money is created by the banking sector. So inside money is created by the banking sector. And we know that, you know, in downturns the banking sector shrinks and the banking sector will not take out so much idiosyncratic risk. More of this risk is then held by the households, so money demand goes up. So just replacing the missing inside money with new outside money, that's what the monetary risks would do in order to keep the total money supply constant is actually not enough, because inside money and outside money are not substitute from a social perspective.

And that's something where we have to be very aware of, that these two things are not separate from each other. They interact and, hence, we need coordination, as came up many times before, but in fiscal and monetary. But we also need it between these two authorities or these two branches of stabilization of the macro economy.

And then there's, of course, the whole balance sheet of a central bank is highly interlinked with all bank regulation. At the moment, why are the banks holding so much of the short-term claims? It's, of course, because liquidity regulation makes it   forces them, essentially, to hold these excess reserves from the central bank. So this interacts very strongly with the central bank, and has implications how big the central bank's balance sheet should be in this regard. Let me just say   and then, of course, it is the case that it is   recapitalizing monetary policy is affecting stealth recapitalization. There are more income effects rather than substitution effects playing out.

Let me, finally, say something about volatility and resiliency. I think there are two ways you can measure stability. One is just looking at the volatility, which is just having a concrete wall, essentially, is that I don't want to be there, and it's more about the standard deviation of variance. The other thing is resiliency, where it's more a rubber wall. So there is a   you know, there's a shock, you give in at the time on the shock, but then actually there's stronger mean reversion along this. And I think we should focus more on the resiliency component rather than on purely measuring the volatility. If you have a resiliency aspect to it, we can have sometimes facing a bigger shock, as long as we are coming back. This is actually an easier way to deal with a certain situation.

I'm running out of time, so it's   let me jump over that.

And let me just summarize briefly. Again, I think what happened in the last 10 years, away from an impulse response, stock floor analysis; more to a volatility dynamics risk focus, from risk in isolation for an institution to the spillovers from systemic   to systemic risk measures; away from measuring just contemporaneous risk at this point in time   which is very misleading   towards analyzing hidden risk, which might suddenly appear when the crisis materializes; and then to be aware that it's not so simple just putting things in separate boxes. The separation principles we have to be very careful about because everything in a crisis tends to be interlinked. And finally, I think there should be a focus more on resiliency on top of purely volatility measures.

MR. POSEN: Thank you very much, Markus.

Let me turn now to Benot Coeur of the European Central Bank.

BENOT COEUR: So good evening. It's very difficult to pay justice to all dimensions of Andy's paper because there's even much more to it than what Andy actually said here. So we could spend a whole week assessing it, which is probably not what you want to do tonight. So I will   I will try to be focused. And let me start by saying right first that paper is very timely. It comes at a time where the regulatory community want to take a step side and look back and what has been achieved since Pittsburgh, and review and evaluate. And the FSB [Financial Stability Board] will have this big evaluation project which is starting. So the paper I think is very helpful to put a little bit of an analytical structure on it, which we are not very good at doing. So it's good that you're doing it.

And second, I agree with most of the conclusions. So I will be very focused. I agree that the ever-higher capital requirements haven't led to lower lending. So that lends some credit to the assumption that capital requirements could have been calibrated at a higher level. Notwithstanding the TLAC [total loss-absorbing capacity], which you mentioned, which add a little bit of complexity. Also notwithstanding the fact that it could look different in an environment with higher rates, generally. So the kind of   (inaudible)   distortion that capital requirements introduce are very benign today, but they could look different in an environment where the   (inaudible)   structure would be   would be wider. So that's an open question.

And I also agree with the conclusion that different roles are complementary, and that the   what you call the multipolar regulatory regime, which I would call silo-based, is probably what we need when we know so little about these consequences of the difference   of the different roles. So this I agree.

So let me focus on two issues. First one is about regulatory spillovers and the   precisely this silo-based approach, or the multipolar approach to regulation. And the second one is something which you've not developed too much, which is about financial structures. And I will make the hypothesis that financial structures are to some extent endogenous to the way we do regulation, and that's something that we   we're not very good at looking through.

So the first point is on the tradeoffs which are involved in multipolar regulation. And again, I agree with the main conclusion of the paper that it's probably right to have these regulatory systems being over-constrained when we look banking regulation. But the point is that everything that we've done since Pittsburgh has created a financial system which is much more diverse than it was 10 years ago, both in the way risk is being intermediated   and that's about nonbanks rising and doing risk transformation the same way as banks do. And that's also about the way risk is being processed, pooled, and redistributed. And that's in particular about the rise of CCPs [central counterparties] as engines of risk transformation. And that was not the case 10 years ago.

And when you move to a system which is very much focused on banks, to a system which is   which has a more diverse set of actors, the risk is that regulatory spillovers can be more likely and can be also more difficult to identify. So just to give you an example which is an issue I've been   I've been spending some time on, which is the interaction between bank regulation and CCP regulation. And more practically, the interaction between the delivered ratio and the clearing mandates. ... You have clearing clients depositing margins with CCPs. These margins are accounted for in the leveraged ratio. So the leveraged ratio is non-risk-based, as it should. But the mere fact that it does include the initial margin and the variation margin creates a disincentive to central clearing.

So here you have a tradeoff. And it's not that clear where we're standing   on which side of the tradeoff we're standing. That's something we have to look deeper into. But we have a clear tradeoff which did not exist before the reform started. And that could be many more examples of it. So that's the kind of issues we will have to look in more depth. And by the way, the FSB will do it   will have the review of incentives to central clearing, under the barbaric acronym of DAT, Derivatives Assessment Team. So when you hear about the DAT, that will be about whether the ratio is rightly calibrated or not rightly calibrated. But that's an example.

And I don't think that   it's very difficult for us to address for us, because if you would like to characterize these kind of tradeoffs you will need first to define the loss function between the social value of central clearing and social value of higher capital   higher leveraged ratio, for that matter. How would you define the loss function? How would you put rates on the different objectives? And then you would need a model which is granular enough to have CCPs and banks and the kind of interactions between CCPs and banks. And we don't have it. And we're still a long way from having it. So it's very difficult to put numbers on these kind of discussions.

And one other example, which is pretty obvious is international spillovers. And coming back to CCPs, again, now that CCPs are big, super-systemic infrastructures, we need   we need to be serious about cooperative oversight of CCPs. And we still have some way to go before we have proper   (inaudible)   oversights for large CCPs. So that's also an example of a regulatory spillover which did not exist some years ago, which is the   in a sense, the outcome of what we've, ourselves, created since Pittsburgh. And the European proposal to have a new third-party regime for CCPs is an attempt at dealing with it by providing instruments to properly supervise risks being created outside of the EU. It is controversial, but it is one attempt to deal at this kind of spillover that weren't there some years ago.

My second   my second point is on financial structures. And it's more difficult, in a sense, because I'm not sure that you've properly accounted for the fact that our own supervisory or regulatory decisions, also regulatory stance, can have an impact on financial structures, which can work through either some forms of financing receiving preferential treatment, or by protecting incumbents from competition, which are two ways where this kind of an impact on the financial mix. So in a sense, regulatory decisions or regulatory framework has allocative consequences for the way the financial system works.

And regulators are really caught between a rock and hard place. You can't ignore the effects of regulation on the financial system, because that would be   that would be delusional. It has an impact on the financial system. At the same time, if you want to internalize too much the impact of regulation on the financial system, you run the risk of moving from a prudential mandate to an industrial mandate, which is, I believe, a deadly sin for a regulator. And not everyone will agree, but I think that that create a huge risk of confusion in mandates and instruments.

And for long, the academic research hasn't been very helpful because it has more or less considered the different financial structures were equivalent, from an allocative standpoint, but it's no longer the case. So we now know that moving from   say, from a bond-based system to a market-based system is helpful when you move closer to the technological fronts here. That's what we are trying to achieve in Europe with the Capital Markets Union. So the   also the kind of academic evidence that we have here has changed. And that comes at a time where banks themselves are challenged by technological shifts. And you mentioned fintech, which is an obvious challenge to the   to the future and the profitability of banks.

So the   so one question we have here is how much should regulators care about it? And I see two scenarios for the future. One scenario is one where banks will rise to the challenge. And they will   they will internalize all the technological change. They will join forces with start-ups, et cetera. And that outcome would probably be benign from a regulatory standpoint, because we know how to regulate banks. So it doesn't change much to the landscape. But it   there are crucial assumptions. First, it assumes that banks will be profitable enough to be able to invest in that kind of new activities. So in Europe, that would mean that they have to cut costs and they have to address NPLs [nonperforming loans] in a more forceful way to be able to do it.

And it may create different challenges for regulators. For instance, if we want to let banks reap the benefits of economies of scale, which are inherent to new technologies, while not recreating the risk of "too big to fail." And that's something that is still ahead of us, in a sense. How much do we want to let banks look more like Apple and Google, without recreating "too big to fail"? I don't think we have a proper answer to that.

So the last remark, and I'm done, is that this is not only about regulation. It's also   it's not only a challenge for regulators. It's also a challenge for central banks. And I would argue that to some extent, as much as financial structure are to some extent endogenous to regulation, they are also endogenous to the way we do monetary policy. And Markus made the point minutes ago that the size and composition of central bank balance sheets has an impact on financial intermediation, and I'm quite sure that Jeremy also will make that point. So we have to be mindful about the impact of our own decisions on the future of financial structures.

And there are a number of examples. Negative rates are an example. So, so far we are very comfortable with the conclusion that negative rates haven't harmed financial intermediation, and that the general equilibrium impact of negative rates and the other monetary policy instruments have dominated by far any negative impact on bond profitability. But that could change over time, so that's something we have to   we have to monitor.

Same for QE [quantitative easing]. It's very hard to believe that central banks keeping very large balance sheets for a considerable amount of time will not have an impact on financial intermediation.

So what I'm   the point I'm making here is, is essence, the opposite of the points Jeremy has made, which is that you may want central banks to have large balance sheets, because if you believe that commercial banks are not doing it the right way or that you can   (inaudible)   created by liquidity creation in the commercial banking system are too   are too harmful to society and are not addressed by regulation. I am making the opposite point, that if for monetary policy reasons we keep a very, very large balance sheet for a very long amount of time, you may crowd out financial intermediation in a way that can spill back into monetary policy.

So I am not arguing that central banks should have a different mandate. We have a price stability mandate, so that matters only to the extent that it spills back into monetary policy or that it would have an impact on monetary policy transmission. But the longer it lasts, the more likely this is going to happen.

And I stop here, Adam.

MR. POSEN: Thank you, Benot.

Nellie Liang of the Brookings Institution.

NELLIE LIANG: OK. So thank you very much for inviting me to be here.

I do recommend that you all take a look at   I'm going to go back, OK   to read Andy Haldane's paper. It's actually   it's very nice, it's a very comprehensive review of the state of financial stability policy and research. And it is a very convenient time for a stock take.

I'm not going to   he mentions also multiple areas for further research. And I'm going to sort of take up two of these. And the two are models of how the financial sector interacts and affects the real economy and, second, the governance structures for macroprudential policies, which are actually very important for taking actions.

And you should take my comments today in the spirit of work in progress, next steps in financial stability.

Let's see. So first, on the models, let me just say Andy points out that the Bank of England as early as 2009, where macroprudential policy was sort of still just a word that wasn't well known and repeated, raised the questions of what are the objectives. Should it be the resilience of large institutions and markets, or should it be the credit cycles, trying to smooth credit cycles of households and businesses?

So, for economists, there's arguments for both lenders and borrowers, creditors and debtors, since there are negative externalities associated with each if either of them become stressed. So then you get a bunch of different kinds of tools. You can have capital requirements, like the CCYB [countercyclical capital buffer], or you can have LTVs [loan-to-value ratios] for the lenders. But there's a whole bunch of different tools.

And then there are arguments for structural versus dynamic. And I guess I would make one point on the dynamic policies. There are important reasons to consider dynamic policies and not just think about structural. One, risk-taking appears to be pro-cyclical. Two, regulations appear to be pro-cyclical and regulations can be arbitraged. And in the U.S. in a very market-based economy, this is not an abstract concern.

But I am very sympathetic to implementation. It's not easy. We don't have reaction functions. We have multiple tools, we have multiple targets. You know, we don't even know what the arguments in a reaction function are. So in this vein I'm going to mention a couple of things that are ongoing.

Again, recent developmental-stage work in this, which try to develop measures that express financial stability risks in terms of risks to output or real activity. And I'm going to mention just three exercises.

So this first one is just an illustration. It's a VAR [value at risk], but it's a threshold VAR, so we're going to allow for some nonlinearities. We talked this morning about VARs. And it looks at whether the effects of financial conditions on economic growth vary with macro financial imbalances. In this case, like, private, nonfinancial credit and we use a credit gap here. So you define   and excess credit gap has been used or a credit gap or excess credit, some measures, have been used by many countries in helping them thinking about implementing the CCYB, or the counter-cyclical capital buffer.

So this is a threshold VAR based on U.S. data, 1975 to 2014. And the idea is to see if the effects of financial conditions on the economy vary by whether you've got a high credit gap or a low credit gap. And just think financial conditions here. Don't think too technically if they're sort of indictors of financial stability risks.

And what this shows is, like   and we use red lines and blue lines. And so in red-line periods, that's high credit. So you shock financial conditions and you find that looser financial conditions, a shock to it is loose, is going to boost GDP [gross domestic product] in the near-term quarters and it's going to be a little bit higher in a high-credit period, and then in the blue, which is the low credit.

But then you can see it's, like, it falls, growth declines, you have a recession. It's just basically less sustainable. So this allows sort of for these nonlinear effects. There's some work here that looks at robustness, that this high credit is not approximating for other factors.

A second exercise, which takes a different empirical approach that I'm going to just mention, it was presented in the IMF GFSR [International Monetary Fund Global Financial Stability Report] current Chapter 3. And here they develop a measure of growth at risk as a macro measure of financial stability. And what they're trying to do is look at whether financial sector conditions can predict the probability distribution of future GDP growth. And they're obviously interested in the left tail of the distribution, the downside risks.

And this chart shows the 95th, the top, and the 5th percentile of predicted year-ahead GDP growth based on financial conditions. This is averaged for 21 countries, so some of the variability is lost in the averaging. And this is work that's built on work by Tobias Adrian and co-authors when he was still at the New York Fed.

And the takeaway from this work is that financial conditions can shift the distribution of future growth. And they find that tighter financial conditions, which are based on asset prices, are predictors of greater downside risks in the near term, say, like, a year ahead, which this shows. And in the medium term, the greater downside risks tend to be brought about by, say, higher borrower leverage. So in this sense, there's a little bit of consistency with what Markus mentioned, a volatility paradox, that you can get looser conditions, can reduce your growth volatility in the near term, but maybe increasing them in the medium to long term.

So basically, this measure is, again, trying to put financial stability risks into the same language that's being used to assess other macro risks. And you could imagine countries could develop such a measure over time, gives them a sense of the need for macroprudential policies or, you know, in some imminent, a crisis-management policy.

And a third exercise is basically to continue to extend this and condition it on whether credit is high or low, similar to the first exercise I mentioned, which allows even more for some nonlinearities. And for advanced economies, sort of some preliminary work suggests that these kinds of nonlinearities exist.

So these three examples are basically testing and finding results that are consistent with a framework for financial stability as the ability of a financial system to absorb shocks rather than to amplify shocks, and they allow for greater downside risks and nonlinearities. OK.

My second area I want to pick up on is governance for macroprudential policies. And this is basically asking a question, who makes the decisions about macroprudential policies? And are the governance arrangements effective?

All countries have preexisting microprudential regulatory structures and they have central banks, and neither were really designed to implement macroprudential policies. And those kinds of policies require a great deal of coordination across regulators. They may need focus on time-varying risks. Many of these skills don't cross regulators.

So, in a new paper I've been working on with Rochelle Edge, we thought we would try to collect some information about what these new authorities look like. And we constructed a data set of authorities for 58 countries. And it's based on public official documents, you know, you look at the central bank websites and the financial stability things, reflects information through 2016.

So what we find, and I have two simple bar charts to simplify a few things, 41 of the 58 countries have established financial stability committees or councils. Most of them have been established since 2010. And 15 countries have designated a single entity to be the macroprudential authority and that's generally the central bank.

Now, if you weight by GDP   so the blue line shows what we call the FSCs, the financial stability, the blue bar, and the lighter blue to the right is weighted by GDP, just to give a sense. So you can see that it's the bigger countries that have created the committees. And if you actually think about global activity, this is basically it. The countries that have set up the central banks as the authority don't account for that much. OK.

And we then looked at the membership and the leadership. So most of these committees have three or four members. A central bank is always on it, one or two prudential regulators. The ministry of finance isn't always on, but when it is it's almost always the chair. So the next chart to the right shows that the ministry of finance is, you know, the chair about twice as often as the central bank. That raises issues about implementation and whether there's any present bias, I think is the word I heard this morning.

And by the way, prudential regulators are always on the committees, but they are never the chair, maybe there's one country. So clearly, they're just sort of delineating responsibilities.

Now, judging the effectiveness of these committees is really subjective. I'm just going to use three things which the IMF has set out as being important. Can they coordinate? Are they able to take actions? And are they willing to take actions?

So, on coordination, you know, all the committees work to promote information sharing, they all say that. And they seem to have the right setup for their membership, and we do a little testing in the paper. This seems like a good step forward. There is coordination.

On the ability to act, we looked at whether any of these committees themselves had specific tools. Did they have the authority to set a counter-cyclical capital buffer, implement a stress test, set an LTV? We found that actually only two councils, the U.K. and France, have what we call hard tools that they can direct an action of any of its members or itself. Another nine have comply or explain, which means they can sort of express a view that somebody should do something, but that agency then has a choice to say whether they think they should do it or not. That means most of these committees meet just to share information.

Now, the separate regulators continue to have these authorities to set the policies. And the central bank can provide the sort of time-varying risk analysis. But many regulators themselves do not have financial stability mandates, so there may well be a very big disconnect in many countries over the authority for tools and the responsibility for financial stability.

A willingness to act, I think it's too early to tell and it's the hardest, but I think this point that they don't have tools, they're run by the ministry of finance suggests that there are some challenges ahead.

So I think one takeaway from this is, in the meantime, monetary policy makers or macro policy makers should not assume that there are these new committees in place to offset pro-cyclicality. OK.

And then I guess to conclude, just to repeat, I'm optimistic on models to make progress on defining targets and assessing nonlinearities and linking them to the economics. But we're pretty far away from setting policies. And there's clearly a need to invest in creating accountability for financial stability.

OK, thank you.

MR. STEIN: No, no, no. So let me start. Let me start with the cons, and then I'll come back to the pros. I'll come back to the pros that you raised.

There's two sort of obvious cons, I think. So actually, let me be clear. I'm not arguing in any way against capital and liquidity requirements, because I think those are distinct. I'm arguing a narrower point, which is, in the world of capital, do you want to have two binding constraints, in some sense, on the same item, which is how much equity, OK? And, you know, the concrete thing is a risk-based capital requirement and a leverage ratio which, in the U.S., has been dialed up pretty substantially via the supplementary leverage ratio.

An easy argument I think everybody sort of appreciates is if the supplementary leverage ratio is calibrated so aggressively that it actually binds   look at that; all right, the little bubbles and everything   OK, if the thing is so aggressive that it actually binds, well, then you're left with just an unrisk-weighted ratio, and that has kind of some goofy properties like it's pretty tough on Treasurys and on repo and all that. OK, and there's some evidence that it's been binding, at least in the U.S., and that it's had some effect on treasury markets.

I'm going to sort of set that aside and focus on a sort of subtler   what may be a subtler point, which is, if banks are heterogeneous and have different business models, and in particular different ratios of risk-weighted to not risk-weighted assets   one constraint may bind for one and another constraint may bind for the other.

So, concretely, think of Wells Fargo, sort of a typical bank bank. It's got a relatively high ratio of risk-weighted to total assets. It's more likely to be bound by the traditional risk-based. Think of Goldman Sachs. It has more securities, basically, on its balance sheet, so it's more likely to be bound by the other. So they're now going to be operating essentially in different regimes. It's like operating in different tax regimes. And that's going to create some forces towards migration that basically, for Wells Fargo, they're not bound by the leverage ratio. They don't face any marginal risk charge for, like, treasury securities, so they ought to do, like, more investment banking.

Lo and behold, Wells Fargo is doing more investment banking. OK, Goldman Sachs has got to think, geez, if I only had a bank, this leverage ratio wouldn't be preventing me from doing the stuff I like to do in the repo market. So, lo and behold, they're going to acquire a bank, which they have done.

So, you know, that's the concern. And basically, if you just work through the algebra of it, what it does is it drives everybody essentially to some kind of a universal banking type of model.

So what I've got here   here's just a picture of which constraints bind or are closest to binding for different banks. Don't stare at it too hard. What it's basically saying is the bankier kind of banks, those that are more kind of traditional commercial banks   Wells Fargo, U.S. Bank or PNC   tend to be bound either by a pre- or post-stress version of a risk-based ratio, whereas many of the others, the investment banks and the universals, are more bound by something like the supplementary leverage ratio.

So what you can do   this is a bit of a project, but basically you can take   for each bank, assume it's operating under only its most binding constraint, and then you can think, for each bank in each type of activity, what is going to be the marginal risk charge, given that most binding thing? This is my multiple-tax-regime point.

And you can see that this has changed behavior. So that would be the concern. Now let's take some of the arguments on the other side. So this is a couple of the ones that Andy highlighted. And I just want to be careful on the tone here. The concerns that animate the leverage ratio are centrally important   OK, absolutely important. And it's just a question of whether the right way to deal with those concerns is by having sort of a competing capital standard or by trying to integrate those concerns into essentially a single rule.

OK, so, for example, there's the sort of idea of Knightian uncertainty, which is, you know, who knows what the risk weights are? Surely the standard risk weights are wrong in some sense, and wouldn't it be good to have another sort of alternative just, you know, for robustness?

So here's the analytical proposition, which is tell me whatever your favorite calibration is of these two things, OK. Now, think about a representative bank. That calibration is going to amount to   like, it's going to have a multiplier of 0.8 on this constraint and a multiplier 0.2 on the other constraint, right. It's going to be doing some averaging, OK.

I would prefer to then take a single constraint; that is, the average of those two. That gets you the same thing for the average bank, but it gets rid of the heterogeneity. That's a thing where I have a very hard time sort of thinking about the logic of there being two, because I can always, in some sense, take the virtues. I don't know that that means you want to go to the extreme of having a uniform risk weight, but that's sort of the idea, OK.

MR. STEIN: OK. So the other point   again, enormously important point   and you have these pictures   you know, the risk-based requirements can be gamed. Yeah, they absolutely can be. I mean, that's in some sense Goodhart's law, which is the risk-based stuff was the stuff that   that was the thing that was in force, so that was the thing that was being gamed under the old regime.

I think, at the same time, the Fed could do much more in terms of ex post transparency. They can explain, this is exactly   this is, you know, the way we varied the scenarios. This is the reason for why we varied the scenarios. Importantly, one thing that they don't disclose now is these are the risk weights that were implicit, essentially, in the scenarios, because, you know, if we had this kind of losses on small-business loans, you have a high capital charge, effectively, on small-business loans. They can do some of that. But if you   I think if you capitulate to the desire for perfect ex ante transparency, you've neutered a sort of important potential virtue of the stress test.

MR. POSEN: Does anybody else from our panel   does anybody else from our panel want to comment on stress testing in general or specific? No?

I'll just remind people, we have published a couple of months ago a book by Morris Goldstein on this topic, which I think, in some ways, comes down similar to where Jeremy does.

Next question. Please, Jose. I mean, sorry. You, Lorenzo. Go.

Q: I feel   and I want to be a bit provocative.

Q: Sorry. Lorenzo Bini Smaghi. Now I am a banker. (Laughter.) So it's a kind of   I don't know in the Frisbee thing, even the dog, and if I am the Frisbee. (Laughter.) I never really understood. (Laughter.) But I understood a lot from this session what the incentive of the regulator is.

MR. HALDANE: Thank you.

Lorenzo, you've changed since you became a banker. (Laughter.)

So I think that is a good thought experiment but   and in some ways that is the one we're about to engage on. So I think the priority immediately after the point where the boat was sinking was to fill the leaks in the boat. And in some ways, that is the job we've been doing for the last decade or more. The public   rightly   don't forgive you if you don't fix the known problems. And in some ways that's what we've been doing, I think   to fix   fixing the known problems, market failure by market failure.

And now is the point, having patched the boat, to ask the question, where do we now sit? Is this actually the boat we want? Is it able to furnish the needs of the real economy, and the way we would wish? And I think that is the essay question that is now posed to us   rightly posed to us, because it's the one that society is posing. And I hope it's one that we come up with a decent answer to.

I should say on that front, I think some of the direction of travel has been pretty positive. And Benot mentioned this   so the fact that Europe, including the U.K., is moving in the direction of having a second engine   in other words, a nonbank engine, a market-based finance engine is very much the right direction of travel. That's a different boat than what we had previously. It means the system is more diverse, and therefore, resilient I think than would have been the case in the past. So I think some of that's happened on the fly and some more of it will happen, I think, in the next decade. It's the right essay question.

On credit and capital, I mean, Sebastian (sp) is here and has a paper that speaks to this question, reaches a slightly different conclusion, as would a number of other papers in a similar sort of direction. I wouldn't say there's nothing here, Lorenzo, by way of tightening credit conditions, but it's   you know, from what I've seen, it's no greater than was already figured in the calculus when we arrived at the initial   at the initial numbers.

MR. POSEN: Thank you. Thank you, Andy.

Would anybody else care to come in?

MR. COEUR: No, I can   I can say a short word and just to illustrate   to illustrate one of Lorenzo's point, which is the adverse impact of capital ratios on lending, I mean, if you look at the eurozone back since, say, 2007, 2008, you had a starting in, say, 2011, 2012. You had a consistent and very quite steep increase in CET1 [common equity tier 1] ratios for a large eurozone bonds. And at the same time, you have an acceleration of credit. So the correlation is that way. It's positive.

MR. POSEN: Anyone else?

MS. LIANG: And I would just add the evidence from the U.S. would support what Benot was just saying. We've gotten to a place where higher capital and higher growth have this positive feedback and not a less lending, and it's the strong banks that are resilient to shocks and can continue to lend. So there is a transition, absolutely. But in some sense, Basel tried to address that by a long transition period that works only somewhat since everything gets pulled forward. MR. POSEN: Jeremy.

MR. STEIN: Can I just say one other point on the capital and transition? So, you know, your point is essentially that the flow cost of raising new equity is higher than the steady state cost of having it on the balance sheet in some sense. So that's the sort of time. So that's of course completely right. Those flow costs tend to be private costs more so than social costs. Like, guys don't want to dilute their shareholders.

And the implication of that   so for example, the   you know, the extreme case of this is during the financial crisis, when the capital's really depleted. I don't think that the right answer is that we should have low capital. It's that sometimes you need to focus not just on capital ratios but on dollars of capital. So I thought the central design in sight of the 2009 SCAP [Supervisory Capital Assessment Program] was   when the stress tests were done, we didn't look at Bank of America and go, oh, you know, you don't have a lot of capital, you need to improve your capital ratio by 2 percentage points. We said you need 33 points whatever billion dollars of new capital. So they didn't have the ability to kind of get to the ratio by shrinking assets. You had to push. I mean, policy has to not take these flow costs as just a given constraint, but has to push against them. So I fully agree that they're there. But again, I think they're more   they're more in the vein of private than social costs, so that's something we're   you know, my wish for the stress test next time around when there's, you know, a stress scenario is that policy makers have the resolve   unlike in 2007   to be more aggressive about essentially, you know, cutting off dividends and compelling new equity issues.

Q: (Inaudible)   Bloomberg View.

Now I have two doubts with regard to that proposition. One is   was actually pointed out in your presentation. How credible are these resolution regimes? We just don't know, particularly for large banks.

And I know this is very important also for the Bank of England, because I believe you've taken a decision not to enforce the full systemic risk buffers, partly because of this idea that resolution regimes can   I think John Baker (sp) has pointed out that that was a mistake you made last year. So I just wonder whether, you know, any   not just you but other people on the panel had comments on that.

MR. HALDANE: So the question is the right one, and it's true enough that at this stage we cannot have the case law on whether the shiny new resolution arrangements that are being put in place statutorily throughout the world, and the accompanying nonequity sources of   (inaudible)   debt will indeed be bailed in in the sort of situations of stress that we saw during the crisis. So I have a lot of confidence that if failures are small and singular, that this will be relatively easy to pull off, and U.S. experience over many decades speaks to that being the case.

But the rubber hits the road   and this certainly has resonance in the systemic states of the world that you mention. In those states, I do think that   you know, a lot of talk today about the time-consistency problem as it pertains to monetary policy. I think that is as nothing by comparison to the time-consistency problem as it pertains to financial stability in those systems, as Adam said next to me. I absolutely believe that. I think the problem, both on the way in, given the longer duration of buildup of stress and the scale of the cost when stress hits, means that the knife edge, the   (inaudible)   is that much greater, and therefore the pre-commitment problem is that much greater.

And if you look at the history of bail-in debt, bail-in debt's got a history of at least 150 years, and bail-in has tended to be honored in the breach. It hasn't happened for these   for just these reasons. Because if you were the finance minister or treasury secretary of the day faced up against, you know, do I write the check or do I punish the widows and orphans, it's easy which   it's easy to know which choice to make.

Will next time be different? I hope so. And lots of thought, much more thought than ever previously over this 150 years has gone into the design of instruments and design of a regime that can make it materially more time consistent than any in the past. But ultimately   ultimately   this is in part an act of faith, and only time will tell.

MR. POSEN: Let me say, since Larry set the model that one doesn't have to be a neutral chair, one can editorialize a bit, I'm nodding vociferously not because I'm a hundred percent in agreement with Andy or because I fully have his sophisticated views, but two weeks ago at his conference I was pushing the idea that the time and consistency moral hazard problem for policy makers and regulators is far worse on the financial side than monetary side. And we've spent so much broken crockery through the years, including recessions and hard money constraints, to prove we're tough on time and consistency, and then we wring our hands about proving we're tough and perhaps taking away some of the discretion from policymakers and say, oh, my god, it's a terrible tradeoff on the banking side. And so just the spirit of Andy's comments, I at least think I'm very much in favor of.

MR. COEUR: Just one short remark exactly   I mean, to that discussion on time and consistency. I mean, there is a   there is always a tradeoff between ex ante incentives and ex post incentives. But if it's only between ex ante incentives and ex post incentive, it's fine. But what we've seen is regulators being soft ex ante. So you   at least you want it to be a tradeoff.

MR. POSEN: Yes. (Laughter.)

MR. COEUR: You want to see the tradeoff. And so if you're too   if you're too nice ex ante, it's not even a tradeoff.

WSJ Pro: Market Talk: Daily Analysis Roundup

Commodity Traders Field Questions On Bitcoin

Attention around bitcoin and cryptocurrencies is leading some to seek advice from traditional commodity traders. Mark Waggoner, president of Excel Futures, says clients as well as friends have been calling or reaching out on Facebook, asking about bitcoin. "A lot of my friends know that I'm in commodities," he says, leading them to question him on whether he trades bitcoin or if they should invest. The uptick in inquiries began about two months ago. Bob Haberkorn, at RJO Futures, says, "We've got a lot of people who normally want to trade gold, and we're getting calls from people who want to do cryptos, and we don't do those." Bitcoin traded at a record high Friday, at one point exceeding $5,800 according to Coindesk. (stephanie.yang@wsj.com)

Basel Chief Optimistic About Global Pact on Capital

William Coen, secretary general of the Basel Committee group of global bank regulators, says the group is close to an accord on modifications to capital rules. The outlines of the agreement are "fairly clear," he said, including on a sticking point between U.S. and European regulators regarding the "floor" that will be set on how banks' internal economic models reduce their capital requirement. Following this agreement's public announcement, he said, "there is likely to be a period during which no major policy initiatives" will be undertaken by the group. (ryan.tracy@wsj.com; @ryanjtracy)

Catastrophes Likely to be Reinsurance Capital Event

After falling for years, reinsurance prices could stay flat or rise up to 5% during the Jan. 1 policy renewal season, says S&P Global Ratings. The global reinsurance industry could face more than $100 billion in losses from catastrophes in 3Q including hurricanes Harvey, Maria and Irma and two earthquakes in Mexico, "which will likely wipe out the industry's annual earnings and ultimately become a capital event for the global reinsurance sector," S&P says. The hurricane season is still not over, and wildfires currently burning in California are likely to add to the annual total. (nicole.friedman@wsj.com; @NicoleFriedman)

California Wildfires Could Cost $3B-$6B

The California wildfires could cause an economic and insured loss of $3 billion to $6 billion, catastrophe-modeling firm RMS says in a blog post. The total value of 15,000 structures within the perimeters of the fires is $13.5 billion, RMS says. Insurance penetration is high in the area, so the insured losses and economic losses should be similar, the firm says. The estimate excludes auto losses, crop losses and smoke damage. The fires are still burning, so the estimate could change. (nicole.friedman@wsj.com; @NicoleFriedman)

Bank of America Enjoys Fast Growth in Zelle Offering

Bank of America processed $4 billion in mobile money transfers over the Zelle network during the third quarter, CEO Brian Moynihan says on a conference call with analysts. BofA and many of the largest US banks launched the Zelle network over the summer as a way to compete with PayPal's popular Venmo service and cut down on processing costs. "We have $5 billion that we spend a year on cash currency, checks moving around our company in the system," Moynihan said on the call. "The way we're going to get there is by coming digitizing those and eliminating cash." (peter.rudegeair@wsj.com; @rudegeair)

Wells Fargo Changes Living Will Strategy

Wells Fargo CFO John Shrewsberry said the bank plans to move to a so-called single point of entry strategy for its next living will submission instead of a multiple point of entry strategy. Shrewsberry said that strategy will "enhance the flexibility of strategic options available to resolve the firm" but said the change isn't related to feedback from its 2017 submission. WSJ reported in December 2016 that the bank was likely to move to a single point of entry strategy used by most other big banks that call for the bankruptcy of a firm's holding company, while individual units are kept running or sold piecemeal. FDIC and Fed in April said Wells Fargo passed its living will for 2015. The regulators had failed the bank early last year and dinged it a second time in December 2016. (emily.glazer@wsj.com; @EmilyGlazerWSJ)

Regling Says Discussions on SRF Backstop  Quite Advanced'

The head of the eurozone's bailout fund, Klaus Regling, says discussions on the creation a common backstop to the eurozone's Single Resolution Fund are "quite advanced" and the European Stability Mechanism will likely take on the task. Another mechanism aimed at pooling banking risks, a common deposit-insurance scheme, "will be useful and will happen," he says on a panel in Washington. Risk sharing remains an issue in the push to make the eurozone more resilient, Regling says, although he's not in favor of full fiscal or political union, nor does he think there should be additional transfers between countries to promote convergence. (harriet.torry@wsj.com; @HarrietTorry)

PNC Blames Hurricanes For Higher Loss Provisions

PNC set aside $130 million for credit losses in 3Q, up 49% from $87 million a year ago. That was more than what the bank had predicted in July, when it said it expected the provision to be between $75 million to $125 million. The bank said that was partly related to delinquencies caused by Hurricanes Harvey and Irma. PNC also said it could raise the provision again in 4Q. (christina.rexrode@wsj.com; @chris_rexrode)

Wells Fargo Community Banking Unit Takes Big Charge

Profits at Wells Fargo's community banking unit were hit by a $1 billion litigation charge related to crisis-era, mortgage-related investigations. Overall profits at Wells Fargo's community banking division were $2.23 billion, a 31% slump from the $3.23 billion it earned in 3Q 2016. Revenue in the unit fell to $12.06 billion from $12.39 billion in the year-earlier period. Besides the expected mortgage-related settlement, that unit also includes the business responsible for the questionable sales tactics over at least the past several years and the bank's more recent auto insurance and mortgage problems. (emily.glazer@wsj.com; @EmilyGlazerWSJ)

Bad-Loan Reserves Mixed At Big Banks

Bank of America says it released $66 million of bad-loan reserves in 3Q, as continued improvements in its consumer real estate and energy-loan exposure were offset by credit-card seasoning and loan growth. Wells Fargo had neither a reserve release nor a reserve build in the quarter; the bank says strong credit quality in its consumer real estate and commercial loan portfolios was offset by $450 million of reserve coverage for potential losses from the recent hurricanes. The other two national banks, J.P. Morgan and Citigroup, both indicated Thursday they built their reserves during the quarter; together, the four banks had a total net reserve build of $498 million in 3Q, compared with a reserve release of $355 million in the second quarter and a reserve build of $194 million in last year's third quarter. (michael.rapoport@wsj.com; @rapoportwsj)

Data Breaches Abound

The number of U.S. data breaches jumped to a record 791 in the first six months of 2017, according to the nonprofit Identity Theft Resource Center and data security firm CyberScout. That is a 29% jump from a year earlier. At the same time, U.S. CEOs surveyed by KPMG LLP this year on average "https://www.wsj.com/articles/cybersecurity-tops-priority-list-for-ceos-after-string-of-high-profile-hacks-1507821018" over the next three years, up from its second-place spot in last year's survey.

Regulators also should exempt guarantees provided by top-rated governments from capital tests or classify those guarantees as "high-quality liquid assets," they write.

Pro Private Markets
Computing, Life Sciences Spur Funding Boom; Auris Surgical Robotics, 23andMe among top deals of the year

By Heather Mack 
610 words
16 October 2017
12:30
WSJ Pro Venture Capital
RSTPROVC
English
Copyright  2017, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

High-risk sectors like biopharmaceuticals and tech-enabled health-care delivery services are playing a pivotal role driving health-care fundraising.

Venture investor attention increasingly is focused on startups that combine deep expertise in life sciences with the advanced computational powers of artificial intelligence and machine learning.

That combination of crossover appeal helps explain why the likes of Data Collective, Andreessen Horowitz and Menlo Ventures have made recent moves recently showing their zeal for startups active at the intersection of biology and computer science.

Venture-backed U.S. health-care startups raised nearly $4.1 billion in the third quarter, up 7% from the $3.8 billion raised a year ago, according to Dow Jones VentureSource. Investment in the sector this year is on track to eclipse last year's total of $13.3 billion, with $13 billion already raised this year to date.

That funding pace comes amid an increased overlap in computer science and biology that has enabled earlier-stage companies to receive venture funding.

"It's an incredible new era of drug discovery, not just in actual drugs but in the therapies and the technology to deliver them," said ARCH Venture Partners Managing Director Kristina Burow. "There are these really difficult situations that we're able to get new insights on because of computer aided technology and a deep understanding of genomics."

Deal flow is also up, with 687 financing rounds done year to date, vs. 641 at this point last year.

Across all investment sectors in the U.S., two of the third quarter's biggest venture deals were in health-care companies. Auris Surgical Robotics Inc. raised $280 million in late-stage financing to continue developing robotics-assisted tools for surgeons, and 23andMe Inc. got a $250 million round led by Sequoia Capital as the consumer-genomics startup turned its attention to drug development and drew a valuation of $1.7 billion.

23andMe's genetic sequencing and analysis coupled with life science discovery opportunities may best exemplify the crossover appeal spurring investments. But smaller, newer startups merging biology with advanced computing are also renewing interest in firms more traditionally rooted in technology.

Data Collective, which has been wading deeper into life sciences in the past couple of years, just led an oversubscribed "https://www.wsj.com/articles/recursion-pharma-gets-second-round-this-year-1507030200"in Utah startup Recursion Pharmaceuticals, which uses artificial intelligence to spot new drug targets. Andreessen Horowitz, which recently hired Jorge Conde as another general partner to its two-year-old bio fund, led "https://www.wsj.com/articles/mariss-section-32-anne-wojcicki-back-freenomes-quest-to-detect-cancer-earlier-1503660622" round in biotech startup Freenome Inc. Menlo Ventures"https://www.wsj.com/articles/for-menlo-ventures-its-back-to-biotech-1507670482"Greg Yap to lead its push into the intersection of life sciences and technology.

Seed funding, while still the smallest in regards to the sheer number of rounds, is also having something of a moment, particularly for biopharmaceuticals and health-care services. This early stage funding accounted for $60 million worth of investment across all health-care categories vs. just a few million dollars a year ago.

Beyond the advances in life science discovery, previously overlooked areas like home health care and lab services are getting more funding. Investment in health-care services rose year-over-year, with a 29% increase in the number of deals and a 26% jump in dollars invested.

"Services are making a comeback," said Lisa Suennen, senior managing director of health-care investing at GE Ventures. "They are coming more en vogue again, thank God, because we know that actually getting the care to people is what matters."

Pentagon Pits Traders vs. Hackers

By Alexander Osipovich 
908 words
16 October 2017
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Dozens of high-speed traders and others from Wall Street are helping the Pentagon study how hackers could unleash chaos in the U.S. financial system.

The Department of Defense's research arm over the past year and a half has consulted executives at high-frequency trading firms and quantitative hedge funds, and people from exchanges and other financial companies, participants in the discussions said. Officials described the effort as an early-stage pilot project aimed at identifying market vulnerabilities.

The Defense Advanced Research Projects Agency, or Darpa, began the initiative before the revelations of attacks on Equifax Inc. and the Securities and Exchange Commission brought public scrutiny of risks to U.S. market infrastructure.

Participants described meetings as informal sessions in which attendees brainstorm about how hackers might try to bring down U.S. markets, then rank the ideas by feasibility.

Among the potential scenarios: Hackers could cripple a widely used payroll system; they could inject false information into stock-data feeds, sending trading algorithms out of whack; or they could flood the stock market with fake sell orders and trigger a market crash.

High-speed traders and quant-fund managers, who use sophisticated computer programs to buy and sell stocks, sometimes in fractions of a second, form the core of the group. Such traders tend to have deep expertise in the inner workings of financial markets and the automated systems that account for huge swaths of trading activity today.

Darpa officials confirmed the effort, which is unclassified but hasn't been previously reported. The Wall Street Journal spoke with several traders who participated in the initiative, called the Financial Markets Vulnerabilities Project.

"We started thinking a couple years ago what it would be like if a malicious actor wanted to cause havoc on our financial markets," said Wade Shen, who researched artificial intelligence at the Massachusetts Institute of Technology before joining Darpa as a program manager in 2014.

Darpa famously developed the technology behind the internet and stealth bombers. It recruits experts from academia and industry in a bid to keep its thinking fresh. The agency declined to release a list of its financial-industry advisers for the project.

There have been three meetings for the project since last year, with another one expected later this year, an agency spokeswoman said. The last meeting took place in a conference room at New York University this spring over pizza, participants said.

High-frequency traders have been vilified by critics such as author Michael Lewis, who accused them in his book "Flash Boys" of exploiting slower-moving investors by "front-running" their orders, though these traders deny doing this and say it isn't even possible.

Some of the biggest names in the high-speed trading business have advised Darpa. They include Jamil Nazarali, senior adviser to the chief executive officer of Citadel Securities, a trading giant responsible for around 20% of daily volume in U.S. stock markets. Misha Malyshev, CEO of trading firm Teza Technologies, has "advised Darpa on vulnerabilities within the U.S. financial markets," Teza's website said. Manoj Narang, CEO of quant hedge fund Mana Partners, said he had advised Darpa, too.

Among potential targets that participants have worried could appeal to hackers given their broad reach are credit-card companies, payment processors and payroll companies such as Automatic Data Processing, Inc., or ADP, which handles the paychecks for one in six U.S. workers, participants said.

An ADP spokesman said, "We work diligently to protect our clients' data, which includes efforts to prevent, detect and respond to attempted cybersecurity incidents."

The Mana Partners CEO said he began taking part in the Darpa meetings as a skeptic, thinking the U.S. stock market was resilient and it was unlikely for attackers to cause anything more than temporary damage. But since then, he has gotten more worried.

One scenario he fears: a hack of a U.S. exchange in which the attacker sends a wave of fake sell orders to every firm offering to buy shares. That could potentially erase hundreds of billions of dollars of market value as prices drop and firms try to cover losses by selling on other exchanges, Mr. Narang said.

Project participants have also debated the impact of attackers transmitting false data via the electronic feeds that traders use to monitor stock prices, or publishing "fake news" to shake investor confidence.

The goal of Darpa's project is to develop a simulation of U.S. markets, which could be used to test scenarios, Mr. Shen said.

Such software would need to model complex, interrelated markets -- not just stocks but also markets such as futures -- as well as the behavior of automated trading systems operating within them.

Many quantitative trading firms already do something similar, Mr. Narang said. His company won a small contract from Darpa this year to test whether its simulation tool could be used by the agency, according to Mr. Narang and a Darpa spokeswoman.

The project isn't the first time the Pentagon has studied such risks.

In 2009, military experts took part in a two-day war game exploring a "global financial war" involving China and Russia, according to "Currency Wars: The Making of the Next Global Crisis," a 2011 book by James Rickards. The Applied Physics Laboratory at Johns Hopkins University hosted the event, a spokesman there confirmed.

Technology
Equifax Runs Into A Fresh Problem

By Robert McMillan and Annamaria Andriotis 
557 words
16 October 2017
The Wall Street Journal
J
B4
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

The security issue that caused visitors to Equifax Inc.'s website this week to encounter malicious software happened because the site was attempting to use a discontinued web product, security researchers said Friday.

The story behind Equifax's latest web problems is a digital whodunit illustrating the complex nature of the credit-reporting company's online operations and the struggles it has had in securing its systems despite several data breaches in the past year.

On Thursday, security researchers at security vendor Malwarebytes Corp. identified a small piece of code on Equifax's website that connected users to the Netflame.cc internet domain, which was the source of the malicious software. That domain once was used by Digital River Inc., an e-commerce and digital-marketing vendor, for a now-defunct web-analytics product called Fireclick, Digital River said.

But the domain's registration was released in October 2016, three months after Digital River ended support for Fireclick as part of an "ongoing domain cleanup," said Christopher Rence, the company's chief information and risk officer.

That domain was registered Nov. 15 to an individual with an address listed southeast of Bangkok, according to internet records.

On Thursday, the Netflame.cc domain was serving up a variety of malicious software, including fraudulent online surveys, adware and software designed to steal online-banking credentials, said Jerome Segura, an analyst with Malwarebytes. It is common for scammers to be on the lookout for soon-to-expire web domains so they can snatch them up and use them to deliver malicious software, he said.

Mr. Segura also discovered the Central America site of TransUnion, an Equifax competitor, connecting to the Netflame.cc domain, he said.

Equifax didn't respond to a message seeking comment Friday. On Thursday, the company blamed its security issue on a third-party analytics website, without naming Digital River.

Neither Equifax nor TransUnion have been Digital River customers directly, but Central Source LLC, a joint venture set up by the credit-reporting agencies to run annualcreditreport.com, had a contract to use Fireclick that expired in May 2014, Digital River said. Annualcreditreport.com is a site where people can check their credit reports with each of the three major firms free once a year.

Central Source couldn't be reached for comment.

That joint venture is likely the reason Equifax and TransUnion were still trying to connect to the defunct Fireclick service, Mr. Segura said. "It's the responsibility of whoever owns the website to ensure that all scripts are relevant," he said.

Equifax has blamed failures in its scanning and patch-management systems for a May 2017 data breach that compromised the personal information of 145.5 million consumers. The security issue reported this week was likely due to a similar type of failure.

Digital River said Fireclick's last clients stopped using the service in late 2015, so there were no clients to notify when it took the Fireclick service down and released the domain.

In a statement Friday, TransUnion said it was aware its Central America site redirected users to download malware. After fixing the issue, TransUnion scanned its other sites and found no unauthorized access to its systems, the company said.

FRONT PAGE - COMPANIES & MARKETS
US lenders report patchy results;  " Shares in JPMorgan and Citi dip  " 'Trump bump' loses steam  " Weak bond trading bites

By Alistair Gray and Ben McLannahan in New York 
456 words
13 October 2017
Financial Times
FTFT
USA Ed1
11
English
Copyright 2017 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Two of Wall Street's largest banks yesterday presented mixed third-quarter results, the latest sign that the election of Donald Trump as US president has yet to produce the expected boost to big finance that has driven a historic share price rally in the sector.

JPMorgan Chase and Citigroup each set aside higher reserves for consumer-loan losses and posted weaker revenues from their all-important bond-trading divisions.

The earnings from both banks were stronger than analysts had forecast, but expectations had been lowered after top executives warned of trading woes in September. Financial markets were unimpressed, sending shares in Citigroup down as much as 2.4 per cent and JPMorgan as much as 1.2 per cent.

The two stocks have been among the biggest winners of the "Trump bump" in US equities as investors have piled into the banking sector, hoping that a combination of lower taxes and lighter regulation would boost earnings. Shares in Citi and JPMorgan are up 50 per cent and 41 per cent, respectively, over the past year.

Yet executives caution that many recent developments in Washington have been a hindrance, not a help. Policy gridlock is making corporate America more reluctant to borrow, putting a lid on banks' revenues from lending.

"We all would anticipate greater loan growth if there was a little more clarity", said John Gerspach , Citigroup's chief financial officer, pointing to uncertainty over tax policy. He added, though, that Citi had seen "good pockets of loan growth around the world".

Yet the results also highlighted several areas of resilience. JPMorgan delivered a 7 per cent rise in net profits, as the Fed's rises in base rates supported lending profit margins. Improved car lease volume and credit card loan balances also supported revenues at its consumer and small business banking unit.

Citi eked out an 8 per cent gain in net income to $4.13bn, although the figures were flattered by the sale of its fixed income analytics unit to the London Stock Exchange, which led to a $355m pre-tax gain. Conditions have been patchy, meaning institutional clients sat on the sidelines, pushing bond-trading revenues down 27 per cent at JPMorgan and 16 per cent at Citi.

The result also showed that banks are braced for more consumer loans to sour after a long period of benign lending conditions. Citi booked $252m worth of credit losses. JPMorgan's credit costs, meanwhile, were about $200m higher than last year because of higher charge-offs at its card business.
