Pro Private Markets
Titanic Cash Pile Sinking Private-Equity Returns

By Jessica Davies 
1,202 words
4 October 2017
16:38
WSJ Pro Private Equity
RSTPROPE
English
Copyright  2017, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Mounting unspent cash is eating into private-equity returns.

The appetite to invest in private equity has been thriving in recent years, causing its coffers to overflow, driving record deal values and extending the length of time funds take to invest.

By the end of 2016, the global private-equity industry had amassed a record $1.5 trillion of unspent capital, known in the business as dry powder, according to consultancy Bain & Co.

Strong appetite for the asset class has been fuelled by investors' appetite for yield amid low interest rates. Private-equity firms raised a record $324 billion in the first half of 2017, according to Dow Jones & Co. research database LP Source. Rising dividends to investors and a growing market for secondhand fund stakes have perpetuated the cycle of money available to the asset class.

But excess capital hurts private equity in three ways: high asset prices dent exit multiples; and, unspent cash drags on fund returns by extending hold periods and inflating fees, which offset investor returns.

Two traditional measures for private equity's performance return multiples and funds' internal rates of return have been falling in line with rising asset prices, slow macroeconomic growth and mounting dry powder.

"Lots of dry powder is bad for returns &It compresses returns from a multiple perspective," said Associate Professor Oliver Gottschalg, who teaches at business school HEC Paris and is head of research at private-equity fund analytics provider Peracs LLC.

Average global buyout return multiples based on a rolling three-year average fell to 1.17 times in 2012, down from 1.21 times in 2011, 1.20 times in 2010 and 1.28 times in 2008 and 2009, well below the heady heights of up to 1.5 times in the early 2000s, according to Peracs. These are the latest fund multiples cited in Peracs' 2017 report because of the three-year lag in buyout returns known as the J-curve, which reflects the time it usually takes to generate a profit from selling an asset.

Funds' returns on exit have been directly knocked by rising asset prices, which rose to a record 10.9 times earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization in late 2016, according to consultancy Bain & Co.

"Superabundant capital has diluted returns across the asset classes, not just private equity," Bain & Co. said in its Global Private Equity Report 2017. "Everyone is chasing yield, and where investors spot a sliver of extra yield, they pile in and bid down returns."

As asset prices rise, private-equity firms must work harder to generate a return. As deal values are now at a record high, they must sell at even higher prices to generate an upside.

Cash and Fee Drag

Overall fund performance is also suffering from private-equity's excess cash reserves.

Global private-equity IRRs dropped to 10.54% to 10.56% for 2014 vintages, from 10.7% in 2013 and 13% in 2012, according to the latest estimates from data provider Pitchbook Data Inc.

Fund performance benefits when investments are sold quicker, and firms that take longer to spend their money and divest assets can find their returns suffer. This is because fund returns are time-dependent and calculated from the point of investment, serving as an indication of the profit a fund has made through sales over a set duration.

"All else being equal, for the same deal a longer holding period will mathematically decrease returns (both fund multiples and IRRs)," Professor Gottschalg said.

He explained that "if holding periods get longer, IRR decreased because to reach the same outcome it takes longer so the IRRs get lower."

Recent figures show that firms are taking longer to make money from their investments. Pitchbook estimates that median hold periods for private-equity funds rose to 5.75 years in 2014, up from 5.49 years in 2013 and 3.29 years in 2009, and were 4.99 years as of 2017.

Funds that take longer to invest also have lower returns due to the effect of fee drag, according to Professor Gottshalg.

Investors pay fees on uninvested capital and the more uninvested capital remains in a fund, the longer those fees will be paid, which reduces fund IRRs.

Christopher Braunack, an investment fund partner at law firm Kirkland & Ellis LLP, said more firms are lengthening their investment period or reusing and recycling capital so they can plough profits straight back into new acquisitions as they struggle to spend their growing war chests.

The length of time private-equity firms take to invest their money has increased in recent years as firms have found it harder to put money to work amid high prices and competition for assets.

It now takes longer to invest a private-equity fund than to invest any other type of private-capital fund, such as vehicles focused on debt, venture capital, growth, real estate or natural resources, according to data provider Preqin's Private Capital Fund Terms Advisor study for 2016.

The data provider said the mean investment period for global private-equity buyout funds rose to 5.6 years in 2015, up from 4.9 years in 2014, but settled back to 5.4 years in 2016. It only began compiling these figures in 2014.

Slow growth, high prices and slow spending have depressed returns, according to Mr. Braunack.

"Gross returns are affected by a slow macroeconomic environment and people paying too much on going in," he said.

Chasing Alpha

Yet despite the trend of declining returns, Professor Gottschalg said that in relation to the public markets, payback from private-equity investments is increasing. Peracs' Alpha metric measures the asset class's outperformance relative to the public markets and shows an increasing trend since 2000.

"There's a downward trend if you look at absolute returns but if you look at alpha the trend is improving," he said.

Distributions to investors rose to record highs in 2016 because firms took advantage of high prices to sell and refinance assets and return capital to investors, according to Lloyds Banking Group's head of financial sponsors Stephen Quinn.

He said unspent capital is rising as firms are cautious about spending money in today's frothy market: "A large amount of dry powder is simply about caution. It's caution and discipline."

With fundraising and unspent capital showing no sign of abating, the full extent of dry powder's drag on private equity has yet to be seen.

"The aggregate dry powder hasn't really worked its way through the system," said Mr. Braunack.

But what is clear is that returns are expected to decline further as vast swathes of capital chase fewer deals, perpetuating the cycle of higher asset prices.

"We do expect absolute returns to private equity will continue to decrease in the long term," said Pitchbook analyst Dylan Cox. "We see no decrease in acquisition multiples in sight. Dry powder continues to be at all-time highs. That, combined with competition from strategic acquirers, means prices continue to move higher. Higher acquisition prices generate lower returns."

Write to Jessica Davies at "mailto:jessica.davies@wsj.com"

Fintech group Nex warns on profits at post-trade arm after revamp costs rise

By Philip Stafford 
374 words
3 October 2017
Financial Times
FTFT
London Ed1
15
English
Copyright 2017 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Nex Group, the financial technology business headed by Michael Spencer , has warned on first-half profitability in a key subsidiary after spending more than it expected reshaping the unit.

The group said yesterday that extra spending on temporary staff and blockchain technology would cut margins by nearly a third at its post-trade business, which processes millions of deals for off-exchange markets.

It also spent more than previously disclosed on sales and marketing in readiness for the EU's Mifid II markets rules that come into effect next year.

Success in its post-trade business, named "Optimisation", is one of the big bets for Mr Spencer since he sold the global broking business of ICAP to create Nex at the end of last year. The Optimisation unit accounts for just under half of group profits.

Nex said that first-half operating margins at the unit would fall to 20 per cent. This compares with 29 per cent in its full-year results and 36 per cent for the year to March 2016. Nex said margins would "normalise" in the second half.

Elliott Broadbent, an analyst at RBC Capital Markets, said he expected a cost-savings programme would boost earnings but "we do not expect that they will make up the shortfall. This is a profit warning, plain and simple."

Nex is counting on a root-and-branch reorganisation of Optimisation to boost profitability. To aid growth it bought two small data and analytics companies.

It also started a project to put more customer data - which belong to banks, hedge funds and asset managers - on to a blockchain database, with the aim of cutting costs. This project reduced margins by four percentage points in the first half, Nex said.

"These investments will enhance the potential for Nex Optimisation," said Mr Spencer. He committed to a long-term operating profit target for the unit of at least 40 per cent by 2020.

Group revenue for the six months to September 30 increased by more than 7 per cent on a constant currency basis. The shares fell 5.6 per cent to close at 625p.

Pro Private Markets
Recursion Pharma Gets Second Round This Year; $60 million Series B backs quest to find new drug targets

By Heather Mack 
490 words
3 October 2017
12:30
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Drugs aren't always designed for the purpose they end up being used for. Pfizer's Viagra, for example, was originally developed for cardiovascular conditions but gained popularity for an altogether different use.

Pharmaceutical companies can gain these insights much earlier if they have access to a blend of robotic microscopes and image-processing software that can spot potential new drug targets in the lab.

That promise has attracted investors to Recursion Pharmaceuticals Inc., which is developing a machine learning platform that may be able to do just that. It is testing hundreds of drug compounds, looking for new targets that could expand their use and profitability.

Data Collective led an oversubscribed $60 million Series B round in the Salt Lake City-based startup, which only eight months ago closed its Series A round. Its funding to date now totals $80 million.

To test a drug's untapped potential, Recursion has set up a fleet of automated microscopes and machine learning software to rapidly assess thousands of biological characteristics of different cells including size, shape and the position of its inner workings.

Over the past four years, the company has amassed more than 2 million images to train neural networks to identify which existing drug combinations could be used to target cells in new ways. From there, Recursion run tens of thousands of automated experiments weekly to test those hypotheses.

So far, the company has generated more than 30 discovery programs on more than two dozen diseases and expects to begin clinical trials next year. It has partnerships with four pharmaceutical companies including Sanofi-Genzyme.

"They have this massive data set and tool that we have only watched get better and better since we've known them," said Data Collective Co-Managing Partner Zachary Bogue, who will join the board.

Recursion is also looking to develop drugs on its own.

"As we do these experiments and identify potential new treatments, we are also generating data that can be mined for other uses," said Recursion Chief Executive and co-founder Christopher Gibson.

Investors are paying attention to other software startups using artificial intelligence to accelerate drug discovery. Khosla Ventures just backed Toronto-based Deep Genomics Inc.'s quest to develop new drugs for genetic diseases using AI, and Builders VC led a $10 million Series A in San Francisco's Notable Labs Inc., which is running drug combination tests on tumors with a proprietary platform.

Recursion's investors are betting the Utah startup could be a leader in the AI-powered therapeutics arena. Lux Capital Principal Zavain Dar, who is a Recursion board member, said, "In an era of Google-meets-Genentech, it is not unreasonable to believe that they could be the next big biotech company."

Business
Finance Chiefs Centralize to Cut Costs, Make Smarter Decisions; To better negotiate prices, Pernod Ricard decided to buy casks for its whiskeys centrally

By Tatyana Shumsky and Nina Trentmann 
725 words
3 October 2017
10:00
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

The parent company of Jameson, Chivas Regal and Glenlivet used to have each brand purchase their own casks for aging, storing and shipping. That turned out to be an expensive exercise for the drinks company.

To better negotiate prices for casks, Pernod Ricard SA in 2015 decided to combine its purchases from a central base in Kentucky, even though the whiskeys are made in Canada, Scotland and Ireland.

The move is just one example of how centralizing costs helped the company shave off 2% to 3% of expenses tied to indirect procurement, said finance chief Gilles Bogaert.

Other companies, including PayPal Inc. and Royal Bank of Canada are doing the same, concentrating certain business functions, such as finance, legal or human resources, in central hubs. Nearly 80% of companies that centralize business services include the finance group in that bundle, according to a study by consulting firm the Hackett Group Inc.

For many companies the move to centralize key business services follows years of cutting costs. Nearly a decade of lackluster consumer spending and weaker growth in the wake of the 2008 financial crisis has forced chief financial officers to double down on squeezing expenses to drive up profits.

"We want the center, the HQ, to have a stronger role in making that decision to make sure that at the end of the day, the group allocates funds to the best opportunities," said Pernod's Mr. Bogaert.

Consolidating control over operations or creating a specialized group to execute specific functions can help companies make smarter decisions and become more efficient, according to the Hackett study. And the cost savings can be substantial. Centralizing a specific business function can enhance productivity and save 6% to 10% a year, the study found.

Many companies also opt to place these hubs offshore in lower-cost countries, which can save 20% to 35%, the Hackett study said, adding that it was also a way to be more flexible and agile, particularly when acquiring new businesses.

"A good global business services unit adds a lot more than just cost savings," said Penny Weller, who leads Hackett's finance shared-services advisory program.

PayPal, which operates in more than 200 markets across the world, is centralizing certain aspects of its finance function, said finance chief John Rainey. The online payments processor has a European headquarters in Luxembourg and an international headquarters Singapore.

The challenge in centralizing finance services is to supply leaders of those regions with the resources they require while meeting the need for "a single source of truth with data and not having a duplication of functions so we realize cost savings," Mr. Rainey said.

But other finance chiefs, including Christopher Kastner of Huntington Ingalls Industries Inc., say their companies are better off without centralizing their business services. The U.S. ship builder organized itself into three reporting units, each with their own sector president and finance chief, when it spun off from Northrop Grumman Corp. in 2011.

"Our philosophy is to have clear accountability to each of those sector presidents for their profit and loss, and that includes programmatic and financial performance," Mr. Kastner said of the divisional CFOs.

The company continues to seek opportunities to cut costs or implement new tools, such as data analytics, to improve efficiency, Mr. Kastner said.

Royal Bank of Canada has been on the path to centralizing its business services for about five years, said finance chief Rod Bolger. The company has consolidated shared finance services that serve the whole bank as one team, such as accounts payable or fixed-asset accounting, he said.

Still, Mr. Bolger is cautious when moving finance talent away from operations, which can reduce the value those employees could bring to the business.

"It's not all positive," he said. "Certainly you get efficiencies, but you also lose connectivity."

When finance employees lose their recurring interactions with operational teams the group can "become more of a utility" and unable to bring additional value to the business such as strategic advice, he said. "The trick is to find balance," he said.

Mr. Wyden pointed to growing adoption of STARTLS in the private and public sectors in his letter, which comes after his office encouraged multi-factor authentication throughout the federal government.

Oracle's Cyber Plan: Oracle's Larry Ellison opened his company's annual conference Sunday by announcing a new database that he said can autonomously patch its own security flaws, "http://www.businessinsider.com/oracle-18c-database-patch-cybersecurity-flaws-2017-10". Mr. Ellison said machine learning makes it possible for Oracle's database to squash security bugs without going offline, and that the program will continuously improve itself without a human in control. "There is no pilot error anymore, because there is no pilot."

USPS Identity Questions: Security researchers and "https://krebsonsecurity.com/2017/10/usps-informed-delivery-is-stalkers-dream/" whether the U.S. Postal Service offers sufficient identity protection measures as part of its new "Informed Delivery" offering. When identifying the correct user, the program, which aims to inform users about when their mail and packages is delivered, relies on a discredited form of validation known as "knowledge-based authentication," which Mr. Krebs noted is relatively easy to exploit. The Postal Service said it will include improved authentication techniques within months.

Insider Threat Hype Debunked: Recent disclosures of sensitive material removed from the U.S. National Security Agency highlight the difficulty of detecting insider threats, even for one of the most advanced intelligence agencies in the world. Two former NSA employees downloaded classified information, printed it and walked out of the headquarters at Fort Meade, Md. before they were identified as possible culprits. That's exactly how insider threat detection software is not supposed to work, "https://www.cyberscoop.com/nsa-insider-threat-reality-winner-harold-martin/". Instead, the pricey programs meant to stop wrongdoing should identify suspicious behavior when it occurs.

Silk Road Proceeds: The "https://www.justice.gov/usao-sdny/pr/acting-manhattan-us-attorney-announces-forfeiture-48-million-sale-silk-road-bitcoins" Friday the government has reaped $48,238,116 in proceeds from the sale of bitcoin confiscated from the Silk Road, a shuttered dark net website meant for drug sales. The arrest and conviction of Silk Road founder Ross Ulbricht marked the point when many in the general public learned about a digital underground where drugs were sold alongside hacking tools and other nefarious goods for cryptocurrency. The government's sale of the forfeited bitcoin comes at a time when the price, and awareness, of the virtual currency is near an all-time high.

Faith Grows In Japan, Defying Past False Dawns

By Ira Iosebashvili and Kosaku Narioka 
932 words
3 October 2017
The Wall Street Journal (Asia Edition)
AWSJ
A1
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Global investors are betting that Japan is on course to pull out of its long economic slump, reflecting hopes that growth is accelerating world-wide and sending stocks to multiyear highs.

Japan's economy, the world's third largest, grew at its fastest pace in two years in the second quarter and is on its longest expansion streak in more than a decade, spurred by its central bank's ultraloose monetary policy and a nascent recovery around the globe. Business and consumer spending has rebounded, a weaker currency has made Japanese exports more competitive abroad, and stimulus from the Bank of Japan continues to support markets.

The combination has meant good news for Japanese stocks. The Nikkei Stock Average hit a new two-year high on Monday. The close was some 2.2% below the 2015 high, which was the highest since 1996.

Yet many remain cautious on Japan, where relatively cheap stocks and the promise of a resurgent economy have beguiled generations of bargain hunters. Rallies in the Nikkei over the past few years have come and gone, but the average has never approached the 1989 high of 38916, amid lackluster growth, low inflation and poor corporate governance. While Japan's stocks have traded at a nearly 40% discount to their global peers, flows from foreign investors have been sporadic, with few willing to buy into Japan for the long term.

Many investors would see a sustained rebound in Japan, where growth has lagged behind Europe and the U.S. for decades, as a sign that the global recovery from the financial crisis is picking up steam.

"The U.S. recovered, and then Europe came on and accelerated, and now it looks like the Japanese data is starting to come along," said Torsten Slok, chief international economist at Deutsche Bank. "Despite the facts staring them in the eyes, people hang on to the old story for too long before they realize a new story has developed."

The Japanese market's long funk has roots in the bursting of the "bubble economy" in the early 1990s. Land and stock prices collapsed, sending Japan's financial system into a tailspin from which it didn't fully recover until the early 2000s. Companies and consumers tried to pare debt, leading to deflation and a sense of stagnation, while political turmoil delayed shareholder-friendly policies.

Through false starts and years of subpar economic performance, many global investors have stayed away, even as Japanese companies have become more profitable.

Return on equity -- one measure of profitability -- has improved to around 8.4% in 2017 from just above 5% when Prime Minister Shinzo Abe took office in late 2012, according to research by AllianceBernstein Holding L.P. Yet Japanese stocks trade on average at 1.5 times book value, or the total value of their assets outside of what they owe -- a 36% discount to the developed- market average. Foreign investors remain thinly represented in Japanese equities despite a roughly 6.5% rally in the Nikkei in 2017, according to an AllianceBernstein analysis.

"Investors have left Japan for dead and are only now starting to come back," said Vadim Zlotnikov, chief market strategist at AllianceBernstein.

But "many investors are still skeptical," Mr. Zlotnikov said. "People have been burned over and over."

Even as signs of improvement mount, the Bank of Japan continues to support markets by buying 6 trillion yen ($53 billion) in exchange-traded stock funds annually. Japanese companies have taken steps to make themselves more attractive to investors, such as adding outside directors to their boards and boosting returns through share buybacks.

Areas where Japanese companies are at the forefront include artificial intelligence and robotics. The country's auto-parts makers are poised to benefit from a move to electric vehicles, while cosmetic makers reap profits from selling to Asia's growing middle class, investors said.

Strong profit growth has helped drive up shares of Sony Corp. and cosmetics maker Kose Corp. this year. Year to date, those two stocks have gained roughly 28% and 31% respectively, according to FactSet.

Benjamin Segal, a senior portfolio manager at Neuberger Berman, says he has grown more optimistic on Japan and added select companies to his equity portfolio this year. "I'm emerging from the 'no way' camp into an incrementally more positive view," Mr. Segal said. "There does seem to be change afoot."

Better profit margins anddividend payouts that have improved from "pathetic to simply mediocre" all argue in Japan's favor, Mr. Segal said. Japanese companies "merit more of an inspection today they would have a year or two ago, or even 10 years ago," he said. Among the companies he owns are chemicals and cosmetics maker Kao Corp. and air-conditioner manufacturer Daikin Industries Ltd.

Still, money managers own too few Japanese equities, and the yen is "grotesquely undervalued," Charles Gave, founder of research firm Gavekal, wrote in a note to investors. Corporate profits have grown faster in Japan than in the U.S. for the past five years, he said, while Japanese companies are sitting on around $4 trillion in cash. "The great beauty of these recommendations is that nobody. . .is positioned accordingly," he wrote.

Japan's economy remains shaky, especially because wages haven't grown fast enough under Mr. Abe to spark solid consumer spending. Some investors are nervous about Mr. Abe's plans to push through a tax increase for 2019. A recession followed a sales-tax increase in 2014.

JPMorgan s investment bank chief executive Daniel Pinto is handing over leadership of the lender s Europe, Middle East and Africa operation so he can focus on global strategy and technological innovation.

Mr Pinto, who is often listed among potential successors for JPMorgan Chase chief executive Jamie Dimon, will pass the Emea baton to Vis Raghavan, at present JPMorgan s Emea deputy chief executive and head of banking for the region.

Mr Pinto and asset management chief executive Mary Erdoes announced the leadership changes, according to a memo to staff on Monday morning. JPMorgan confirmed the contents of the memo but declined to comment further.

The news comes four months after London-based Mr Pinto, who remains JPMorgan s global head of investment and corporate banking, took on the role of joint global head of technology and operations across the JPMorgan Chase group, following the departure of the bank s chief operating officer Matt Zames.

Mr Pinto was already leading the world biggest investment bank by revenue and overseeing an Emea division that includes Europe s biggest investment bank by revenue as well as an asset management business and private banking.

The memo does not give any reason for the changing of responsibilities, but a person familiar with the decision-making said Mr Pinto would spend more time on the investment bank s global growth and on technological innovation.

Technology is having a profound effect on all parts of JPMorgan s business, and the group has responded with everything from chatbots at its retail bank to using artificial intelligence to execute some of its trades.

JPMorgan also has not been afraid to speak out against financial innovation it believes is dangerous   Mr Dimon recently precipitated a sharp fall in Bitcoin s value by declaring the cryptocurrency a  fraud 	. 	. 	. 	worse than tulip bulbs .

Mr Pinto will remain London-based, will continue to oversee JPMorgan s Brexit planning and will remain covered by the Financial Conduct Authority s Senior Managers Regime, which confers extra personal responsibility on the UK s most senior bankers.

Mr Raghavan, a 17-year JPMorgan veteran who was named Emea co-chief executive in April 2015, will continue in his role as head of Emea banking. Mr Raghavan was already JPMorgan s lead for liaising with regulators on the bank s Brexit strategy.

 As deputy CEO of Europe, Middle East and Africa, Vis Raghavan has been dedicated to serving our clients and colleagues every day while also upholding the firm s governance in the region,  wrote Mr Pinto and Ms Erdoes.

COMPANIES
Falling prices put pressure on shopping centres; Property: Commercial sector

By Judith Evans 
919 words
2 October 2017
Financial Times
FTFT
London Ed1
22
English
Copyright 2017 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Warning of 'slow bleed' in valuations as retail industry faces multiple challenges

The Bluewater shopping centre in Kent claims to "set the benchmark for the ultimate shopping and leisure experience".

But the recent sale of a stake in the 240-acre complex has prompted concerns about falling prices for even the UK's most highly prized shopping centres. Investors are questioning whether property owners have fully absorbed the impact of the changes buffeting the retail industry - from the shift to e-commerce and rising inflation to falling consumer confidence.

Hermes Investment Management agreed in September to sell 7.5 per cent of the Bluewater centre to insurer Royal London for 155m - some 7 per cent below its original asking price.

The figure implies Bluewater's total worth is about 8 per cent lower than the value Landsec - the UK's largest listed property company - holds on its books for its 30 per cent stake and lower than the price it paid for the stake in 2014, according to an analysis by Numis Securities.

Robert Duncan, analyst at Numis, said the price may begin a "slow bleed into valuations" across the UK's most upmarket shopping centres, most of which are owned by listed companies.

Falling values come partly from downward pressure on rents, Mr Duncan said: "Landlords face a substantial challenge if they are trying to gain any kind of rental growth."

Landlords of the UK's largest shopping centres - often listed companies such as Landsec, British Land, and retail specialists Hammerson and Intu - have so far argued they are less subject to the pressures affecting smaller, cheaper retail properties.

As retailers shrink their numbers of stores, landlords say, they are focusing on key locations as showrooms for their brands, plus hubs for click-and-collect deliveries.

"When we are having conversations with most of the prime retailers, they want more space [in our centres], not less," said David Fischel , chief executive of Intu.

But Mr Duncan and other analysts believe top shopping centres are not immune from the pressures affecting their smaller competitors.

Next, one of the UK's largest clothing chains and Intu's second-largest tenant, told investors earlier this year that stores were "an important part of our online service to the increasing number of customers who collect and return their orders through our stores". But this did not translate into a willingness to pay higher rents, it said, adding that it was agreeing rents for new stores opening in 2017-18 that were 24 per cent lower per square foot than existing ones.

So far, property companies have been cushioned by landlord-friendly contractual terms and have generally been able to secure small rises in average rents year on year. But they are losing some of their negotiating power, analysts say, while landlords' own expansion plans may not help.

According to Hemant Kotak, analyst at Green Street Advisors, big "Grade A" shopping centres plan extensions amounting to 12m sq ft in the next six years. "That's a material amount that you are adding," he said.

"Grade A malls are going to grab more and more market share, but can they grab it at a fast enough pace to offset the square feet they are adding?"

Shopping centre operators are also seeking to enhance the "experience" they offer customers, with more food and drink options, along with activities from cinemas to dry ski slopes. However, "casual dining" chains such as Byron Burger, which had helped drive this trend, are now slowing their expansion and even considering closing branches, according to reports.

When it comes to the shopping centre landlords, markets are sceptical that their assets are worth the prices currently on their books.

Intu is currently trading at a discount of 43 per cent to the book value of its assets, and Hammerson at a discount of 31 per cent, according to Numis figures, against an average 17 per cent discount for real estate investment trusts.

Capital values for prime UK shopping centres had already fallen by 3.5 per cent in the year to the end of June, according to figures from commercial property group CBRE - although rents continued to edge upwards by an average 1.8 per cent.

The Bluewater stake sold by Hermes came with no management power over the centre, which may have depressed its value. So investors are watching what happens to the next stake due to be sold.

The Singaporean sovereign wealth fund, GIC, and Australian group Lendlease are together selling stakes that amount to 42.5 per cent of the development for about 900m, having earlier sought separate sales totalling more than 1bn.

In the sector as a whole, short sellers believe values have further to fall. In the US, there are similar worries about the future of Grade A shopping malls and internationally, short positions against retail real estate investment trusts have almost doubled this year to $12.4bn, according to financial analytics company S3 Partners. This includes $455m of current positions against Intu and $124m against Hammerson.

Online shopping is more common in the UK than other countries including the US, Germany and France, according to figures assembled by research firm Green Street. But this does not mean the transition is over, said Richard Hyman, a retail consultant.

"We are seeing fundamental structural change," he said. "This is going to be painful, and it is going to last for some years."

FT REPORT - FUND MANAGEMENT
JPMAM takes long-term view of North Korea and Mifid; Chris Willcox

By Chris Flood 
1,106 words
2 October 2017
Financial Times
FTFT
Surveys FNM1
04
English
Copyright 2017 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

CEO not concerned about short-term volatility, but worries about end of QE

Global tensions over North Korea's nuclear ambitions are the biggest threat to the stability of financial markets, says Chris Willcox, chief executive of JPMorgan Asset Management, who is in London to meet clients after attending an annual gathering of Asia's business leaders in Singapore .

"I was quite startled by how worried people in Asia appear to be about the possibility of a missile accident or mistake from North Korea . There is a higher level of anxiety than I anticipated," says Mr Willcox, who leads a $1.6tn business that sits underneath the US bank's asset management and wealth unit led by Mary Callahan Erdoes.

But Pyongyang's bellicose rhetoric has barely dented investor confidence. The S&P 500 hit an all-time high shortly before North Korea's foreign minister accused Donald Trump , the US president, of declaring war .

JPMAM's default position is to advise clients to ignore the short-term volatility caused by such geo-political tensions, which, historically, have proved irrelevant for long-term returns to investors.

But Mr Willcox is concerned about developments in bond markets after his long years of experience managing fixed-income teams. He initially worked for Citigroup after leaving university and joined JPMorgan in 2006 as global head of rates before assuming his current role in 2012.

"I am concerned about what fixed income markets are telling us," says the 49-year-old Englishman who has lived in New York since 2012. "The unwinding of accommodative monetary policy by central banks makes logical sense but it also presents a very asymmetric risk. There is no real evidence of inflation, so monetary tightening could very quickly lead to positive real interest rates and deflation, which would be very hard for the world to deal with."

JPMAM's assumption is that central banks will move extremely cautiously in normalising monetary policy. This lengthy process, combined with unfavourable demographic developments, high levels of debt and weak productivity growth, will slow the pace of economic recovery and weigh on returns from asset markets.

JPMAM is forecasting that nominal annualised returns from US bonds will average just 3 per cent over the next 10 to 15 years, while US equities are expected to deliver returns of around 6 per cent over the same period. This weak performance compared with historic returns will lead to further downward pressure on fees across asset management, says the father of two daughters.

"The whole investment industry will need to become more efficient over time. Asset management is still very profitable, with profit margins close to 30 per cent. But lower investment returns will intensify pricing pressures," he says, pointing to reductions of $270m in mutual fund fees by JPMAM over the past three years.

Around 90 per cent of JPMAM assets are actively managed, but Mr Willcox dismisses the suggestion that index-tracking funds are attracting more inflows because they offer better value to investors.

"People forget that the vast majority of passive products are not particularly cheap. Fees for active funds have also been going down. Pricing pressures across active and passive funds have proportionately been about the same," he says.

JPMAM is expanding its beta strategies and exchange traded fund business, and Mr Willcox sees a valuable role for both active and passive.

He estimates that US retail investors pay total costs of between about 150 and 250 basis points annually, with investment management fees accounting for just 40 bps.

"The rest sits between the end client and the product manufacturer. It is the person [distributor] in the middle that will be most affected," he says.

But he is sceptical about the potential for robo-advisers - which use algorithms to recommend a portfolio based on an investor's answers to an online questionnaire - to disrupt existing distribution channels.

"The technology behind robo-advice is pretty good but it is not super sophisticated. It is an efficient way to help investors, but these are services that asset managers could eventually give away for free. What is more interesting is if we can use big data and artificial intelligence to truly understand a client's spending patterns, their stock of wealth and their risk appetite. That would be a game-changing technology, but we are still a long way from that."

"It is active managers that will be best placed to harness big data and artificial intelligence. It will be those that can find new sources of information that can inform their decisions and make their investment process more efficient that will thrive."

Large investment managers such as JPMAM often draw on research and information gathered continuously across different time zones to run global portfolios that invest in multiple jurisdictions.

Under new European rules known as Mifid II , which come into force in January, payments for research must be separated out from trading costs so that clients understand clearly what they are being charged for.

JPMAM has decided to absorb the cost of third-party research rather than passing it on to clients.

"We want to do the right thing for clients. The previous model was outdated, so it was an easy decision for us," he says.

Mifid II will change the contours of the playing field.

"An asset manager like us that has invested heavily in its proprietary research platform as the main driver of investment insights and excess returns will be less worried by the Mifid changes than smaller managers. The ability of small managers to access a broad range of research over time will be compromised under Mifid."

Mr Willcox says that what asset managers really want is certainty.

"We seldom disagree with what regulators want to achieve, but what the industry and clients need is certainty, an even playing field that is not open to different interpretations. Markets are becoming increasingly global and we would like regulators to be more co-ordinated," he says.

Pro Cyber
Under GDPR, Data Protection Officers May Help CEOs Stay Employed

By Adam Janofsky 
936 words
2 October 2017
12:22
WSJ Pro Cybersecurity
RSTPROCY
English
Copyright  2017, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

After a data breach at Equifax Inc. compromised the information of 143 million U.S. consumers and cost the company's chief executive his job, cybersecurity experts say a key provision in an upcoming European rule may insulate CEOs in the event of similarly massive hacks in the future.

The General Data Protection Regulation, which will apply to companies that collect data on European Union citizens starting on May 25, 2018, requires businesses to appoint a data-protection officer who reports directly to the firm's top management level.

Cybersecurity experts say this new role may have two immediate implications in an Equifax-like situation: The data-protection officer could notify key executives of vulnerabilities and breaches shortly after they are discovered, and that person could also be treated as a scapegoat if something goes wrong.

"Security experts at U.S. multinationals say they often cannot find time to speak to the big boss," said Gonca Dhont, the managing director of Brussels-based DPO Network Europe, a recruiting firm that specializes in data-protection officers. "Under GDPR they want to elevate this person--give them more visibility and weight."

Faster Response

The main role of a data-protection officer is to monitor compliance with GDPR--a vast regulation that requires companies to disclose a breach within 72 hours of becoming aware of the incident, delete personal information upon request, and rigorously document cybersecurity practices, among other things.

"Their job is to keep the CEO and the board of directors apprised of the status of the organization--are they in the green zone, or are there problems," said Aaron Tantleff, a partner at Foley & Lardner LLP who specializes in privacy and information management.

In this capacity, a data-protection officer may well have been able to alert Equifax's top managers of vulnerabilities before hackers could exploit them, said Jeff Dennis, managing partner at Newmeyer & Dillion LLP and co-chair of its cybersecurity practice.

"My sense is if they had this person in place it would have ensured the software they were using would be secure," Mr. Dennis said, adding that a data-protection officer would have a key role in conducting security audits, penetration tests, and monitoring for software vulnerabilities. "It certainly is a vital role and one that companies need to carefully consider who they're going to appoint."

Having a data-protection officer in place could have also spared Equifax from reputational damage in more than one way, according to Marcus Christian, a partner at Mayer Brown LLP.

The company has come under investigation after several executives sold shares in the company after the breach was discovered but before it was disclosed. The company, which has formed a "https://www.bloomberg.com/news/articles/2017-09-29/equifax-board-to-review-executives-stock-sales-following-hack" to review the stock sales, initially said the executives were not aware of the hack when they sold nearly $2 million in shares days after underlings learned of the incident.

"We don't know if they had insider knowledge or not, but it looks bad," said Mr. Christian, adding that a data-protection officer would have likely ensured that key executives were informed of a breach shortly after its discovery. "It's not good on the reputational front, regardless of what happens."

Equifax was also criticized by politicians and potential victims for the amount of time it took to disclose the breach, for example. Under the GDPR, data-protection officers must disclose a data breach to supervisory authorities "not later than 72 hours after having become aware of it," along with the measures that will be taken "to mitigate its possible adverse effects."

Equifax was infiltrated in March, discovered the breach on July 29, and disclosed it on Sept. 7, "https://www.wsj.com/articles/hackers-entered-equifax-systems-in-march-1505943617" reported.

"Companies are looking at this incident and it's increasing the sense of urgency in reporting a breach," said Mr. Christian. "Being perceived as not notifying affected people or regulators soon enough is problematic."

DPO Scapegoats

Data-protection officers could also end up in a situation where they're blamed after a data breach if something goes wrong.

Equifax chief executive Richard Smith said last week he would leave the company--a move meant to show that the firm's leadership would be held accountable for the breach, "https://www.wsj.com/articles/equifax-ceo-richard-smith-to-retire-following-massive-data-breach-1506431571" reported.

Since data-protection officers are meant to advise chief executives on vulnerabilities and data breaches, they may end up as easy scapegoats.

One factor that prevents this practice from being abused is that the regulation forbids companies from firing or penalizing data-protection officers for performing their duties.

"What it comes down to is if they did their job or not," said Mr. Tantleff.

The answer to that question could depend on the affected organization, though, and the specific situation.

Kenneth Sanford, U.S. lead analytics architect at Dataiku Inc., a data science software company that helps clients with GDPR compliance, said the job of a data-protection officer is an "impossible role" and it would be easy for a chief executive to say they failed to carry out their assigned tasks.

"These are giant responsibilities--you have to show what information you have, how you use it, and delete it if someone requests it," he said. "The big question is will these individuals be given the funding to realistically do these tasks."

(Adam Janofsky writes about cybersecurity for WSJ Pro, with a speciality in small business. He previously worked at Inc. magazine, Bloomberg News, and managed the WSJ's startup blog. Write to Adam at adam.janofsky@wsj.com.)
