Economy
Transcript: Conversation With Fed's Patrick Harker in Philadelphia; Central banker discusses his outlook on interest rates, the Fed's plans to reduce its bondholdings and cybersecurity at a fintech conference

6,564 words
29 September 2017
22:39
WSJ Pro Financial Regulation
RSTPROFR
English
Copyright  2017, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Federal Reserve Bank of Philadelphia President Patrick Harker took part in a question-and-answer session at a fintech conference at the Philadelphia Fed on Friday, Sept. 29, 2017. He discussed his outlook on interest rates, the Fed's plans to reduce its bondholdings and cybersecurity. The conversation was moderated by Mukul Pandya, editor-in-chief at the Wharton School's Knowledge@Wharton online journal. Here is a transcript of the exchange, lightly edited for length and clarity.

MUKUL PANDYA: So we've been talking a lot about fintech. And maybe we should start by taking a little rest from that before we come back to fintech. And let's talk about what's on top of everyone's mind when   especially when they hear from you. And when they think about you as a Fed president, the thought that comes to mind is inflation. So what do   I mean, this week, as Chair [Janet] Yellen said on Tuesday, I think, that the Fed may have overstated the strength of the labor market and the rate of inflation. It has come down to   I think this year to 1.4 percent. What do you think is happening on the inflation front? And are rate increases justified this year or next year? What are your views?

PATRICK T. HARKER: So what's happening in monetary policy land?

Well, first, let me thank, Mukul, you and Dick. By the way, I'm still doing railroads. Instead of steel rails, they're financial rails, right? (Laughter.) They're rails of the payment system. So it's all railroading. And I want to thank Julapa and the whole team and all our sponsors and supporters. This was a great conference.

So what's happening in monetary policy? And I'll put the inflation picture in context. So, if we think about GDP [gross domestic product] growth, GDP growth continues along just as we pretty much have anticipated, according to our forecasts: running slightly above trend, around 4   2.3, 2.4 [percent], you can pick a number somewhere north of 2. Q1 was weak. Q1 is always weak. You should recognize that we have a seasonal issue with measurement of GDP in quarter one, and so take that with a grain of salt. Q2 was just revised up slightly to slightly over 3 percent, 3.1 percent. So, in terms of GDP, things seem to be going along just fine.

Now, of course, the devastation that we're seeing from these hurricanes   this horrific devastation   is obviously terrible for the people who are involved, and our thoughts go out to them. But in terms of the economy as a whole, the best estimate right now is that we'll see about 100-basis-point decline in GDP this quarter, and then it'll bounce back to about 1.3 percent in a subsequent quarter, some number around there. So it will have an effect, but it will be a transitory effect as that works through the economy. So that's on the GDP front.

On employment, I think the best   I can say, by the way, everything I say are my views alone and the views of nobody else   (laughter)   in the Federal Reserve system or on the Federal Open Market Committee. But with respect to employment, we're running below what most people would think is the natural rate of unemployment. The labor markets feel really tight. And that's not only in the data in terms of unemployment, but if you look at the JOLTS [job openings and labor turnover survey] data, the job opening and quits data, things are really tight. And you're hearing this. I mean, yes, the plural of anecdote is not data, but we're hearing this over and over and over again of the skills mismatch question. Companies can't find the skilled workers. And, again, we're not talking about Ph.D. economists and computer scientists. We're talking about truck mechanics, electricians, plumbers. You can go down the list. There's just a real   it's a very tight market.

Now, we get to the inflation question. So if that's true, where did the Philips curve go, right? What happened to it? Why   did it just disappear? Well, the conventional wisdom is it's flat. That it's been flat for a while. And that relationship between unemployment and inflation we're just not seeing. So what's happening with wages? Well, they are flat. But some recent work that was done by our colleagues in San Francisco point out the fact that if you look at somebody who's staying in a job through a sustained period of time, their wages are going up well above what we're seeing on average. It may be just a compositional shift. Part of the story of the wage issue is that we, the boomers, are retiring   high-price boomers being replaced by lower-price millennials   and that is keeping the average down for now. Again, there needs to be more work in this area, but we are starting to hear and see some wage pressure.

Now, there's some recent work done by Mike Dotsey and his colleagues here at the Philly Fed research department on the Philips curve. And so the conclusion of that work is if it's   whether it's dead of not, for the last several decades it's not been a very good predictor of inflation, period. And there may be other metrics and other measures that are better predictors of inflation than what we've traditionally thought of with the Philips curve. So that is the one thing, if you think of our dual mandate, that is problematic, is inflation.

The job market seems to be incredibly healthy on average   again, there are pockets where we need to bring people off the sidelines into the labor force and help them get the skills they need to   and that's something we're doing here in Philadelphia with what we're calling the economic growth and mobility project, is working with communities trying to do research and outreach in that area. Things like apprenticeship programs. We just put out a report on apprenticeship programs. They are not just in   they're not your mother and father's apprenticeship programs. They're no longer just in the trades. But you're seeing it in IT, in health care, financial services. Apprenticeships are now becoming, as the market's gotten tight, one way for companies to get the talent they need.

So on inflation we are running below our 2 percent target. Now, only for bankers and central bankers is this a concern, that oh my gosh we're behind on the 2 percent target. The average American walking out in the street right there is saying: I have a job. Inflation's 1 percent. Life is good. And the Eagles won an amazing game last week, right? (Laughter.) So what's wrong with this picture? Everything's great. Well, of course, there are risks to   when it comes to   bankers would like higher margins   obviously, spreads. And we have risk of if and when another negative shocks hits the economy having the legroom to be able to move rates down. That is a problem.

So inflation is the one area that does give us a little bit of pause   gives me pause. And it's a complicated story, right? And it may be that the Philips curve or Philips curve-like mechanisms will reassert themselves. There's also work being done here in Philly led by Leonard Nakamura that is pretty persuasive that we may be overstating inflation, even as low as it is. And as a result, we may be understating productivity growth in the economy

And, you know, Leonard's favorite example is the iPhone. We no longer have GPS. We no longer have all these other things. It's all on the phone. We no longer have a Game Boy. I used that once at a college campus and nobody knew what I was talking about. (Laughter.) So we no longer have all those things because we have the iPhone 10 years out. But we don't know how to capture that shift in the statistics. We can do it hedonically, in retrospect. But in real time, for policy makers, we're having a hard time figuring out how to capture that.

So I think that, then, leads me to be   as a policy maker to say, well, given that, let's just let things play out a little bit. Let's just see how the data evolves over the next several periods. Today's PCE [personal-consumption expenditures] report was weak, so we'll have to see how this plays out.

I think hitting a pause for a little bit on the fed-funds path is appropriate for two reasons. One, see the data play out. Second, we are unwinding our balance sheet, and that process will start, as we announced last week after the FOMC, in October. And so I think it's time to just let that play out. We'll start that process. That process   as I've said to other audiences, we want to do this in a way that is as exciting as watching paint dry, all right? I mean, we want to lay this out very clearly, what it is, the caps are very low.

And the best estimate that I've seen is that will have an impact the equivalent of a 25-basis-point increase in the fed-funds rate over a 5-year or 4-year period. So we're not talking about a very large impact. But we don't know for sure. And I think prudence would call for just letting that happen, see how the markets react. I've still penciled in an increase in December and three increases for '18, assuming that inflation comes back. But I do emphasize the word "pencil in." We'll just have to see how things evolve.

MR. PANDYA: So we'll keep the pencil fairly light for now. (Laughter.)

MR. HARKER: Right.

MR. PANDYA: But just to follow up on the last point you made about the unwinding or the winding down or the $4.5 trillion balance sheet, how long do you think the process will take?

MR. HARKER: It'll take a while. It will take several years. And again, going   the question is not so much the timing, but what are we going back to, right? That's what you really need to know.

And there, if you just do the math, so our currency liability, say   call it 1 trillion, and then we have about $400 billion in the Treasury account, plus some other foreign, reverse repo, and there's little things here and there. So we get up to some number, call it $2 trillion, right?

So how   the question is, how   what do we need in order to effectively run monetary policy, right, to execute monetary policy? Well, this gets into a debate about whether you want a floor   a floor system or a corridor system, and you need to know that elasticity of the demand for reserves. We've been way out, right, on that curve   (laughs)   for now quite a while. So we're not quite sure, in this economy, what that elasticity is. We're on the flat end for sure. And there's some view   and I'm sympathetic to this view   that a floor system makes some sense.

The issue is we don't know when we stop being in a floor system and go into a corridor system. That is when the elasticity increases. Again, there's some recent research by some of our economists here that said, you know, that number could be as high as a trillion dollars. We just don't know. And others will say, well, it could be as low as a hundred billion. So, given the tried-and-true scientific method of picking the middle, right, you pick some number in the middle and it would get you to a balance sheet of 2.5-ish, somewhere around there, going forward. So what's really when we   but we'll know it when we get there. I mean, we'll be able to see how the market's reacting. We'll be able to respond appropriately.

MR. PANDYA: Looping back now briefly to the topic of fintech, which is   we have had some fascinating conversations over the last couple of days on this. I mean, one thing that has struck me in listening to all the wonderful speakers is sometimes it seems like we almost have this tsunami of financial technology innovation going on everywhere, but to what extent do you see it as being revolutionary and truly disruptive, and to what extent is it evolutionary and something that can be integrated into the existing financial system? I mean, it would be wonderful to have your overall perspective on that issue.

MR. HARKER: Yes. (Laughter.) I think it can be both, right? It doesn't have to be one or the other. There are clearly important   and we've heard from people here   important innovations happening in the market. That said, financial services have been in the business of innovating with technology for a long, long time, right? This is not something new to financial services. It feels different now because of just it's not the traditional banking institutions and other institutions that are doing it. There are new entrants into the market. As I think the conversation yesterday when I was here we were talking about, but I think people are finding out   not everybody, but   they're very good at innovation to the customer. But then I've got all this compliance stuff and I've got all this other stuff that I have to worry about, and it may be better to partner with the institutions that know how to do that and also get scale.

And this is not   this is not very different than many other industries, whether it's retail or you can sort of go through the history of this innovation. There are going to be the Amazons of the world that emerge, but then others will figure out how to adopt this technology or partner with people and be successful.

The fundamentals don't change very much, right? People still want some basic things out of the financial system. You know, access to credit. They want to be able to pay their bills. They want to have safe and secure funds, right? And they want   and they want to have other services that can help them succeed, whether they're a business, small business, or a consumer. So I think those fundamentals are still there. It's just how we deliver them is changing. And that will continue to evolve. So I think it can be both.

MR. PANDYA: So, in addition to the revolution versus evolution issue, the other big thing that has come up in the past couple of days is what are the key benefits of fintech and how do you   how do you see the tradeoff between the risks   I mean, the benefits and the biggest risks of fintech?

MR. HARKER: I think there's clearly   and you can see the uptake, again, not only in the traditional banks, but also in the new entrants   there's clearly a desire, and I'm one of them, to   I don't walk into a branch unless I guess I want to yell at somebody. That's about the only reason I would. (Laughter.) I mean, so I'm   I enjoy having access to all the mobile platforms and all the ability to do that, and I think that's the advantage that we're seeing across the board, right?

And so hopefully we'll also see an advantage of people who are marginalized in the financial system being able to get access to the financial system. I think, for me, that's more aspirational right now, but I think it's really important. And I think it's important for the system that we continue have ubiquity, that everybody can have access and plug into the financial system. And in particular, if there are entrants coming into the market who can bring the unbanked into the market, help educate them about how to create a sound financial future for themselves, we're all better off as an economy.

The risk   I think the large risk   is that many   not all, but many of the new entrants were born in a period where they've never seen a deep down-side of a business cycle. And banks generally have been through that. They know how to manage that risk. If there's a concern I have, it's that the institutions   to make sure they have the proper risk procedures and policies to protect themselves when that happens. We will   I'm not saying when, because nobody really knows when   but we will have that down side of the business cycle. And to make sure that institutions are protected, and hence consumers are protected, in that process I think is critical.

MR. PANDYA: "Fintech" is such a sort of catch-all phrase. It's so vast.

MR. HARKER: Yeah.

MR. PANDYA: So in the next few minutes I was wondering if we could drill a little deeper into some specific areas of fintech. And one question that came up yesterday that was very interesting was in the session with the OCC's [Office of the Comptroller of the Currency] acting comptroller. There was some discussion about the fact that Barclays, Credit Suisse, and many other large financial institutions have now formed a consortium to create a digital currency. Japan also   as you may have seen in the Financial Times this week, some leading Japanese banks are creating their own digital currency   I think it's called J-Coin   with the support of Japan's central bank. Looking to the U.S., do you believe that the creation of these digital currencies will threaten the ability of central banks to maintain control of money supply?

MR. HARKER: I'm not sure yet. At this point, I would be on the side of sort of doubting that for a couple of reasons. One, I agree with the acting comptroller that we should let the market evolve and see how this technology evolves and we should not   whether we can create a sandbox or other ways of doing it so that people can have the ability to innovate.

That said, I think you have to   some of the fundamentals haven't changed. For the economy as a whole, the paper that's in your pocket that we call money only has value because we believe it has value, because we believe that the government stands behind it, right? It's all trust issue. And so whether cryptocurrencies and other forms of currency emerge, I think the basis of that has to be how do they create that trust. In the case of a central bank issued digital currency, it is the government standing behind it, right? The sovereign state standing behind it. You could have other models of that happening, but they have to play out over time. And because, again, everything can work in good times. It's when the bad times hit.

And the other issue I am personally concerned about is   whether it's a government-issued or central-bank issued digital currency or another large player comes in and issues this digital currency   when things really go back, where do Americans turn, right? I mean, if things really turned out bad? Well, they're going to come back to the government. That's been the history of the country in recent vintage. And so we'd have to think through that. And again, this is one where we're not ready for this year. We're seeing it evolve. But it is something we continue to study, and I continue to study and learn from, and the experience of others who have done this.

MR. PANDYA: To make sure that things don't go back, the role of the regulator is very critical. And the question there is, how can you regulate in such a way that you mitigate the risks that you are talking about? And even more fundamentally, how do you regulate an algorithm? (Laughter.)

MR. HARKER: That's a good question. I don't know yet. I think the answer is we have to continue to study this. And as these technologies evolve and the innovation evolves in the market, we need to continue to work and study how this is evolving. The issue of regulating algorithms is interesting. One of my colleagues, a computer scientist, is doing some interesting work on fairness in machine learning algorithms. If you think about all of you in this room, and everybody's adopting machine learning algorithms.

But how do you plug into that some sense of fairness, right, whether it's a   required by a regulatory constraint or just you believe that's part of your business   the right way of doing business? That's actually a lot harder than it sounds, apparently. It's a very difficult thing to do. So before you even think about how do you regulate an algorithm, how would you even build an algorithm that would have that sense of fairness in it is a technical, but a fairly deep technical question.

MR. PANDYA: The fairness issue is especially interesting because the presentation that we just heard this morning about how important financial inclusion is as a dimension of fintech. I mean, we just heard presentations from, you know, companies that are being so proactive in bringing unbanked and underbanked populations, at lowering their costs of borrowing, et cetera. How do you see the relationship between fintech and financial inclusion?

MR. HARKER: Oh, I think done right and done well it can have a tremendous impact. But we also have to recognize that we are human beings   we are flawed human beings, and there are also players in the market who are behaving in different ways. And so you hear these stories all the time. I hear these stories constantly of small business in particular getting into a situation where they didn't quite know what they signed up for, and then they walk into their community bank and say I got to get out of this deal, it's killing me. I signed up online for this thing and I thought it sounded good, but I didn't really understand it, I guess. And now I'm really in trouble.

And so here are good actors and bad actors in any industry. Financial services is not very different from anything else. And I think that is the role of the regulators. And not just at the national level, but even if there was no national, you know, OCC charter for a fintech company, there's still state regulatory requirements with respect to fairness to the consumer and so forth. So there's always going to be some form of regulation. And my message to the fintech companies is get involved with the regulators early and often to start forming what makes sense to protect the people who want to do it right, right?

Because I think that can protect the innovation, right, this sphere of innovation that you all need, and we want as an economy. But if there's too many bad actors in there, that can stifle that innovation, right? It can squash   because we know how regulation goes. It's always been this case. We tend to overreact to a crisis. When something bad happens, the pendulum swings too far. It's better to be in the conversation right now about what those regulations look like now, whether it's at the national level or at the state level.

MR. PANDYA: Well, one other thing about consumers especially need to be protected is because of what's happening with cybersecurity, especially in the wake of what's happened with Equifax. What is your view about the cybersecurity environment and how should consumers be protected?

MR. HARKER: So I think one of the big challenges for consumers right now, and actually this   we had a pretty robust conversation with a group of bankers the other day. So everybody can try to protect that account at that bank. But the consumer needs to figure out how to protect themselves. And increasingly, that's not just in financial data, but it's health data. You can go down the list of all the things, your identity. It creates challenges for the consumer and for the financial institutions. We were talking, I know, throughout the conference on AML [anti-money laundering] and BSA [Bank Secrecy Act]. That's going to get harder. I mean, much harder.

How do you really know your customer? I mean, especially if you've never   even if you physically see them. Given how much data and information is now out there about all of us, how do we   how do we change the system? How do we innovate to protect my identity   not just my financial identity. So all of us, because we got hacked here when OPM [U.S. Office of Personnel Management] got hacked. So everybody in the Fed has their credit fraud detection and so forth. But we have to continue to innovate in this space, too.

I actually worry about this a lot, because if we don't do this, again, a lot of the innovation that you are all fostering can be really stifled when customers just say, eh, I'm going back to microfiche and coin, right? (Laughter.) Because I've been hacked so many times and I've lost so much money and it's such a hassle to keep trying to clean up my credit or my health records or my false tax returns that somebody put into the IRS that, man, I'm just dropping out of this. That would be a dangerous step, I think, for the industry   all of you. What it means, though, is some innovative players have to come in with, I think, different ways of thinking about how to protect their identity.

MR. PANDYA: Just a couple of final questions before we open it up for everyone else. Looking to the future, maybe two or three years, what is your most optimistic and your most pessimistic scenario about fintech? And between the two, since you said "yes" to one question before, what is your most realistic scenario? (Laughter.)

MR. HARKER: So I think the optimistic one is   we were just talking about, that fintech not only reaches the consumers that have deep pockets, but also starts to bring the marginalized consumer into the market, right, and that financial inclusion. That that's critically important for the economy. The negative is that, again, whatever it is, there's continued hacking or there are regulatory issues that stop the innovation in this industry, right, that this   you can see   if we don't get this right and if the consumer doesn't learn to trust these innovations, they simply will not happen, right? And so it's all a trust game.

And so I think that's   the realistic scenario is there are going to be people who do it well. There are going to be people who that don't do it well. People who succeed. People who don't. That's the market. And I think we just need to make sure that we, as regulators, continue to make the playing field level and not get in the way to the extent we stifle this innovation.

MR. PANDYA: And can we ask one last question before we open it up? What is the biggest prevailing myth about fintech? And if you could say one thing today to dispel it, what would that be?

MR. HARKER: So, for my kids somehow it's that money magically appears in an account. (Laughter.) So I think that that's probably the biggest myth. Like, I don't know  

MR. PANDYA: That's not really related to fintech. (Laughter.)

MR. HARKER: Right. At least when they had to steal it out of my, you know, drawer in my bedroom   (laughs)   but now it's just   you know, that somehow a lot of the basic rules of financial services don't seem to apply.

Also, the other myth   which I am very concerned about with my three Millennial children   is not all technology is equal. Some of   some of this stuff you're putting yourself out there at tremendous risk because it's not very secure. They don't think about that. And so I think that's one of the bit myths that I know you are all concerned about, and we're concerned about as well.

MR. PANDYA: That's a great point.

And on that note, let's open it up. And who wants to go first?

Q: Hey. Michael at CoinDesk here. And thank you for asking about the work with Utility Settlement Coin.

I'm also curious what your thoughts are on the possibility of maybe someday issuing fiat on a blockchain. Is that something that you're exploring?

MR. HARKER: Well, I think there's work going on right now in terms of more exploration of   and watching what is evolving in the market, not just here in the U.S. but globally. I don't think we're ready to do that yet, but it is worth continuing to study. I don't think at this point, though   I would say, at least for me personally, I don't   I don't know enough yet and I don't think we collectively know enough yet to be able to make that decision.

MR. PANDYA: Right here.

MR. HARKER: Here.

Q: This is Will (sp) from University of Chicago.

I do have a question for President Harker. You mentioned about the optimistic outcome where there's more financial inclusion. I think there's also a lot of discussion related to fintech democratizing financial services, or kind of disintermediation, so to distribute the surplus to either consumers or some new entrants in the market. What's your view on that? Is that a function that fintech is already performing and can optimistically perform well in future?

MR. HARKER: Yeah, and it's a good question. So I think you really have to break down the services. I don't think you give, at least in my mind, an answer for financial services writ large, right? It's service by service, or what kind of things could be, will be disintermediated, or continued disintermediation. I mean, that's the other thing. It's not like all of a sudden that this is happening; it's been happening for a while. So it really   I think it really depends on the actual service itself.

And that, to me, comes down to you look at   take two completely different industries. Having just bought and sold a house, I used a real estate agent. Why? Because I don't buy and sell a house very often. As opposed to I don't use a travel agent. Why? Because I travel a lot. And so I think some of it is the familiarity with the service for the consumer and the opaqueness of the information. That's very clear in the real-estate business; you don't see a lot of   there's not a lot of transparency into some of the things that happen.

So I think if you map out   you parse out what financial services provide, there are going to be some services that people use on a routine basis, they easily can get the trust to do it, they can   and people can be disintermediated. There are others that people do on a very infrequent basis. And there, there may be some disintermediation, but often if I'm really sort of lost I need to find somebody that knows what they're doing because I don't think I know what I'm doing, right?

Because the disintermediation assumes   makes a big assumption, right, in my mind, that everybody is a rational actor that can be fully informed on this thing. Well, that's just not true, right? There's, A, some things that I   real estate is a good example. No matter how hard I try, those local agents have more inside information about the market than I can ever get, right? And so I just   there's no way, no matter which website I go on, I can't find out about the plumber who put in that work in that house, whether it was good or bad, right? It's just hard.

And the second thing is there are a lot of things I could do it, but I just don't want   it's just not worth my time to worry about it, right? So, yeah, I   because it takes so many cycles of my brain to think about this, and I only have so many in my life, and I'm not going to spend some on these things, right? I'm just going to   I'm just going to let somebody else do it and not worry about it.

So I think it really depends on those specific markets and the tastes of the consumers, right? So it's   there will be different flavors for different people. There will be different kinds of people, but there will be different players in the market, too, and there will be a matching issue.

So I think there will clearly be disintermediation. That's just in the nature of what we've seen in many other evolutions of technology. But how it turns out, it'll really depend on the specific service.

MR. PANDYA: Want to go   yeah, he has a question here.

Q: Thank you, President Harker, for these very interesting remarks.

Going back to Mukul's question about the framework that the Fed would eventually return to, on Wednesday the New York Fed published a blog post that linked the ability to pay the interest on excess reserves exclusively to going back to a floor system. I was wondering if you think that the Fed would need   continue to need the ability to pay interest on excess reserves if they went back to a corridor system. Or would a corridor system with a floor at zero be acceptable?

MR. HARKER: Yeah, that's a really good question. So, I mean, just to put it in context for those who are not deeply immersed in monetary policy world, if you're in a floor system, what defines the floor? Well, it should be the fed funds rate, but the trading in the fed funds rate, the reason there's no demand is there's very little trading in the fed funds market. So Congress, prior to the Great Recession, gave the Fed the authority to pay interest on excess reserves, and during the crisis asked the Fed to accelerate its implementation so that we have that tool. That creates a floor. It's a leaky floor because it's only open to certain players in the market. The overnight reverse repo market actually provides the floor floor. So the fed funds trade between those two.

So, if we went   if we truly went back to something that was a corridor system, we wouldn't need to have that tool in the same way, assuming we can stay in that corridor system. I think right now it is imprudent to remove that tool in any way, shape or form. I mean, it is what we have right now. Without it, we will have a very difficult time   the New York Desk would have an incredibly difficult time trying to execute what the Federal Open Market Committee says. Raise   I mean, so the committee says: Open Market Desk, thou shalt raise the fed funds within this 25-basis-point range. And they need the tools to do that. Right now, IOER [interest rate on excess reserves] is really the only one they have, along with the overnight reverse repo.

MR. PANDYA: Maybe one last question, perhaps. No, I think you   oh, yeah.

Q: You know, one of   in one of your responses to a question earlier you said something about come and talk to the regulators frequently. The problem, as I   as I see it, is that which regulator? So many of these   many of these new business models we see sort of fall in the interstices between different types of models. Is bitcoin a security? Is it a currency? So who do you talk to? I mean, there doesn't seem to be that much coordination between the regulatory agencies in trying to figure out what to do in this scenario here.

MR. HARKER: Yeah. You know, that's a   that is a fair criticism, but actually that's why I think meetings like this are really important. Because when I meet and talk, it isn't that we just have to sit over coffee and chat, you know, one on one. But I think we are collectively trying to figure this out, right? I mean, I think it is a fair criticism that we haven't completely figured it out because, frankly, nobody's completely figured it out because it's a fast-moving target. And so I think these kind of gatherings really matter. And the follow up from these kind of gatherings, continuing the conversations, really are important.

I think there is a movement within the supe and reg community across at least the federal agencies to have some   start to have some deeper conversations. But honestly, in this case, you know, as still a dyed-in-the-wool academic, I actually think the academic community can play an incredibly important role here in both providing the research, the different perspectives, and some of the convening. Yes, we can do these convenings, but I think it's also important for academic institutions to play a critical role in this area. Again, when you see this kind of summit, hazy future of what this is going to look like   and that's starting to happen. I mean, one of my colleagues, Bill Laufer at Wharton, just had a conference. He's a lawyer, and so they had a conference that's more on the regulatory front at Penn last week. The more of those we have, the more we talk about this, the better off we'll be.

Pro Private Markets
ECI in Talks to Buy Make It Cheaper

By William Louch 
320 words
29 September 2017
16:21
WSJ Pro Private Equity
RSTPROPE
English
Copyright  2017, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

London firm ECI Partners is nearing a deal to buy Make It Cheaper Group Ltd., a company that helps small and midsize businesses save money on bills, according to a person familiar with the matter.

The firm is in exclusive negotiations to buy the company, the person said, adding that there is no certainty a deal will be completed.

Make It Cheaper helps over 40,000 business customers compare and change their service providers, with a specific focus on the energy and telecoms sectors, according to accounts filed with Companies House in January.

The company posted turnover of 12.6 million ($16.9 million) last year and generated an operating profit of 1.62 million. The business said it has helped its customers save over 500 million since its inception a decade ago, the filings added.

Included in The Sunday Times's list of the 100 fastest-growing U.K. companies three years in a row between 2011 and 2013, Make It Cheaper has commercial partnerships with comparison websites including Moneysupermarket.com and Gocompare.com.

It also has a tie-up with the U.K.'s Federation of Small Businesses under which it provides a dedicated energy-switching service to the FSB's 200,000 members.

The company now employs 175 staff in the U.K., up from just five when it was founded in 2007.

ECI, the backer of companies including cycling retailer Evans Cycles, announced its first investment of the year in July, buying a minority stake in data analytics company Media IQ.

Financial details of the transaction weren't disclosed, but ECI typically invests in companies with enterprise values of between 20 million and 150 million.

The firm is currently investing its 10th fund, a 500 million vehicle, which was raised in 2014.

Nasdaq explores  alternatives  for its PR and digital media units

Nicole Bullock 
382 words
28 September 2017
18:45
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2017 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Nasdaq is exploring  strategic alternatives  for its public relations and digital media services units, stepping up its efforts to reshape the company to focus on technology, data and analytics.

The New York group, which owns trading venues in the US and Europe, may sell, restructure or seek a partner for the units, which generated about $195m of revenue in the 12 months to June 30, or about 8 per cent of Nasdaq total revenue.

The move marks a further refinement to Nasdaq s long-term strategy since chief executive Adena Friedman took over in January.

Since the start of the year Nasdaq has intensified its push into data and analytics, buying eVestment, which sells analytics to asset managers for $705m, and Sybenetix, a London-based software company that uses artificial intelligence to snuff out rogue traders.

In the same period it has taken a $578m charge on its underperforming US Treasuries trading venue and closed a struggling London trading venue.

 As a technology company, our growth path requires a review of our core strengths matched with the evolving needs of our clients,  Ms Friedman said in a statement on Thursday.

Nasdaq said it had not set a timetable to conclude the future of the public relations and digital media units, which provide outsourced media relations services to companies, such as publishing media releases as well as web hosting and webcasting of quarterly earnings calls and investor meetings.

In 2015, an operational error at one of the businesses led to the premature release of disappointing results from Twitter, causing some confusion. Twitter s share price also declined. and Nasdaq apologised.

 We believe exploring strategic alternatives like what they ve outlined today and refocusing capital and resources on the market technology, information services and certain other businesses better positions [Nasdaq to concentrate] on where the puck is going and the more valuable exchange opportunities of the future,  said Richard Repetto, an analyst at Sandler O Neill.

The exchange said it would retain the corporate services units that provide tools to boards of companies and investor relations teams.

Mid-morning in New York, shares of Nasdaq were up 0.9 per cent to $77.83. Year to date, shares are up 16 per cent.

The value of global private equity transactions has risen to $212bn, its highest since the boom years of a decade ago. The boom has been fuelled by record sums of unspent capital, cheap debt and highly favourable borrowing terms. Worldwide mergers and acquisitions activity totalled $2.4tn during the year-to-date 2017, up 2 per cent compared with the same period last year.

Among the deals is Toshiba s $18bn sale of its memory chip business to a consortium led by Bain Capital, which was signed on Thursday. The agreement came after a fraught eight-month bidding war that has jeopardised the future of one of Japan s biggest conglomerates and shone a light on the weakening ties between government and big companies that had long underpinned the Japan Inc model. (FT)

We are hosting a get together for our readers in New York City on Wednesday, October 18 at "http://click.newsletters.ft.com/f/a/5oLI8lq7XWoyUfZx00mBnQ~~/AAAAAQA~/RgRbradQP0EIAGzlabuAJfFXCGZpbnRpbWVzWAQAAAAAQgoAAZUiy1k5c8ZqUhdqYW1lcy5mb250YW5lbGxhQGZ0LmNvbQlRBAAAAABEHGh0dHA6Ly93d3cubG91aWVhbmRjaGFuLmNvbS-HWgF7InNvdXJjZSI6InNpbXBsZS1lbWFpbC1zZXJ2aWNlIiwidHJhbnNhY3Rpb25JZCI6IjBiNzU2MThmLTE4YjMtNDUwMS1hNWQ1LTc4OGVlM2QzOWFkMSIsInVzZXJVdWlkIjoiNDNjZjhhNzYtNDVjNy00NGUzLWE4YWQtNWYyMmUwZWZmY2EzIiwiYmluZGluZyI6ImVkaXRvcmlhbCIsImNhdGVnb3J5IjoibmV3c2xldHRlciIsInJlcXVlc3RlZFNlbmRUaW1lIjoiMTUwNjQ4NDgxMiIsImxpc3RJZCI6IjU4ZGI3MjE5MDBlYjZmMDAwNGQ1NmEyMyIsImVtYWlsSWQiOiI1OWNhNjM5ZGViMmZkZTAwMDQ3OGQ3NzYiLCJwcm9kdWN0IjoiRW1FIiwidGVtcGxhdGVJZCI6IjU4ZGE1ZjljMWYyNTllNjYyMzAwNmY1NCJ9" from 6.30pm-9pm. With less than two weeks to go until Procter & Gamble shareholders vote on whether to allow activist investor Nelson Peltz on to the board, his hedge fund Trian Partners is ramping up its publicity blitz.

The fund has been plastering adverts across social media   DD has spotted them on Twitter and Facebook, plus on several news sites, including the Financial Times   while churning out an increasing number of statements and letters to P&G directors and shareholders.

In the latest missive, five alumni of the HJ Heinz board, on which Peltz served, wrote to P&G directors on Wednesday to sing Peltz s praises. ( While discussions could get spirited, we viewed that as a good thing and all directors including Nelson were always open-minded to others  perspectives,  they wrote.)

The fund has also won over the proxy advisory firm Glass Lewis, which recommended late last week that P&G shareholders vote for Peltz to join the board. While the more influential proxy advisory firm, ISS, has yet to publish their recommendation, industry insiders are already predicting Peltz will get his coveted board seat   not because of any specific plan he is proposing, but more out of a  Why not?  mentality by shareholders, they say.

But Peltz s record in proxy fights is thin   he won one at Heinz more than a decade ago, while losing his fight to get a board seat at DuPont in 2015.

He was quick to anger P&G in the fight, too   by claiming the white ballot card. While whichever side files their proxy statement first can claim the ballot colour they d like, the company typically picks white, leaving the activist to claimanother colour. It definitely caused some consternation, said an insider.

P&G has fought back, filing a 106-page document with the Securities and Exchange Commission last week castigating Peltz s record on company boards, suggesting he added little value by being a director at similar companies, and saying he  has not produced any new ideas that make sense for P&G .

Energy M&A: Not a Total secret

Total s $7.5bn acquisition of oil and gas assets from AP Moller-Maersk of Denmark last month is still generating lots of chatter in the industry.

The surprise deal, involving about 1bn barrels of reserves in the unfashionable North Sea region, is being seen as a sign that the global oil majors are beginning to feel pressure to replenish their resource bases after a period of retrenchment since the 2014 oil price crash.

Just as interesting for dealmakers was the cloak-and-dagger manner in which Total snared its prey. Chief executive Patrick Pouyann (pictured) kept negotiations in an even tighter circle than is usual for such deals, with much of the company s top leadership kept in the dark until the last minute.

 It s because of you,  Pouyannjoked to the FT s Anne-Sylvaine Chassany, when asked about his secretive approach, implying that he wanted to avoid leaks.  I don t need many people to know internally 	. 	. 	. 	I wasn t alone, there s a competent CFO, chief legal counsel at Total.  He also used external lawyers and consultants, he said.

These big operations take a long time, he explained, and can be distracting for employees.  I need them to be focused on their daily job 	. 	. 	. 	Also I believe that for these big transactions it s the job of the CEO to be directly involved 	. 	. 	. 	and I don t need to inspect every well. 

What was critical was to convince the Maersk management to drop their plans for an IPO of the exploration and production unit:  So I went to see them myself. 

Total followed up its Maersk deal with a very different kind of deal last week, when it paid  237.5m for a 23 per cent stake in renewable energy company Eren. If the Maersk takeover was an expression of faith in oil and gas, this latest transaction was intended to show that Total is also preparing for the long-term transition towards cleaner energy.

Total s busy month has added to a rising tide of energy deals as confidence gradually returns to the battered sector. This year is on course to see the first growth in oil and gas M&A since the 2014 downturn, with transactions worth $270bn agreed in the first nine months of the year, up 40 per cent on the same point in 2016.

This week s jump in oil prices   Brent crude hit a two-year high of $59 per barrel   will only reinforce the sense of animal spirits returning, writes the FT s Andrew Ward. However, Graham Watson, partner at Freshfields Bruckhaus Deringer, the London-based law firm, says it is the relative stability of prices over the past year, rather than the partial recovery, that has been the most important catalyst for deals.

 Oil has been in a range between $40 and $60,  he says.  Buyers are confident it is not going down to $25 and sellers are confident it is not going back to $100. Reducing that delta between the two has been key to reaching agreement on valuations. 

Tough times for Chinese M&A

For Chinese investors cross-border M&A is becoming nearly impossible. At home, regulators are telling them that they can t park their money abroad. In Europe, the EU isworking toerect new barriers to entry. And in the US,the Trumpadministration is clamping down on inbound Chinese deals.

This week, Chinese investors seeking to buy a stake in a European mapping company were told to  apply directly  to the US president after their  241m offer failed to win regulatory approval, according to one of the bidders, reports Yuan Yang in Beijing.

NavInfo, a Beijing-based mapping provider for tech giant Tencent, agreed last year tobuy a 10 per cent stake in Amsterdam-headquartered Here. The deal to buy a stake in Here, which isco-owned by German carmakers Audi, BMW andDaimlerand US chipmakerIntel, was blocked in Europe and regulators told the Chinese investors to try their luck in America. They are unlikely to have much of it.

The Committee on Foreign Investment in the US (Cfius), which has the power to block deals over national security concerns, has been very aggressive of late in crushing Chinese deals. This isn t just a Trump thing. Former president Barack Obama had also bolstered the scrutiny of deals coming out of China.

The number of transactions referred to Cfius is expected to hit a record high this year, beating the 2016 high of approximately 18 cases (exact data only comes out more than a year later). Nearly half of these cases are likely to be Chinese.

Bloomberg BNA has interviewed Mario Mancuso, a partner at Kirkland & Ellis, and Luci Hague, an associate in Kirkland s international trade and national security practice, on essential tips on how to deal with Cfius reviews. Read the "https://www.bna.com/cfius-lattice-boards-n57982088242/".

Pro Private Markets
Enevo Bags $12 Million for Waste Management; Company's software helps monitor and manage waste with the help of sensor data.

By Olga Razumovskaya 
136 words
29 September 2017
00:03
WSJ Pro Venture Capital
RSTPROVC
English
Copyright  2017, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Enevo, a waste management company, has raised $12 million in Series C funding led by Lifeline Ventures and existing international investors.

Enevo's software is used to monitor and manage waste with the help of sensor data. The startup provides data analytics to help with managing alerts for waste pickup, as well as compares dumpster pickups against collection schedules. It also works with haulers to manage pickup schedules better.

The company's customers in the U.S., Canada and Europe include retailers, restaurants and other businesses.

The company has raised $34 million in total to date.

Write to Olga Razumovskaya at "mailto:olga.razumovskaya@wsj.com.
